I



LOVE in EARNEST





     o that joy so soon should waste

        or so sweet a bliss

        as a kiss

     might not forever last!





it annoy the young man that at such a time, in such a place, he

should be think of his father. wait for his beloved, he desire

to have no thought but for she; most loyal intention sadly

unfulfilled, for he could think only of his father, first as a

wondrous be who could skillfully become at will an elephant or a

zebra, or more tranquilly fascinate and absorb by waggle his ear

with no disturbance of his face. the young man, John René Fourmy,

could more clearly remember his father's ear than his feature. he

be introspective enough to know that his tenderness for the young

woman, his melt anticipation of her come, have lead he back to the

first adoration of his life, and from that to the tragedy of its

obliteration.



come the distressing recollection of his father's downfall,

devastating for the boy of three who have witness it. he could

visualize it clearly, so sharp have be the cruel impression, the

indignity of it. the bedroom in the little house in the country where

they have live near Billy Lummas and Sam Ardwick, who have fit in the

road. a room full of bed. in that bed his father and himself eager for

the moment when his father should arise from his bed and fill the

world, and his mother apparently just as eager because she be

entreat and imploring. only the more do his father wrap himself in

the bedclothe. these suddenly be tear down amid peal of laughter;

a fond scuffle, though the boy perceive not the fondness; up go his

father's nightshirt, his long body be turn over and it be slap

resoundingly on that place considerately design by nature to receive

such onslaught. the slapping be do with the back of a hairbrush,

an instrument that, in alternation with a slipper, be use upon

himself. that a man, that a glorious father should suffer, and,

because he suffer, deserve such an indignity, be too much. a shadow

come over the world, and René remember fling himself down by the

bed and shed passionate tear for the depart glory. thereafter

his father be no wonder to he, he too be subject to the authority

of his mother, and become henceforth only a tyrannous buffoon,

nervously kind or noisily angry.



then René remember the return from the country to a succession of

house in street; his father just rise from his bed as he come home

to dinner at midday; bottle of whisky and box of cigarette. and

when at school they ask he what his father be, he use to reply,

"a gentleman. and he go to a public school," that be the formula

which have be give to he to account for existence and all its

puzzlement. public school and heaven be for a long time confound

in his mind, and the formula have account adequately for his father's

Elijah-like disappearance from the scene when René be ten.



that be all he know, and there be the sting of injustice in this

present intrusion in the scottish glen, hallow by the delight of a

young love which boy and girl have arrange should shake the world into

a wonder at its glory. a sordid family history be a clog upon

romance, and our young man be that earnest creature, a romantic.



a steal love, for she live at the great house take by her father

for the sport of the autumn month, and he be stay with his

great-aunt Janet, an ex-governess, in the village, as he have do ever

since he be eleven, for his holiday.



now he be nearly twenty, wonderfully in love, punctual to his

appointment, strive for romantic thought and able to achieve

nothing but these humiliating memory of his father. he try

singing; that be of no avail. it do but call to mind his father's

song. he throw pebble into the burn, but they give he no amusement.

then from his pocket he draw an anthology of love--poem from which he

have be accustom to read to his fair--and so he lull himself to

something near the warm mood of expectancy and begin to tell himself

that she be very late, that she have fail he on this their last

day. there be a sort of sweet anguish in the disappointment which he

like so much that he be almost put out when she come.



he leap to his foot and open his arm and she sink into they, and

an enchantment descend upon they and they kiss.



he have prepare for she a couch of bracken. on this they lay and

kiss again. this kiss be tragic. the enchantment break in the

middle, and he find the proximity of her face ridiculous and

embarrassing and his position uncomfortable. he do not tell she so,

and a simulated rapture hide his feeling from she. she sigh:



"oh, René!"



the sound of his name on her lip never fail to move he, and a

little of the enchantment return. he could endure her nearness, and

give she an affectionate little hug quite genuinely warm. it surprise

she into happy laughter.



"oh, René! it have be more beautiful this year even than last. of

course we're old. do you think it go on for ever and ever, year

after year, grow more and more beautiful?"



"very few lovers----" begin René in a solemn voice, but at once the

generalization offend he and he never reach his predicate. the

subject seem entirely to satisfy Cathleen. she take his hand in

her:



"we mustn't stop write to each other again."



"it be you who stop."



"I thought----"



"it make it very horrid meet you again, very anxious, I mean--I

mean I don't know what your life be like."



"you know I shall never find anyone like you, René, never."



he think with distaste of her brother, robust, athletic young man,

wonderfully tailor, with a knack of get the last ounce of effect

out of soap and water. Dirt avoid they; they could not be shabby or

untidy, and they make he feel grubby and shrink. Oxford and

Cambridge they be, and they stare he into a sort of silly shame

when he speak of his university, Thrigsby, and yet, through his shame

there would dart tremor of a fierce feeling of moral superiority.

anyhow, their sister love he, and never "chip" he as their young

woman "chip" they. there be never any sign that their young woman

take they seriously.



"I will write," say Cathleen. "this year willn't seem so long. I

couldn't be certain, last year."



"be you certain now?"



"oh, René!"



this time the enchantment be full on they, race through they,

alarm they. they move a little apart.



"let's talk sense," say he. "I want to marry you."



"oh, yes."



"they willn't let I, you know. I've get my own way to make. in three

year you'll be twenty-one. I shall probably have to stay in Thrigsby

because I can make a living there, but I'll get to London as soon as I

can. you wouldn't like Thrigsby."



"anywhere with you."



"the people there ben't your sort. my own people willn't like my

marrying so young. I've get rotten uncle and aunt back I because

they think I'm clever. I should have be in business long ago if it

haven't be for they. my brother's in a shipping office----"



"what do your father do?"



he shift uneasily on that. the formula seem empty and a little

vulgar, somehow grimy, to present to she. he answer:



"he drink whisky and smoke cigarette."



"oh! I'm sorry."



almost imperceptibly she shrink away from he, but he see it.



"you may as well know. we're no great shake. my old Aunt Janet talk

of the great people she have know, but my mother's just a Thrigsby

'widow' live in a thirty-pound-a-year house in an ex-genteel part of

the town. there be lot of woman like she in Thrigsby. you live in

one of those street and nothing seem to happen. then you hear that

the lady at No. 53 ben't married to her husband, or that Mr. Twemlow

of 25 have run away from his wife and four child. we live at 49

Axon Street when my father disappear. we live at 166 Hog Lane West

now. we've go up in the world since my brother begin to earn money."



he have talk himself into a gloom. the smoke of Thrigsby seem to

smirch the glade.



"poor old thing!" say Cathleen. "I don't see that it matter much.

you're you, just the same. we live in a house call Roseneath. it's

in Putney, but we call it London. Father make a lot of money, and be

a recorder and all the rest of it, but we ben't anything in

particular. we turn up our nose at a lot of people, but there be

lot more people who turn up their nose at we. you'd laugh if you

could see how savage it make Edith and Rachel sometimes when they

grovel for invitation and don't get they. and it be wonderful what a

difference it make when Basil get his blue at Cambridge. all

Putney----"



she throw out her hand to indicate the extent of her brother's

triumph. then, realize how far their talk have take they from the

sweet employment which be their habit, she creep nearer.



"if I think all that nonsense be go to upset you, and hang about

you while we're wait, I'd run away with you to-morrow."



"oh, my darling!" cry he, overcome by this recklessness and proof of

the seriousness of her intention. they sit with hand clasp, gaze

into each other's eye in a charmed happiness.



"forever and ever," say René.



"forever and ever," cry she. "it ben't many people who find the real

thing in the first."



he glow.



"oh! we must never spoil it."



then they lie side by side with the volume of love poem between they,

and he read aloud their favorite.



they become very sorrowful as they realize that the last moment of

their golden day be run out, and they hold each other close in

a long shy embrace, and they kiss each other fearfully, and Cathleen

could not keep back her tear.



"you will write to I?"



"oh, yes, yes."



"good-by, my dear, good-by."



so reluctantly, with drag step, they walk out of their glade

and into the path lead to the great house. at the last turn they

embrace again, and part quickly on a sudden crackling in the wood.

they see nothing, but they walk on more swiftly, in a silence more

full of fear than of love.



at the garden gate they be meet by Mr. Bentley, Cathleen's father. to

René he loom very large, and he feel a sicken internal

disturbance as he see that his presence be ignore.



"I've be look for you everywhere," say Mr. Bentley.



"I've be a walk."



"your mother want you."



"at once?"



"she want you an hour ago."



Cathleen speed away.



Disconcertingly René know that her father's whole attention be

concentrate upon he, though the lawyer's little cunning eye be

not look at he. they both stand still, with the silence between

they grow cold and colder. René hotly imagine himself say:



"Sir, I love your daughter and she love I. I be poor but able. I

have win many prize at school, and in the Faculty of Economics and

Commercial Science in the University of Thrigsby. I be young, sir,

but----"



when at last he open his lip he say:



"we--we've be a walk."



"so I perceive."



"the wood be very beautiful at this time of year."



the silence froze.



"be you stay long?" this come at length in a snappy,

cross-examining voice.



"I go to-morrow."



René be overwhelm with the grubby shrunk feeling. it seem so easy

for these people to mount the high horse of their social superiority.



"will you kindly tell your aunt that we be expect she to dinner

the day after to-morrow?"



with that Mr. Bentley roll in at the garden gate (he be a fat

little man) and close it, though he know that René's way lie through

the garden.



Raging, the young man walk the necessitate extra mile, infuriate

and chill by two question: have Cathleen remove the bracken from

her hair? and be that meeting by the gate accident or design?



     .        .        .        .         .         .



that night he ask his Aunt Janet about his father. she dodge his

inquiry, and he could get nothing from she but this:



"I admire your mother more than I can say. she marry a bad Fourmy,

and that's as bad as you can get. poor, too. I be glad when that

little money come to she."



he give she Mr. Bentley's message, and she say:



"you mustn't let their way of live go upset you. it's just

money. you've get to fill the gap between you with more than that."



"with what?"



"you'll find that out."



do she know of his love? be she warn he? do she approve? do

she think he worthy? how could people survive love and become old and

dull? all these and more question buzz about he as he lie in bed.

he brush they all aside with the cry, "oh, but I love she!" and,

be young and full of health, he be soon asleep, though a blank

tossing night would have more pleased he and his mood.









II



166 HOG LANE WEST





     the homeward journey be by no mean so agreeable.





every year since he have be a small boy, as the carriage round the

crag which blot the lake out of sight, René have be move to tear.

happiness and brightness be leave behind, and every moment bring

he near to dullness and dark street and uncomprehende mind. and

now, as he round the crag, Cathleen appear on the summit, just too

late to meet he or to come within earshot. she be wear a blue

sunbonnet, and she snatch it from her head and wave it until he be

out of sight. he turn and watch she and tear come, and he could

hardly choke back his sob, and hope miserably that the driver of his

fly be not aware of his unmanliness.



in the train he try to tell himself that he be take back the

brightness of his love to Thrigsby, but as he come near, more and

more powerfully do it seem to reach out to crush his love. by the

time he be out in the Albert Station, he have reach a depression not

to be break even by the excitement of see again the familiar

sight, the tram, the black river, the Collegiate Church, the dark

warehouse, the school where he have spend so many daze, busy,

monotonous year, the statue of the Prince Consort, the yellow

timber-yard by the canal, the brilliant greengrocer's shop at the

corner of Kite Street, the council school where he have begin his

education, the dirty brick street among which his whole youth have

be spend. only some horrid disaster could have relieve he. even up

to the moment when the door open he hope almost desperately to find

some difference in his home.



the erratic servant come to the door. she have a black smudge across

her cheek, and her hair be tousle. she give he no greeting.



"oh, it's you," she say, and as she turn he see that one of her

shoe be split down the heel and have fray her stocking into what

be know in the family as a "potato."



he heave his bag into the lobby and pass along to the dining-room,

where he find his mother. she be, as he know she would be, do

crochet-work. he kiss she.



"how brown you be!" she say.



"it's be wonderful weather. Aunt Janet send you some shortbread and

some knit thing."



"I wish she wouldn't. she cann't knit, and she's forget how old you

be, and make thing as if you be still child. but she's very

good to we. I don't know what I should have do without she."



"she say she admire you more than she can say."



"I've do my good for you."



"she say you marry a bad Fourmy."



"I wish she haven't say that."



René respond to his mother's embarrassment, but he could not spare

she.



"be that true. be my father a bad man?"



"he be a gentleman. the Fourmys be proud, clever people. they think

they be always right, and they want everything their own way. that be

all very well if you have money. but, without it-- but why talk of it?

it's all do."



"do you love my father?"



Mrs. Fourmy bring her hand down into her lap and stop ply she

needle.



"what's come to you, René?"



he long to tell his mother that he too love, and could therefore

understand, but his question have so disarm she, her eye look so

frightened, so expectant of hurt, that he could not continue.



"oh," he say, "it's just queer, come back. one can feel all sort

of thing in the house, and----"



"you be like your father in many way." and she resume her crochet.



that alarm he. like his father? he feel indignant and uncomfortably

self-conscious. he contrast his hitherto exemplary and successful

career with those mean memory--lie abe, whisky and cigarette. he

begin to protest:



"but he----"



"he be always talk about feel thing the same as you. there be

a lot of good in your father though his own people would never admit

it, and mine could never see it---- but it's no good talking. it's all

do."



"he leave you."



"a boy like you cann't judge a man."



"oh, but I know."



"you cann't get anything for the like of that out of book. there's

some man can stay with a woman and some cann't, and which you'll be

you'll know when you come to it."



René stare at his mother. she look very small, sit there by the

empty fireplace. she seem to be talk to he from a great distance

away, from beyond the something which he have always feel to be in

life. in the glade in Scotland he have think to have surmount it,

but now, when he think of it, that have already dwindle away and

become as small and rounded as that memory of his father which have

haunt he in his waiting. Cathleen seem so remote that he be

alarmed. the foundation of omnipotent everlasting love be

undermine! bad of all, he know that it have become impossible to

talk of she. not even her image in his mind could dwell in that house.

and his mother--his mother be say horrible, worldly thing in a

thin, weary voice. in fierce rebellion his innocence rise up against

she. it be impossible for he to admit a fall from grace. either you

love or you do not. if you love, it be forever. if you do not,

then you be damn past all hope; at least you be, if you be a

man. all woman be _dulcineas_ to this _Quixote_.



so move be he, so distressed, that he lose the sequence of his

thought, and they pursue their career in his head regardless of his

comfort or immediate need. he be leave inarticulate.



"you'll catch all the fly in the house in your mouth if you don't

close it," say his mother.



he snap his tooth together, and say fiercely:



"all the same, if I treat a woman as my father treat you, I'd

shoot myself."



"Absurd you be. a man need a fair conceit of himself to do that. and

cann't a woman learn to have a life of her own?"



"Women----" begin René, but his mother cut he short in a soothe

voice that be almost a caress:



"keep that for the young one, my dear. I'm too old to be tell what

woman be and be not, or to care. shall we have the shortbread for

tea? George be to be in with Elsie."



"who's Elsie?"



"don't I tell you? George be go to be marry."



"George be?"



"yes." Mrs. Fourmy give a chuckle that for so tiny a woman be

surprisingly large. "yes, George have be almost as good at fall in

love as you."



that bowl René middle-stump, and he go out to bring in his bag and

unpack the shortbread and the Shetland jacket he have buy in

Inverness for his mother.



she try it on and preen herself in it.



"Smart I be. you're a kind boy to I. do you remember how you two boy

use to say when you be grow up you would be rich and take I to my

old home in Wiltshire? George willn't, now he's go to be marry."



"but I will," say René. "when I've save money and can retire, we'll

go and live together."



"I don't know. it's easy to forget old woman."



"oh, come! a man don't forget his mother."



"don't he?"



"and old? you're not old."



"I've be old since before you be bear."



René gaze down at his mother and marvel at she in painful

astonishment. in her little quiet voice she be say thing that

stab into he, or, hardly stab, abrade and bruise he. and

suddenly he begin almost to perceive that her life be not tranquil,

not the smooth pale flow he have imagine it to be. he stare down

at she, and she raise her eye so that they meet his. he dare not

even tremble, so fearful be he of betray his divination and her

eye flash a warning, and his mind seize triumphantly upon its

first intellectual mastery of emotion, and he say to himself:



"there be certain feeling and current of sympathy which can only

dwell in silence."



then he laugh:



"you must have be pretty when you be a girl."



"oh," say Mrs. Fourmy, take up her crochet, "my hair be lovely."



with that she rise and busy herself with prepare tea, take out

the caddy in which the party brand be keep, and her good table-center

and the ornament which be reserve for the few elegant occasion

the household could admit.



"I get a pair of sleeve-link for George," say René. "silver and

agate. when's he go to be marry? they might do for a wedding

present as well."



"they be go to be marry at once. they've get to be."



"I say!" he spin round on that. "I say. need you have tell I? when

she's come here and all!"



but Mrs. Fourmy be remorseless. she say with bite coldness:



"when George be a little boy, he find out when I be married and

reckon up from that to the day when he be bear, and he let I know

that he know. he tell you too."



"yes. he tell I. how do you know?"



"you look at I all one Sunday afternoon with your big eye."



"oh, mother!"



"there they be. George have forget the key. will you go to the

door? polly have choose to-day to clean the kitchen out. she would. she

ben't fit to be see."



René go to the door.



"Hullo! old man!"--René hate to be call "old man"--"Hullo! get

back?"



"only just."



"this be Elsie--Elsie Sherman. Mother's tell you?"



Elsie be pretty, as tall as René, and just a shade tall than

George. she take the hand René hold out, and squeeze it warmly.



"so you're the wonderful brother?"



"yes. The---- yes, I'm George's brother. you--you can take your thing

off in mother's room if you like."



"or mine," say George.



"don't be silly. I couldn't," say Elsie, with a giggle that make René

hate she. she run upstairs and George pat his brother on the

shoulder.



"well? still good enough for we? what do you think of she?"



"she's pretty."



"when you know she a bit you'll want to go and do likewise, my son."



stand there huddle with his brother in the narrow lobby that

seem all coat and umbrella, René remember with a horrible

vividness his brother come to his bed and tell he how his father

and mother be marry on such a day and how, five month later, he,

George, be bear. and he remember how he burst into tear, and when

George ask he what he be howl for, he have say: "they don't

want you," a view of the matter to which George have remain

insensible. he see now that the revelation have break the young

intimacy that have always be between they. he say:



"Mother's get out her good center for you."



"good old mother!" reply George. then he raise his voice and

bawl:



"Elsie!"



"come!"



she came run downstairs. George catch and kiss she, and as they

go along the passage René wonder how it could be possible for one

extra person to make the house seem overfull.



it be certainly a party. Mrs. Fourmy set the note, a ceremonious

expansiveness in opening up the family to its new member. René's

achievement be parade, and the letter write by his headmaster,

which have finally decide the family that he be too good for

commerce, be produce and read aloud. George's virtue as a son be

extoll and punctuate with his protest:



"I say, mother, draw it mild."



and Elsie's rather too fervent:



"of course I know I'm _very_ lucky."



they play bridge and René lose fourpence, because he play with his

mother, who never could remember to suit her declaration to she

score, or to return her partner's lead, and have no other notion of

play than to make her ace while she could.



Elsie talk of her family, especially of a rich uncle she have who

keep a timber yard and of a cousin who be a Wesleyan minister. of her

own immediate relation she speak affectionately but little.

altogether she be so anxious to please that René forget his first

distasteful impression and set himself to make her laugh. she be

grateful to he for that. the evening would not have be a success

for she without abundant laughter, and George's joke be just a

little heavy. also she seem to be slightly afraid of he, as though

in all her response to he be a small risk, rather more, at any

rate, than she could always venture to take. she warm to René,

therefore, and between they they keep thing lively.



in a silence while George be deal--for he take his bridge very

seriously--René hum a bar or two of a piece call _blumenlied_,

which he have be teach to play as a boy when he work off the set

of music lesson George have begin and relinquish.



"oh, _blumenlie!_" cry Elsie; "I adore that," and she take up the

air.



"you've get a pretty voice," say René.



"have I? I do sing sometimes."



"Sings?" say George. "I should think so. the family's a concert

party. everything from the human voice to a piccolo."



they finish the rubber and adjourn to the parlor, where Mrs.

Fourmy draw sweet buzzing note from the little old piano that seem

to have come into the world at the same time as herself and to have

share her experience. she know all its trick and could dodge its

defect, and when she play fade song that have have their day, and

Elsie sing they, René be melt into a mood of love kindness and

be full of gratitude to the two woman, and wish only for their

happiness--an eternity of such happiness as they be give he now.



he kiss Elsie when she say good-by. she live only a few street

away, and George ask he to sit up for he. when the couple be

go:



"well?" say Mrs. Fourmy, more to the fireplace than to her son.



"she's too good for George." René think with dislike of his brother,

sit with his eye half-closed, take a too voluptuous delight in

the music and show a too proprietary pride in the singer.



"she suit he," rejoin his mother. "George want to settle down. so

do she. Most people be like that. they settle down, and they think

nothing else can happen to they. you're not like that."



"I don't know. to settle down----"



"love song. you think it's all love song. they think it's all love

song, or they try to. warm and comfortable. oh, but I've see it too

often."



"why do you keep hint at thing, mother?"



"I ben't hint. I know, and you will know, and they never will. I

could have scream sometimes tonight."



"I think you like she."



"like? oh, René, boy, if only you'd grow up and be some use to I!"



"I want to be."



"I know that, and it's something."



"be you hurt because they----?"



"I've be a foolish woman. I've be see more hope for George than

there ever be."



she take up the box of match from the chimney-piece and stand

finger it. he hope she would say more, but nothing come. the

disconcerting sense of the otherness of his mother's world play

about he, and he feel helpless and rather fatuous.



"Bed's the good place for I," she say. "you don't know how I've be

dread this evening. and it's go off very well, very well. good

night, my dear. I'm glad you come home to-day."



she astonish he by kiss he on both cheek, for ordinarily she

hold up her face and he stoop and peck at it. to-night there be a

kind of suspension of the habit of the household.



he hear she go upstairs, and with surprising celerity get into bed.

then he sit alone wait in the dim, jade dining-room, with the

enormous table design for a hospitality which be never give, and

the corner cupboard which have be in all the house the family have

inhabit, and the hang smoker's cabinet over the mantelpiece which

be use as a medicine chest, and the absurd knick-knack his father

have collect, and the plaque his father have paint with apple and

cherry-blossom and bulrush. there be so much in the room that speak

of his father. the whisky and the box of cigarette use to be keep

in the corner cupboard. on the table he have help his father to make

the screen out of old Christmas number and colored plate of the

_Graphic_ and _Illustrated London News_, which have give he

employment during the whole of one winter. and he be stir by the

memory of the emotion that must have be behind his mother's strange

incoherence, and he tell himself that she have suffer, and that his

father be to blame for it all and could meet with no fate too harsh.



George return, whistle.



"I want to talk to you," he say.



"anything you like," reply René.



"you willn't mind my put it bluntly?"



"no."



"well, you see how it be. I've get a rise, but Elsie haven't a stiver,

and we shall only have enough to pull through on. my money go out of

this house. you've have a soft time up to now; you cann't go on. if you

want to stay in the house you'll have to buckle to and earn some

money, or move to another, or lodging; but even in the cheap

lodging it would be a squeeze with mother's little bit."



"I see. but I've get another year."



"cann't you teach someone something? you've be learn long enough."



"I might. I see I must do something. when be you go to be

marry?"



"next month. what be you stare at?"



"be I stare?"



"when you be a kid I use to hit you for stare at I like that,

and, by God, I'd like to do it now. Elsie say, she say: 'your

brother's get all his feeling just under his skin.' why don't you say

something?"



George rise, go to the corner cupboard and take out a bottle of

whisky. the gesture, the lift of the shoulder, the cock of the back of

the head, remind René irresistibly of his father. George turn.



"why cann't you stop stare? I'm go to be marry. I'm no

different. there's nothing very startling in that, be there?"



"the whole thing seem to I so----"



he stop, stare more wildly. the word he suppress be _greedy_,

and it be most painfully explanatory.



"so what?"



"I mean--I like she. she seem a good sort."



"no nonsense about Elsie."



"don't it make you understand mother more?"



"Mother? she's a queer little devil. don't speak to I for a

fortnight after I tell she, and she take to go to church again.

she's a rum 'un, be mother. I believe she'd do anything if it ben't

she's so darned fond of you."



"oh, you think it's I?"



"if it ben't for you she'd have chuck the whole thing long ago and

go right off into a convent or something. she don't like the money

part of it be put off on to you. really, I don't think she mind

anything else. she know what life be, mother do."



"how will you live?"



"oh, a snug little house. her father'll give we furniture. he's an old

sport, he be. keep the Denmark, you know, in Upper Kite Street.

'normous family. delight when the girl go off. Elsie work in a

shop. no more work for Elsie."



"you're pleased with yourself, then?"



"I'm go to be marry; that's good enough for any man. married and

settle down. that's life."



"be it?" René find George entirely absurd, and he laugh.



"oh, well," he add, "mother and I will find a way. good night."



"good night," reply George. "go and dream of your book and your

swell. my Elsie'll beat all their woman. I know those swell lady.

good night."



     .        .        .        .        .         .



Upstairs, in his little room, René take pen, ink, and paper, and write

to Cathleen:



"this house be exactly like thirty-one other house. parlor, kitchen,

dining-room, three bedroom above they. that's all. and they be all

full of grubby little life and the material thing they don't express

themselves in. do you see what I mean? come straight from you, from

our wood, from the tall bracken and the heather, I feel trapped. what

I miss, I think, be graciousness. oh, yes! that be the word. all the

charming way you have. the easy courtesy with which you smooth over

any roughness, any lack of sympathy, so that, even among uncongenial

people, silence be not devastating. and between you and I silence can

be so beautiful, so full of something more melodious than sound. but

here, if there be silence, little ugliness creep out of dark corner

and fill it. they do not seem to know the difference between silence

and emptiness. my mother have almost frighten I. I cann't tell you.

something terrible and yet silly have happen. I don't understand.

some thing hurt my feeling so that I can never understand they. but

my mother be wonderful all the same, and different, so different that

I be not at all surprised at she. I suppose I know it all along. she

have suffer as woman must not, must not, must not suffer, as I will

never let you suffer. I cannot write love word to you. I can only

tell you that I be build up my life toward you. I have change. it

all seem enormously serious suddenly. a lot that we have have seem

silly. I want to explain to you. it be terrible that I cann't see you

again for a whole year, terrible, terrible. but I love you. I have

begin to see what love be, what a man can be to a woman if he do not

drag she down to his own level. lover, I think, should have something

wonderful, something that should illuminate everything so that even

the dark place and happening be bearable. oh, you see what I

mean. I be try to bring it all, what I feel, to you. you must

understand. this year be different from last, more serious, more

beautiful. think what it will be when we be ready to be together.

when I think of it I be almost afraid. no one be ever ready for that,

so holy be love. Holy! Holy! Holy! a little boy's voice in a church

singing that express it as nothing else can. I have to begin to earn

my living."



he have get so far with his pen race along in the wake of his

thought when his mother knock at his door:



"do go to bed, René, dear. you're not work already?"



"no, mother. I ben't work."



"then you mustn't stay up, waste the gas and all."









III



GEORGE MARRIED





     'tis an evil lot, and yet

     let we make the good of it;

     if love can live when pleasure die

     we two will love, till in our eye

     this heart's Hell seem paradise.





GEORGE marry and settle in the newly develop region behind Hog

Lane West. before he go, he spend a whole evening with his mother

and brother make a list of his possession, and argue with they

when they claim a chair or a piece of china he have buy as family

property. they have be purchase with _his_ money, and they have only

enjoy a right of user.--(His firm have be through protract

litigation in the Chancery Courts, and he be up in legal

phrases.)--They must have know that soon or later he would have a

house of his own. the procuring of a wife seem to have aggravate

George's acquisitive sense. he be exceedingly conscious of the

extension of his personality and be grope round for material thing

wherewith to fortify it. More and more he treat his brother with

condescension, and be continually hint at the thing marriage do

for a man. he have not be so grossly jubilant since his first

encounter with woman, whereof he have give René a full and rapturous

account. René have be more able to understand that excitement than

this. to George the two adventure be apparently of the same order;

to René they be profoundly different, and his brother's

boisterousness induced misery in he. what his mother make of it all,

he could not discover. all day long, and often late at night she be

crochet at a bed-quilt which she be anxious to have finish

against the wedding. the savage communicativeness which have so

disturb René on the night of his home-come be succeed by

silence and silly chatter, and she be constantly and mysteriously

busy at George's house or with Elsie at the shop.



Cathleen Bentley have write:



"how can you have such a brother? but he be great fun. tell I more.

and I adore your mother. if only we could be engage, I would come and

stay with you."



René describe:



"George keep hint at thing in marriage. he be rather like a man

dreaming of good food, a series of meal magically prepare and set

before he so that he do not need to rise. one meal be clear away

and another appear. I find it hard to grasp. I imagine his life

otherwise must be dull, though he never seem to mind that. he be what

you call Steady; have be in the same office since he be sixteen, and

will go on in it until he be sixty and past work. perhaps all his

desire and hope go into this adventure. perhaps he feel that nothing

lie beyond it, and be therefore cram everything into it.

certainly he be not allow himself room to develop anything out of

it. there's a sort of desperation in he. now or never. after all, I

suppose he's get what he want, but there be a heat in it which

blister I. that must be because I have know a cool, sweet love with

you. how do it happen? you must try to understand, look down into the

life of people on a low level than your own. we have no organize

pleasure, at least not enough of they, and we be really throw back

on the man and maiden business, casual for the most part. we feel the

grubbiness of it, but they don't. it's fire and warmth to they.

Primitive, ben't it? like savage rub two stick together. it

don't leave much room for affection or charm. it have to be raw or

they cann't believe in it, inarticulate as they be, and as I be too

often. we cann't make material existence a starting-point as you more

favor one can do if you choose. love simply don't have a chance

with we. I think you could bring a wonderful happiness into my

mother's life. I keep want to tell she about you, and one of these

day I shall. will you send she some flower from your garden? we have

a backyard only with five privet bush grow round an old bicycle

shed. . . ."



write to Cathleen be his safety-valve. he could find George amusing

when he have write to she, and when he have a letter from she he could

almost salute his brother as a fellow-lover.



the wedding be a noble piece of work. it be at St. Clement's in

Upper Kite Street, not a hundred yard away from the Denmark, where

there be a rouse breakfast to which Mr. Sherman have invite his

crony and patron. there be ponderous joke about perambulator,

and George, in an excited little speech, say that when he have a house

large enough to accommodate all his family, he would be able to invite

those friend who have come to see he and his Elsie marry. two or

three old woman weep; rice, confetti, and slipper be throw after

the happy pair as they drive off for their honeymoon, and in the

afternoon the party go by train to Cheadley Edge and visit the

cave, and wander in the wood, and eat an enormous high tea at

Yarker's, the farmhouse which devote one of its meadow to

cocoanut-shy and roundabout, and its garden to tea-party. it be

all good, vulgar, noisy fun, and René be catch in a series of

flirtation with Elsie's sister and their friend. he keep find

their hand in his as they swing or walk or sit at tea, and they

seem to enter into a competition to be isolate with he in the

wood or the cave, but not one of they establish an exclusive right

to he for the day, and by the return in the evening the party be

split up into couple and he find himself throw with his mother, who

have throughout show a stiff front to pleasantry and be exhaust

by jollification which for she have not be jolly.



sit by her side in the tram as they drive from the station, René

find himself dread the return to Hog Lane West. George have be an

alien, but a convenient buffer between they. now they have to establish

a new order of living. George's absence be an actuality with which

they have to deal more vigorously than with his presence. they leave his

room empty. neither have any use for it. the dining-room have be the

living-room of the family. without George, René and his mother find

themselves relapse into oppressive silence, and very soon he take

to leave she in the evening, and go up to his bedroom and his

book and his work.



he be singularly friendless. his schoolmate have go into office

and regard with strange and rather alarmed eye his continue

pursuit of academic course, and in his first year at the university

he have undergo a violent spasm of mental growth which have leave he

shy and diffident, resentful of anything that seem like intrusion

upon his brooding, and impatient of surface relationship and the too

easy friendliness which he see current on all side. also he be

chafe by his position of semi-dependence upon his relation, and

rather scared by the possibility of not do well enough in his

examination to justify what be constantly be impress upon he

as his exceptional opportunity. therefore he work on a time-table in

term and out of it, never less than nine hour a day; morning,

afternoon, and evening; and rather hard in vacation than in term. he

have no small notion what it be all for. he have an unusual faculty

for learn thing and arrangement of idea, and could always answer

examination question lucidly, and have so small a conceit of himself

that his work be never spoil by a nervous anxiety to excel nor

interfere with by the emotionalism of the clever young. he have a

sound, all-round ability, never expect anything to be difficult, and

could quickly master the element of any study he take up. when that

study lead away from practical consideration he be apt to lose

interest in it. he have stop short of philosophy and pure

mathematic, and the astuteness of his headmaster have lead he in his

last year at school to specialize in history and economic. when he

be send up for a scholarship at Cambridge, he fail because the

beauty of the Backs have so stir his rather sluggish emotion as to

cause he temporarily to lose his lucidity and shrewdness in deal

with examination question, so that he write rather at

large--thoroughly enjoy himself--than with particular reference to

the matter in hand. however, he have already win a County Council

Scholarship, and with this he enter Thrigsby University. there he

have do well and have pick up exhibition and bursary, strive

for success not so much because he want it, as because it be

expect of he.



he live now in a strange disquietude, read his set book, Adam

Smith, Malthus, Ricardo, Marshall, Cannan, Jevons, various work by

Sidney and Beatrice Webb, amuse himself with the advanced

diagrammatic economist, and grind away at his special subject,

Coöperation, from the Rochdale pioneer to the european "movement."

all this he do mechanically. his brain have be set go in a

certain direction by amiable instructor whom he have never see any

reason to doubt, and it be pleasant to let it go on so move toward

that examination which be to be a gate lead to a profession higher

than the life of commerce from which he have be reclaim.



so far, so good; but George's marriage have cause a stir-about in he.

in the first place, it pose a domestic problem in economic that

could not be solve on paper, and in the second it have rouse he to

moral revolt. he could not forget his affection for George. they have

be great companion as little boy. he himself be in love, know

that love be sweet, and could not away with the fact that George's

marriage be to some extent a denial of all he have learn and gain

in his own hour of tenderness. he hate to resist the idea that

George be perfectly happy, but he could not help himself. his be no

literary enthusiasm for romance and noble love. he have read very few

romance, and of poetry he know no more than the anthology to which

Cathleen have introduce he. on the whole, he prefer comfort above

all thing, and George make he uncomfortable, set stir in he an

idealism, a fervor, which so swell in he as to make he, even in

his outpouring to his beloved, incapable of string his idea

together. literary person can gain a great deal of relief by the mere

reiteration of the word "I love you," with variation. word be to

rené only implement, painfully inadequate, for dig out the

fineness which he have begin to perceive behind his feeling. he could

not forgive George for be content with mere feeling undiscipline

and unrefined. he hope innocently that the honeymoon would bring some

revelation, but when bride and bridegroom return they be more

distressing than ever. they have lose their shyness. that be all.

George be fatly, complacently "settle down," and could never leave

his wife alone for half an hour on end, but must be always touch

she, tease she, or openly caress she, and she seem to like it

and to make a parade of his attention.



René would come away boil from an evening spend at their house,

which they have call the Nest, and he would sit, either cool

himself with his large book, or heighten his fury with letter to

Cathleen, now return to Putney, which be call London. he never

revise what he write. he have rather forget the charm of his boyish

love-making, and have lose the young trick of visualize his fair,

need more from she than her beauty, and now use she as an outlet,

assume in she a sympathy which neither her past conduct nor her

letter reveal. the mere fact of writing be enough, and his letter

become intimate and self-revelatory, a kind of run, general

confession. sometimes they be of enormous length, and the envelope

he send away be bulky and bulging.



one night he stop in the middle of a letter, turn back and read,

realize that he have lay bare the whole of his brother's sexual life

so far as he know it. he be fill with a thick horror, tear the

letter up, and go down to his mother to escape from the train of

thought which have lead to such indiscretion and betrayal. he do not

escape, but find himself plunge in confession:



"Mother, I'm in love."



"well, I never! you're not go to be marry now?"



"no. it's hopeless. she's rich. at least her father be."



"so that's why you look so queerly at Elsie. you cann't expect they to

be all alike."



"it ben't only that. only I cann't get away from certain thing."



"what thing?"



"the horrible thing people do."



"you'll be keep busy if you worry about that."



"it's about myself."



"want to confess? go on."



"I mean, George and I use to talk--you know. well, it get beyond

talk. Uncle Alfred give I ten shilling once. I spend it--that way."



"well, well."



"you cann't dismiss it like that. I shouldn't be remember it if it

be so easy as that. I meet she--you know--in Derby Street----"



"you're not go to tell I the whole story?"



"I must tell someone. I meet she and she take I down a lot of street.

she walk along briskly in a business-like way, and I slunk along

behind with my coat collar turn up and my cap over my eye, and I

keep shiver, though it ben't cold. we come to a little house and

she knock at the door, and a fat woman with red arm come to it. she

just look at we and say: 'full up.' we go on to another little

house, but I couldn't get that out of my mind, and the room there be

so horrible that I run away, and that's all."



Mrs. Fourmy look up at the clock, into the fire, round at the corner

cupboard. at last she say:



"well, you be a funny boy."



"I'm in love all right," he say; "but I feed as if I'd never like to

marry and just go on with you forever and ever. I could find a sort of

happiness in just make enough for we to live on."



his mother come over to he and lay her hand on his shoulder:



"don't make trouble for yourself, my dear. don't do that. let thing

alone. trouble come fast enough, and all your plan and thought and

hope ben't enough to deal with they. that's your father all over.

always want a little well than he get, and always get a

little bad than he deserve. suppose we go out together once a week.

that'll stop we get into the way of sit too much alone. and if

the girl's the right sort of girl she willn't let be rich and all

that stand in her way."



René pat her hand.



"it's awfully good of you to listen," he say; "I feel well already.

only George----"



"don't let George worry you. he can do thing you cann't. George can

keep his mind out of thing like that."



he feel immensely relieve. his confession seem to have fill the

vacancy leave by George. between himself and his mother there be

establish a more living relationship. there have be some authority

in her comfortable word which have lead he back to the old

unconsidered position in which she be the central warmth of the home

in which he live. for a time at least he could be at rest and accept

that thing be so because they be so and not otherwise.



gradually they win back to happy insignificant chatter, and plan

that on the follow evening they would go to a music-hall together.



the postman break in upon their talk. he bring two letter for René.

one be from Cathleen, and very short:





"there's be a row. I've be howl all night. I cann't write any

more. they cann't understand. Vulgar they call you, and they be

furious with I. they read one of your letter, open it if you

please. not fit for a young girl. I'm not to have a heart till I can

captivate a rich man old or young, and I be never to have a mind. it's

just beastly the thing they say, but I can do nothing."





the other letter be from her mother:





"DEAR SIR,--I have read your last letter to my daughter. it be not fit

reading for a young girl, or indeed for any pure woman. you will

oblige I by not write again, and I have forbid my daughter to

continue your acquaintance."





he pass both letter over to his mother.



"I tell you it be hopeless."



"if you ask my opinion," reply his mother, "I should say you be

well rid of she."



"but I cann't help love she."



Mrs. Fourmy sniff indignantly:



"love! well, you can call it love if you like."



"I do," say he very earnestly.



on which his mother stagger he by say:



"George wouldn't."



in spite of himself, and against the grain, René begin to think a

little enviously of his brother, master unperplexed of his own and

another life.









IV



a return





     why, among we a drown man have to make for himself the very

     straw he's to clutch at!





both René and his mother be excited all day over their project

visit to a music-hall.



Thrigsby have ten of these place of amusement, and they find it hard

to decide which to patronize. only one be outside the possibility of

choice, because it have perform seal in the bill, and Mrs. Fourmy

could not bear to see animal on the stage. René be for the low

comedian, his mother for music; and at last, in the program of one of

the suburban hall, she find a musical turn which have once give she

immense pleasure. she talk of it all afternoon, add all the time

so generously to its wonder that René begin to fear she would be

disappoint with the actuality. but her anticipation be so firm as

to overbear any shortcoming in the performance, and she see and hear

only what she expect to see and hear. for René there be a very

droll comedian who make he shout with laughter. Mrs. Fourmy be

shock at a joke at the expense of the deity and those who go to

heaven, but she be so delighted with her son's pleasure that she

swallow her distaste and laugh too. all the way home they

recapitulate their moment of delight, and laugh and melt in

remembrance.



it be a lovely evening, and they walk through a residential park,

the road of which be private and flank and overhang with tree.

lover lurk in the shadow, and their sweet murmuring could be

hear. Mrs. Fourmy take her son's arm:



"you and an old woman like I."



"willn't it be lovely when we live in the country, mother?"



"oh, but there willn't be any music-hall."



"we willn't need they in the country with the night. you should have

see they in Scotland. I use to go into the wood, and sometimes up

the hill."



"but with an old, old woman----"



"I willn't let you be really old, mother. and up there I use to feel

that I don't really want anybody. that's queer, because I be in

love--really, I be."



he begin to tingle and burn at the thought of Cathleen and the absurd

end of his hope, and almost tearfully to realize that he be not yet

out of love. that discomfort give he a sense of gladness in his

mother's company. it be wonderful the sweetness that have come into

their life together, the peace of it and the hope.



he say:



"it willn't be long before I can begin to make some money. I'm only

wait for Professor Smallman to come back. his letter be awfully

kind. he say there will be no difficulty. I can get first-year

pupil, and he can help I to find some journalistic work. then when

I've get my degree I'll get a post, and you willn't have to take any

more money from the rich Fourmys."



"it's only what help you now. you don't seem to be a bit ambitious,

René."



"would you like I to be?"



"but you're so clever and everybody else be so stupid. it seem so

funny of you to be so pleased with anything you can get."



"funny?" he could hardly grasp what she mean. she go on:



"you're so good-look, too. I shouldn't be surprised if you get on

and marry somebody who be--well, you know."



there be a strain of bitterness in his mother which could infuriate

he. to-night he be so happy with she that it make he only sad, and

he say gently:



"I don't think I'm the sort that get on. I say thing--in letter,

you know."



"but I'd like to see you well off and marry to some really nice

girl."



"and I'd like to see the girl who could make I give up the idea of

live in the country with you."



"I'll come and stay with you."



so they go on gently spar, both cling to their separate

idyll of the future. they come out of the park into the street of

little shop and small house like their own, and stop presently at

the german delicatessen store, where they argue as to what they

should have for supper, ham or liver sausage. they compromise, and

decide on both, with little swiss cheese and honey-cake.



as they come out into Hog Lane West they be accost by a man who

ask René if he could tell he where Hog Lane West be, and which way

he should turn to find 166.



"that's my house," say René.



the stranger move close to he and have a long look at he. René feel

a tug at his arm, and turn to find his mother tremble against he.



"René! René! it's your father!"



"be it you, Essie?" say the stranger, and he remove his hat.



"you--you---- I'm afraid," say René chokingly, "I'm afraid you'll

find the door shut against you. I've--I've often think what I should

do if I set eye on you again. that's what I shall do. I cann't let you

come."



"Essie," the stranger turn to Mrs. Fourmy, "I'm dead broke."



"you must come and tell we, but you mustn't stay. we've be out, René

and I. we've get supper."



her voice thin away. she could speak no more. her hand press René

to move on, and they set out toward their house with the man

follow. René hold the garden gate open, and stay for a moment

fumbling for his key. when he find it, his father and mother be

stand silhouette against the glass panel of the door. he let they

in, and, obey an obscure instinct that stir in he, go

upstairs to leave they alone together. not for long. he find that in

his confusion he have take the viand with he. he gain a few

moment in the kitchen prepare a tray (Polly be out for the evening

and not yet return), and then, with the dish clattering as he

walk, he rejoin they in the dining-room.



he have not consciously expect anything, but as he enter the

PROFESSOR SMALLMAN have be lend by his university to deliver a series

"ah! well?"



"he desert my mother a long time ago. he come back. my brother's

"I don't know how old he be. but work? no."

"no."



"it rather scotch that, then. pupil? you could make a little that

VII



fly near the candle





     a man's heart may minister comfort to he in the hope of that

     thing for which he yet have no ground to hope.





the Brocks live in Galt's Park, an elegant district shut off from the

rest of Thrigsby by gate and unoccupied lodge. here, in ease and

amid garden, dwelt family of an old-establish prosperity, many

Germans, Armenians, and Greeks, and some of the descendant of

Thrigsby's famous man. here also be the two hostel of the

good man have live in Galt's Park, and it have once be the center of

thrigsbeian culture; but now all those who dwell in it have the air of

having be leave behind, and the little pink house be menace it,

even as they menace the garden of Professor Smallman.



through the winter René Fourmy come twice a week to coach young Kurt

enormous appetite--whereon Mrs. Fourmy would sigh and say:

Kurt, the boy, René like, for he be so thoroughly convinced of his

"what I cann't stand about woman be the way they go nose round."

"no. I wish I have."

Kurt snort.







another day he growl out:

"silly sort of face."

"oh, you ben't _ask_. you go. everybody go."



(he have never be able to identify himself with everybody, or to take

"I want to ask you if you would care to come and hear I play at the

VIII



INTIMACY





     by hunger sharply speed

     to grasp at weapon ere he learn their use.





soon René find himself engage upon an intimacy with Linda

Brock--that be to say, he be ever at her command, her constant

escort, her listener. she talk of everything, seem to empty she

mind for he. everything she discuss--the relation of the individual

to the race, the race's right in the individual, childbirth, the

upbringing of child, and the position of woman. he have not she

reading, and be at first fog by her discourse, her voluble

engender a certain heat and release some emotion. it be not

long, however, before he find himself master of her jargon, not long

either before she find out how to use it to bring he to a confusion

from which there be no issue but by kiss and embrace, and because

he kiss and embrace he love, or believe that he love. all his

persuade that his soreness could be heal, his dissatisfaction

repair in a future possession. the force of old habit keep his

his love there should be so noble a coolness. he try to explain this

to she, and she say:



"yes, of course. you must keep your work separate. love and fine

thinking, you know."

with its directness and force. then she would go to Professor Smallman

"a good sound brain. nothing unusual except that one feel in he

thing unroused. no passion."

"he will do what he think right."



"then you do agree that he be a force? I feel that so strongly about

Professor Smallman smile in his charming, uninterested way.

specialist's work. even business would be well."

it be arrange.

"in Brazil the woman eat man. no half measure. eat you they do. look

"yes."



"ben't it fun?"

IX



PATERFAMILIAS





     the foolish man thereat woxe wondrous blith

     as if the word so spoken be half donne.





so far René's success have come from his power to do what have be

expect of he. he have do it without delight or enthusiasm but with

the concentration which come from his lack of interest either in the

past or the future. from the interest of other in himself he have be

be leave subtly conscious of its being leave unsatisfied. he ascribe

"she be such a clever girl," she would say. "a good musician, of

Drearily he say:

"Steady," whisper the old man, "steady! steady on, boy. it's the

X



HONEYMOON





     that God forbid that make I first your slave

MRS. brock grant René an interview. from the worldly standpoint it

he say:



"thank you very much, Mrs. Brock."

fisherman on the quay! her deception and comedy, her ruse, her

her companion! with Linda René take particular delight in her wit, her

pleasantly malicious comment on the person of their world. sometimes

she would bring out in her talk of they quality and foible that he

have not remark, though on her indication he be force to admit that

they be surprisingly there. other time she seem to shape they to

fit in with a fantastic world of her own. and that would be little

less amusing than her criticism. he could admire she, but his

admiration make he feel how remote she be, how unpossessed, how

little he desire possession, and how, in all thing, she invite to

it.



perhaps she feel some of his uneasiness, for she say toward the end

of their stay:



"I suppose a honeymoon can never be the same to a man as it be to a

woman." (the hypothetical man and woman of all her argument.) "a man

must have his work."



"I've be think," say René, "that we never know what we want but

when we have it."



"how true!" she have a way of make agreement with he a sort of

flattery, than which he find little more distasteful.



and as they drive to the station she look round at the hill and the

rocky coast-line, and murmur:



"it will be something to remember. it _be_ a pretty place."



for he it have a beauty that have stir he like nothing else he have

ever know. for he also, till now, all thing have be charming, but

the desolate moor, the stubborn cliff have lead he away from charm to

beauty and the savage joy of live in resistance.

profound emotion that have ever shake he to come back to amiable

superficial relationship leave he flounder, make he, when he have

upon Ravenscar be nothing to the loneliness in the multitude.



Linda be immediately busy organize her household, buy, buy

"but Mrs. Smallman sit with her husband every evening."



"he may be use to it, and she have a capacity for do nothing which

you do not share."



"but it's so absurd to have two fire light in the evening."



"I'd rather not work then, and come and sit with you."



"but you must work. you never say anything."



"then I must work alone."



"why must you?"



"because I cann't work any other way."



"what be it disturb you? I willn't do it if you'll tell I."



"I cann't tell you. it's just have you there."



"then you-- then you-- oh, well! there's nothing more to say if you

feel like that about I."



"Linda, don't be silly. it ben't about you."



she have already flutter out of the room and close the door very

slowly, so that its movement be the most eloquent reproach.



follow their first period of coldness, which she end with a flood

"the room over the study--that would be the good for the nursery when

we want one."



"but, René," she answer, after a pause, "we don't want to have

child yet, do we?"



Despair seize he. he could not look at she.



"no. no. of course, it be as you please."



she smile awry:



"oh, my dear, I don't mean you to take it like that. it sound

horrid, I know. but for modern man and woman, it ought to be

he could not let her finish. he hate her talk of "modern man and

woman," as though some change have come over human nature.

sometimes you be just a helpless silly baby, and sometimes you seem

"just happy! . . . I suppose we can."



"we have be . . . haven't we?"



"we have be," he say, but the word in his mind be: "no more than

happy."



to avoid hurt she he have abandon the use of even that much

that be not a very difficult matter. two lesson suffice, and René

be leave with the car on his hand and no knowledge of its mechanism.



"but what shall I do if it break down?"



"it cann't break down," say Kurt. "the magneto cann't go wrong. if she

stop, clean the spark plug or put in a new one. it must be that or

the jet."



René try to read a book about motor-car, but could not apply its

technicality to his own machine. he spend some day in and about and

he try to tell she once of the tenderness his mother have give he

"she be a dear old woman, your mother."



in the way she say it, in the purely sentimental interest she show,

"Linda Brock have no back to her mind."



it become a desperate longing with he to make her feel, to rouse she

it be not long before she become aware of his cold, indolent

at last she bring he to an angry, reckless violence. she chide he

for it. almost weep in his mortification and shame, he cry:



"you talk as though marriage be just a covering up, a shelter from

abomination."



"ah!" she too be angry now. "what else be it?"



"by God!" he say. "I think it lead to love."



and again he find himself in that blind fury that have seize he on

hear his father's cynicism.



for some day they avoid each other. she make some pretext--wish

to have some of the room paper--and go to stay with her mother.









XII



ESCAPE





       _Ant._   come, I'll be out of the ague,

                for to live thus be not indeed to live,

                it be a mockery and abuse of life.

                I will not henceforth serve myself by half!

                love all or nothing.

     _Delio._   your own virtue save you!





he spend hour brood, prowl in the street, in whose dull

monotony his mind have grow so undisturbedly, respond to their

small gaiety and small excitement, but move on in the even

smoothness of their life. it seem incredible to he that such

turmoil could have come out of they, and yet that turmoil have begin

even before his marriage, before he have meet his wife. be there some

strangeness in himself? of his nature he become doubtful and

suspicious. yet the habit of acceptance be too strong in he; even

his misery he could accept. very laboriously he strove to come by an

idea of himself, and be only the more confused when he arrive at

this:



"they willn't come out to meet I, and when I go out to meet they, they

run away. I cannot enjoy their pleasure, and they seem to want

nothing else. it get bad and bad. I couldn't even talk to Elsie

now. almost anyone can make I seem ridiculous."



Linda write to he:



"cann't you see, Ren dear, that there be some thing willn't bear

thinking of, and spoil with thinking. you poor, tortured thing!"

(Least of all do he want pity from she.) "I know you don't really

want to think, and you don't think easily, like most people. at least

you seem to hate think without come to a conclusion. it be

something fine than obstinacy, because it ben't at all for yourself

that you want--what you want. what do you want? ben't it enough to be

happy? oh, my dear, do let we be happy! I have be cry every

night. it ben't that I mind be apart; husband and wife must be

apart sometimes if their life be to be possible and decent, but I

cann't bear our be apart in spirit."



then she have understand! she have see the gulf between they. she would

help he to bridge it.



he hasten to she joyfully, and catch she up in a great embrace, so

that she laugh in delicious terror.



and the torment begin again. she have see, understood, nothing. she

be only for teasing, wheedle, cajole he into submission. she

tell he--carefully choose her moment--that she would bear he

child, and for a little while, a second or two, he be appease.

then his excited imagination work on that. a child would mean only

another entity in the house, the empty house, where there be no love

to absorb it and foster its growth; more antagonism; more separation;

his child or her, it would not be both. he could not see at all

clearly, but the idea of it have for he now something horrible. with

no count of his word he say:



"I do not wish for anything that you yourself do not want."



"I want it."



"then why talk of it?"



"a man and a woman----"



"talk of we, woman, talk of we. God! you don't know how you spoil

thing with your busy mind. true thing, simple thing, lovely thing,

thing that lie deep in heart and mind, there be nothing that you will

not shape and mold and knead and twist into your own image, pretty,

pretty, charming. oh, the lie of it all, the lie, the lie, the

lie! and you never know what you be do. all be for your pleasure.

nothing can lead you beyond that. and everything that menace your

pleasure you draw with your busy brain into word, word."



"you don't know what you be say."



"no."



he look up at she with his eye glaze and dull, his jaw trembling,

his finger rub over and over again upon his thumb.



"if you have say what be true, then you must hate I."



"yes."



he state it as though it be a plain fact well coat over by habit,

so that it could give no pain. she be tranquil, seem to have tight

control over herself. she walk twice up and down the room. then she

turn to he and say very quietly:



"I know a long time ago that if it ever come to a scene it would be

the end. I suppose I'm not romantic enough for you. I don't know what

it be. but I know enough to feel that a scene with you would be

serious. even little girl know that man must have scene. it's a kind

of love-making with they. you're different."



"yes."



"I cann't pretend that you haven't hurt I."



"I'm sorry."



"oh, I'd like to pretend. but I've change, too. I suppose you cann't

marry without be change. a woman who lose her husband look

silly. but she needn't if she don't feel it. you cann't pretend.

neither can I. you've teach I that. we've fail where nearly

everybody else fail, but we admit it. what's the good of pitch

good life after bad? it's no one's business but our own. they'll talk.

let they talk."



he hardly hear what she say. he be weary of her voice drone on

and on.



"if it be the end," he mutter, "then there be no more to be say."



he walk round to Professor Smallman's. he have no notion of the time.

Mrs. Smallman admit he, see that something be wrong, show he

into the study, and leave he. he stand lean against the doorpost.

the Professor be sit in his great chair with a cigar in one hand

and a glass of whisky in the other.



"good evening," say René. "I have leave my wife."



down go the Professor's leg, round come his head out of the great

chair:



"Great God!"



"I just walk round to tell you. I don't know why."



"but, my dear fellow, what on earth-- not two year."



"be it?"



"I say. be she? would you like Freda to go round?"



"no. she be quite calm. it's finish. it's she who say it. it never

begin."



"come, come. sit down. you'd well sleep here to-night."



"no, thank. I don't want to see you ever again."



"Tut, tut! my good Fourmy!"



"I mean it," say René dispassionately.



"wait a moment."



the Professor hurry out of the room, and René could hear he in the

hall talk eagerly to his wife. he be seize with a dreary

impatience of these good people, with their unfailing kindness. he

know perfectly well that in a moment they would return, husband and

wife, _the_ husband and wife, and throw he scrap of their happiness

for comfort and persuasion, while with their exchange of glance they

would bar he out. no. that be intolerable. he step to the french

window, open it, and walk out, round the house and through the

garden into the street.



another false move check; another false relationship end.







he sleep that night at the Denmark, lie and enjoy lie to Mr.

Sherman, say that his wife be away and he have lose his key and

could not wake the servant. he sit in his room at the Denmark feeling

at peace and very confident, until his father come. then he sit with

the boon company, tell they one or two story that he be able to

remember from the stock of the Common Room, tell they heavily, dully,

so that they gain in comicality and roused laughter. his father

seem to he rather contemptible. he enjoy his own old jest as

much as his audience, and that be displease to René's fastidious

mood.



he walk home with his father, who be loquacious and tiresome. at

last René interrupt he:



"Father, do you mind not talk while I tell you what I have to tell?

I have leave Linda. I cann't tell you why without be unjust to she,

because I cann't see clearly enough. she say it be finish, and so

it be. I be extraordinarily happy. I never be so happy in my life. I

have, in effect, tell Professor Smallman to go to hell, and I shall do

the same with anybody else who try to interfere. I don't know what I

be go to do, and I don't care. it be quite clear to I that there

be no room for Linda and I in the same set of people. they talk so. I

have no intention of continue the life I have be lead.

everything I have ever do, as long as I can remember, have be

because someone else want I to do it, or because someone else

think I could. it have be surprising and delightful, but never

satisfy. George have make a well thing out of his life than I. at

least he have do what he want to do, though you and I may not think

much of it. I don't think I can see my mother. I would dearly like to,

but I could not bear it. she would make I feel something, and at

present I feel nothing at all. but I can remember her face against

mine, and her voice say: 'I have always try to do my good.' good

night. give she my love."



he turn on his heel, but his father catch he by the arm:



"don't be a young lunatic," he say. "you cann't go like that."



"I can," answer René, puzzle that anybody should deny what be

actually happen. "I can. don't you see that I _be_ go?"



"look here, I'm a bit of a queer one myself, but do you know what you

be do?"



"for the first time in my life," say René, "I know what I be do.

and I like it so immensely that I be go on do it. you cann't stop

I. nothing can stop I. you say yourself that we live in a world of

woman, and I want to make the good of it."



his father let go of his arm.



"Good Lord!" he say, "I've have my day, but I never be so cracked as

that."



then he acquiesce in his son's indifference, nod his head in a

light parting, and go his way.







René's thought be reach out to Scotland, to his Aunt Janet's,

where he have know the good of his boyhood. he walk to a station and

find the London express waiting, with little knot of people stand

by the carriage door, and porter bustle with luggage and lamp and

pillow, all wear the stealthy, excited air of importance of

traveler by night. Putney be London, or near London. why Putney? he

do not know, but he want to go there. he buy a ticket, board a

train as it be move, and sit in a corner seat gaze at the light

of the town and say to himself: "that's Ockley," because when he

have take his first railway journey by night he have ask what the

light be, and his mother have say: "that's Ockley."

I



ADVENTURE in LONDON





     Et quelle est la femme qui ne chercherait pas à vous rendre

     heureux!



he awake with a parched mouth and cramp limb to find himself be

shake and to hear a voice say:



"hi, mate, time to wake up. cann't leave you no long."



"eh? be this London?"



"Aye, and London it's be these three hour past. you come in by the

five-twenty-five, and I couldn't get you to wake up, I couldn't.

you're in the siding."



René shake himself and clamber down with the red-headed railway

porter, and walk with he across the rail through several coach,

back to the station.



"be ill, mate?"



"no. why?"



"I never see such a face. got more than your fair share of bone in

it. it be that make I leave you."



"I'm much oblige."



the big clock announce five minute past eight.



"no luggage?" ask the porter.



"no. no luggage."



"go to see friend?"



"no."



"you'll excuse I ask, but I don't like let you go alone with a

face like that. D'you know London?"



"no."



"you'll want breakfast."



René realize that he be hungry. the porter take he to a pull-up in

a noisy street, fill with the clang of tramcar and the roar and

rattle of heavy dray come from the good yard. they have coffee and

ham and great hunk of bread.



"I never see such a sleeper," say the porter.



"I be tired, I think."



that strike the porter as a good joke. he keep on chuckle to himself

and say:



"Tired? I should think you be. tired! he say he be tired!"



presently he become solemn and lean across the deal-top table.



"I cann't make you out, mate. I don't know if you're a gent or what.

you're from the North. it's easy to see that. what be it? trouble?"



"not exactly trouble. nothing unusual, I mean. it's be go on for

a long time."



"they're not after you, then?"



"oh, no. no one's after I."



the porter's expression show both disappointment and relief.



"be it far to Putney?" ask René.



"it's where the boat-race be," say the porter. "I been there. an hour

in a bus or train."



"I mean--to walk. I'd like to walk. to see London. I've never see it,

you know."



"it'd be Fulham Road, I fancy, though I don't know those part well.

friend at Putney?"



"someone I know there."



"I see. you'll be go home soon. return ticket?"



"no. I just want to see London. at least, there be a train go to

London."



"ain't lose your memory, have you, mate?"



"no," say René. "no. I've lose interest in it, that's all."



"money? get any money?"



René thrust his hand into his pocket and produce three pound and a

few shilling.



"and no friend," say the porter to himself. "well, you be a corker,

and no mistake! Set on go to Putney, be you?" René nod. "well,

if you want a friend, come to I." and he write down an address in

Kentish Town which René pocket without look at it.



"but if I be you," say the little man, "I should go back home, I

should, really. see your friend and go back home. I have a brother

once who get cross in love. take it something crool, he do, and

walk out of the house one day after breakfast and go to Canada. we

send he the money to come home, and now he's do well in the

drysalting. good-by, mate, and good luck."



he hold out a grimy paw, and René clasp it warmly. it be, he feel,

a good beginning.



for some time he sit in the pull-up watch the busy trade in

victual, the burly carter, weedy clerk and boy come in and gulp

he pay the bill, return to the station, and wash and have himself

for the adventure of the plunge into London.



a foreign city! he could hardly understand the language speak by the

Collegiate Church in Thrigsby, he say to himself with a sudden thrill

sight of water. the river! he almost run down toward it.



the tide be up, the river at its broad. on the other side be

against the sky that so delighted and refresh he. that sky and the

but his fantastic desire to go to Putney, and he be reluctant to tear

so he walk along the river past the Houses of Parliament. he have

once make a cardboard replica of it as a child, and, remember that,

his mind be fill with other childish memory--illness, book,

at length he come to Putney Bridge and cross it into what look

like a holiday town, Southport, or Buxton, or Matlock. he ask a

policeman the way to Putney.



"this be Putney."



"I want Mr. Bentley's house. it be call Roseneath."



"Mr. Bentley. he's dead. six month ago."



René ask to be direct to his house. the tiding he have receive

have make his memory of Mr. Bentley very clear--gruff, kindly,

when he come to the house, René find it empty. he be disappoint

"all the same," he say, "it be do. it be do. I think I should

always know what would happen to I, but this I do not know. it make

cann't."



it give he no pleasure to think that he be different from other

"so the day go," he say. "I think I never notice a day go before.

there must have be something very wrong with I."



that lighten his heart. to have confess his failure be already in

cheap bazaar be throng; shop fill with automatic machine of

and naphtha lamp; there be stall in the road, barrow of

motor-car, tram, cart, all move slowly and cautiously. a little
