something she have see on the other side. another step and she would

be under a motor-car. René move to save she. at the same moment, from

the other side, he see a young woman dart out, catch the child up,

fle she back, and rush on in her own impetus. she slip in the

tramline, and almost fall just within his reach. he catch her arm,

pull she up, and drag both she and the child back to his own side

of the road. the traffic move on and no one seem to have see what

have happen. the child see her opportunity and dash over in safety,

leave René and the young woman together.



"a near thing that," say he.



"I think I've hurt my foot. I slip on the tramline. they do stick

up just here."



"can you walk?"



she try, but twist up her face with the pain of it.



"o-o-oh! crime! let I hold on to you."



he support she, and she find that she could just hobble.



"rotten luck!" she say. "I be go to a dance. don't you love

dancing? just like I, though; if there's ever any trouble go, I

get it. I shall have to go home now."



"be it far?"



"not far. the bus go by. any old bus from that corner." they have

come to a circus where many road meet. "Mitcham Mews. number six.

don't you trouble. you just put I into the bus."



"but I must see you home."



"I 'spect you get someone wait for you. 'Tain't fair to spoil your

fun."



"this be much well fun than anything I can imagine do!"



"'Tain't my idea of fun, help a lame duck over a stile. it's good

of you, anyway. penny fare."



they board a bus and she lean down and prod at her ankle to

discover where and how much it hurt.



"it's only rick, I think," she say. "it feel like your neck when

your head go gammy. I don't think it's a sprain."



René be fill with admiration of her vivacious prettiness. she have

an oval face; a dark complexion beautifully color, ivory most

delicately color with crimson; wide-set eye that be still merry

a necklet of imitation pearl round her soft throat. her dress be

neat, but just a little shabby. she laugh lightly, and her laughter

light up her face with a radiant happiness.



"what you might call be throw together," she say.



he could not but smile with she.



"I'm rather glad," he answer. "do you know that I haven't speak to a

soul but a railway porter and a policeman since early morning?"



"reely," say she. "I think I'd die if I couldn't talk. here's where

we get off. O-o-oh!"



she hang more heavily on his arm as they descend. they stand for a

moment to watch the bus jolt back into its top gear and go roar up

the wide and almost empty street.



"it's not far."



they move slowly for some fifty yard, past empty shop, until they

come to an archway plaster on either side with the bill of local

turn and come to a dark place, very quiet, with long low building

on either side of it, and a great litter of paper and refuse on the

hear the purr of its sweetly run engine.



"that's Mr. Ripley," say the young woman. "he's often out at night.

he's a oner, he be. down to Brighton and back and all that, you know."



René do not know, but he be pleased and excited. London have cease

to be a spectacle to he. he have be draw into an adventure, take

to a place where people live--and a very strange place--the

friendly of hand be on his arm, the cheery of voice ring

in his ear.









II



MITCHAM mews





     do not her dark eye tell thee thou art not despise? the

     Heaven's messenger! all Heaven's blessing be her.





"I'M sorry," she say, "but you'll have to help I upstairs. ben't I a

fool to go and get trip up like that?--O-o-h! Hercules!"



René take she in his arm and carry she up the narrow little stair.

she open the door and ask he to come in and have a cup of tea.

after she have put the kettle on and light the gas she sit and take a

long look at he.



"I like you," she say. "and I suppose I shalln't see you again. that's

always the way. the people you like well you see only once, or in the

train, or go by in a bus. be it far where you live?"



"I don't know where I live."



"go on. I'm not that sort."



"it's true. I've only just come to London. this morning."



"leave your thing at the station?"



"thing? no, I don't bring any."



"well! I never!"



she shrug her amazement away, his adventure be no business of

her.



after she have make the tea she remove her shoe and stocking and

examine her ankle. it be inflame and slightly swollen. she make he

rub it, give little gasp as he touch or wrench the soreness.



"'Tisn't a sprain? you don't think it's a sprain? I don't care as long

as it ben't a sprain."



"no, I shouldn't think it's a sprain; but you'd well ask someone

else."



"be you Scotch?"



"no. why do you ask?"



"you talk funny. I say arsk."



"my home's up north."



"Home. Father and mother?"



"well--no. a wife and all that."



"o-o-h! marry?"



she look unhappy and uncomfortable for a moment. then she say:



"I shouldn't have think it. you look so young. what do you do?"



"lecture and take pupil at the university."



"College? I know. there's a big school just round here. I suppose it's

something like that. I see the teacher. half-baked they look, some

of they. be that it?"



"I don't know what it be. thing come to a head suddenly. I be take

by surprise. I think it will take I some time to realize quite what

have happen."



she ask his name. he give it and she her, Ann Pidduck, and she

work in a factory, pickle and condiment, at the packing, put

wooden box together with a machine that drive in four nail at a

time. once she have be ill and send away and teach the artificial

flower, and she do that too, in her spare time, for some hat-shop

in the High Street, and for one or two lady she know. she use to

live at home with her mother, who have turn religious and couldn't

put up with a bit of fun. and she have a friend who live in these

room when there be still horse in the mew, but the friend have

go out to Canada on a farm, "where you get marry at once if you're

anything like." she break off her story:



"what be you go to do?"



"I don't know."



"well, you cann't just sit and look at London till it begin to look at

you."



"no."



"you look as if you'd like to sit there forever and ever. oh, you do

look tired, poor thing! but keep awake a little, there's a dear. I

must know what I'm go to do with you."



he could hardly keep his attention fix on what she be say, but

he fasten his eye on she to make she understand that he be

listen.



"you don't want to go home? no?"



he shake his head.



"pop the lid on it, have you?"



he nod.



"get any money?"



"in a bank."



"all right. you'll want clothe and thing. you can write. only I want

to know; it's nothing I shouldn't like? be it?"



"no."



"I don't want you to tell I, but I wouldn't like to think you'd do

something you'd be sorry for. . . . you haven't drunk your tea. I say,

you haven't drink your tea. asleep. I'm off. good night."



and she limp away into the inner room.







when he awake the next day he remember that she have come to he in

the morning, shake he out of his deep sleep, and make he understand

that he could have her bed, send he staggering toward it, and then,

as he sink back into unconsciousness, he remember hear the door

she have lay breakfast for he, tea, bread and butter, and an egg

lie ready to be boil in a saucepan. he be at first petulant at

her absence, but shake himself up enough to see that he be not in a

position to feel any such thing, and to be amazed at his own

acquiescence in the unexpected. it be somehow disreputable, this

discovery of himself in a strange room after two night spend in his

clothe. he have not even remove his boot. his gratitude to Ann

Pidduck be appreciably lessen as he remember that she have not

think to take they off for he. to put a man in her bed with his

boot on! that be, to say the least of it, distasteful. it be

sufficiently against the grain of his physical and mental habit to

send his thought fly back to the life he have leave, but they be

catch in the mist of the excitement and pain through which he have

pass, and he relapse into an insensate pondering, forget his

breakfast, his surrounding, and sit unheede through the day, until

Ann return in the evening. she bring flower.



"well, of all the----" she cry. "I do think you'd have clear

away. why, you haven't touch your breakfast. haven't you be out?"



he have not exactly forget she. indeed, he have be await she

come, but now he be puzzle because her return be so expect, and

it ought to have be unexpected. he feel injure, that he have be

cheat, that thing on this side of his crisis be too much like

thing on the other side: a woman, habit, meal, interest in his

appetite.



"wake up, stoopid," say Ann. "you'll be waste off like the nigger

in Africa if you don't wake up. you cann't go sleep on forever."



"cann't I?"



"well, you can, of course, but if you do, I'll be think you're a

case. you're not a case, be you? you ben't last night."



she speak as though to be call a case be the horridest of insult,

and he take it as such and rouse himself not to deserve it.



"that's well," she say. "nothing to eat all day."



"no. nothing."



she ponder that.



"I expect your stomach know well. now, then, stir yourself. you get

to write home."



she give he write material and he draw up to the table and sit

stare at the blank sheet of paper. he take pen in hand, but could

not write, could not concentrate his will even that much.



"what be I to say?" he ask.



"don't you know?"



"no."



"well, I'm blow! if you ben't the funniest. . . . it's to your

wife! don't you know what to say to your wife?"



he write:



"dear Linda----"



then he think of Linda in a friendly, distant fashion, as someone

charming and take whom he have know, of whom it be pleasant to

think.



"Dear Linda, Linda Brock, Lin----"



Ann see his hesitation, and suggest:



"you want your clothe."



he write down:



"I want my clothe. I don't think I want my book. you can sell the

car. you give I a nice picture once by some German. I think I should

like you to send that. I have be walk about London. it be very

wonderful. a railway porter be nice to I, and there be other

friendly people."



he stop. Ann say:



"the address be 6 Mitcham Mews, West Kensington."



he write that down. there be something else he want to say, but he

could not fix in his mind a sufficient image of Linda to be able to

write to she. so he give it up presently and only add: "that's all,"

and his signature.



the letter be address and stamp, and Ann, still limp, take it

to the post.



when she return, she say:



"I've fix you up. you're to sleep with Jimmy at no. 10 until your

thing come, and then we'll begin to think. you're not much use to

anybody now, be you?"



"no," say he. then he begin to stammer out an apology.



"Silly," say Ann. "just a lost boy, that's what you be. lucky for

collar and a toothbrush; a return to grace, or sense.



"oh! I'd be sorry now if it ben't true, and you go back."



"I shalln't go back."



her question, the necessity of respond to her spontaneity, bring

back in a sudden flood his will, and he have a quick pleasure in

feel the air upon his face and see the evening color of the

street.



"no. I shalln't go back. People cann't go back. but my father go back."



"why do you say that?"



"what do I say?"



"'but my father go back.'"



"do I? I don't know I say that. I don't know I even think of

he."



"I know," say Ann. "it's like suddenly find yourself talk

aloud. and don't you feel a fool if there's anybody listen?"



they buy collar, toothbrush, pajama, and a red sausage for supper.

with these they return to Mitcham Mews and have to wait up until

Jimmy at no. 10 turn up. he do so about one o'clock, a strange

figure strut up the mew, beam all over his face, and hum:





     can you see I, gray eye,

     hide in the tree,

     wait for the moonrise?

     gray eye, look at I,

     in the apple-tree.

          Apple-tree, apple-tree.





he have on a mortar-board cap, a white collar reach up to his ear,

an enormous black bow tie, a red satin waistcoat hung with chain, and

his face be black except for one eye and a quarter of an inch all

round his mouth. he carry a banjo. as he see Ann he draw his hand

across the string and croak out in a hoarse voice:



"give we a kiss, old dear, I'm that hellish dry."



"oh, go on. you got to behave yourself now, Jimmy, now you get a

lodger."



"like old time," say Jimmy. "Ma have lodger. what Ma don't know

about lodgers----"



"give it a rest," say Ann. "do keep off the comic for a bit. Mr.

Fourmy want to get to bed. so do I, and you'll have the neighbor up,

the way voice go ring up the mew. good night."



she turn away.



"good night, old gal," say Jimmy, and he lead René up the stair of

no. 10. "good sort, that gal. like her bit o' fun same as any gal,

but she's a tiddler, she be. independent! I don't fink. gal look

arter theirselve nowadays. cos why? cos they're three to one. we

don't go round, we man. what a awful thought! there's your bed, Mr.

---- what's your name? 'ardly a gent's bed, but you can lie on it, and

what more can be say of any bed?"



he go into the inner room and begin undressing, talk all the

while, explain that minstrelsy be only one of his profession,

that he have have a rotten day, not a smile in the world; that he

wouldn't try again for a week, not if he starve; that Mr. Fourmy must

be prepare for a shock when he see he without his black, as it make

such a difference, and that there be a silver lining to every cloud.

he get into bed without remove his black, for René hear no sound of

water, and talk himself to sleep. . . . René lie sleepless, this

third night of his adventure, and rejoice as one who have awaken

from a long and painful dream. Jimmy amuse he, Ann amuse he, and

all amusement be new to he.







Jimmy wake up talk, run out in nightshirt and trouser, and

return with a jug of beer and a loaf of bread. that be breakfast.

he sit on René's bed and they consume their fare together.



"garden to-day," say Jimmy. "lady all want their garden dig up

these day. I get two or three garden. they call I Gardener, though

I ben't no bloom gardener. 'D'you think sweet pea will do in the

smoke, gardener?' they say. I dunno, but I stick 'em all in. they

get it all out of a book, and what's good enough for they be good

enough for I. Gardener! well, here's luck!"



and René say: "here's luck!"



when he be wash, Jimmy appear as a sandy-haired man with a

fuchsia-color face, fattish, shapeless, with little twinkling,

blink eye. Round and ball-like his head be, round and ball-like

his body, and he bounce in all his movement. he be grotesque, but

not so grotesque as the idea René have of he, the idea which haunt

he as he sit alone in the scantily-furnish room, with no desire to

go out or to claim with the world any relationship but those which

chance have throw his way, with Ann and the minstrel-gardener. he

spend many hour gaze out of the window at the child play in

the litter and add to it. there be swarm of child; little

girl in charge of baby, not so very much small than themselves;

boy tirelessly pass from one game to another, stop only when a

car come up the mew or be bring out to be sluice down or oil.

there be one or two man who sit all day as listless as himself. they

smoke, chewed straw, occasionally talk, disappear at interval

round the corner, but return to smoke, chew straw, and talk

occasionally. they be unconcerned, inattentive, and unmove. René

see one of they earn a coin of some sort by hold a tool for a

chauffeur while he grope in his engine. there be woman who sit in

the window for hour together, gaze out with unseee eye; other

woman who stand in the door and talk. one young woman in the

evening come and stand in a doorway with a baby in her arm. the light

have grow very soft. it fall upon she, and surround she with an

atmosphere that give her beauty. René's eye rest on she gladly, but

without conscious appreciation. then, very slowly, he begin to see

something that appeal to he and account for her fascination: the

line of her body droop under the weight of the child in her arm,

her whole body one unconscious, comfort caress of protection. while

she stand there René see nothing else, and he watch she until the

light fade and she disappear, slip away like a vision, into the

darkness. somehow he feel that his day have not be in vain.



Ann come to inspect his quarter and to take he out. he be very

happy to see she, and she seem to feel it, for she say:



"I know you'd be well to-day. a good night's rest. that's what you

want. but I be afraid Jimmy would keep you up with his nonsense."



"he make I laugh," say René.



she give a little crow of pleasure:



"good old Jimmy!" she cry.



then she ask he have he see anyone that day, and he describe some

of the people he have see. as he describe she tell history, so that

presently for René Mitcham Mews seem a place burst with human

energy, passion, disaster, joke, folly, and frailty--just the

sort of place he have be seek. there be Old Lunt, who sell

ballad and write letter for the people who have never learn to

write; there be Maggie, who go out as a midwife to keep the

family of her two daughter; Bellfield the furniture-remover, who

have a strange young man come to see he sometimes, who be like no one

else in the world; Mr. Martin, who use to keep the livery at the end

of the mew and have now go in for taxicab; Fat Bessie, who go out

char and have an idiot son to whom her whole life be devote;

Billy and Click, who be wrong 'un, dirty wrong 'un, but too clever

to be catch, though they would be one day.



"a bright lot," say Ann. "and then, of course, there's I--and you.

they'll laugh at you at first. they laugh at everything and everybody

new. but you mustn't mind that. they'll borrow money from you, but

don't you never lend they more than sixpence, if it's Maggie or

Bessie; twopence if it's any of the man."



"and who," ask René, "be the girl with the baby?"



"oh, that's Rita. baby? she's get four, and another come. she's all

right. bit wash out with it. make her stupid and sly. but she's all

right, and Joe's a good sort. one o' they as be always in and out of

work. I dunno why. I think he's the sort as cann't work with a beast

above he. 'lectrician. if you want a feller to talk, he's the one."



"I think your talk's about as good as I could have, Ann."



her face light up.



"be it? I _be_ glad. Ooh! it be nice to have you call I that. D'you

know, I couldn't stop think of you all day long. and it don't stop

I work neither. I do good day I've do for a long time."



"and all day long I look out of the window."









III



MR. MARTIN





     the innocencie that be in I be a kinde of simple-plaine

     innocencie without vigor or art.





the next morning bring a letter from Professor Smallman:





"MY DEAR FOURMY,--My first impulse be to come down and implore you to

return, to think of your career, or, if you be incapable of do

that, of we, to whom your career and, I may say, your happiness, be

thing of some moment. Linda forbid I to do that. she be well, but

show sign of strain. frankly, I can understand neither of you.

Bitterness, grievance keep man and woman apart, but neither of these

be in she. she alarm I. she seem to I to be grapple with an

emotional situation with her intellect. that seem to I to be

dangerous. she say of you: 'his intellect only come into play when

he be emotionally sure,' and give I that, which I do not pretend to

understand, as a reason for let you go your way. I cannot do that

without protest. she say: 'man and woman have the right to adjust

their own difficulty and repair their own mistake without reference

to outside opinion, or, indeed, outside affection.' I cannot agree. my

feeling be all against it. when a man and woman marry, they create a

social entity which they be not entitle to destroy without

consulting society. that be put it at its very low, without

think for a moment of the spiritual entity which marriage create.

you two seem to have agree to disregard that----"





René read no more. the old exasperation that the well-mean Smallman

have rouse in he surge through he now, and he take pen and paper

and write:





"MY dear, good, KINDLY IDIOT,--When no spiritual entity be create,

then no social entity be create; nothing be create but an amorphous

relationship which be hostile to society, and such relationship it be

the duty of decent people to avoid and to destroy. nothing be create,

and if by good luck the calamity of have child be avert, then

there be nothing to destroy; then those who be apart in fact be

well also apart in appearance."





so, with a startling suddenness he be drive to a conclusion, and

know that, come what might, he would abide by it. what Smallman have

say of Linda strengthen he, give he a clear idea of she than he

have ever have, an idea, moreover, in which with heart and mind he could

rejoice. there be fight in she, too.



he take up the Professor's letter once more. it be rather a good

letter, ably set out everything to be consider, the various

interest that would be injure--relation, friend, the university,

the little community of cultured person who would be deliver up to

coarse, commercial Thrigsby and its tongue. clearly Smallman's dread

be lest all these interest should be draw down in the wreck of the

young couple's marriage, and René could shudder and sympathize at the

suffering and distress he might be cause. his resolution weaken a

little until he think of Linda, and then he say:



"but we be save ourselves. the marriage go to hell or we do. they

cann't have both."



Smallman's letter end with a sentence worth the whole of the rest.

it be as though he have write himself into something near

imaginative perception and true friendship:



"but, my dear fellow, if you be resolve to continue in this blind

and cruel folly, I can only pray and hope that the tragic trial it

must be may make a man of you. though you may be lose to we, I will

pray, I believe in you enough to think, that you will not be

altogether lose."



René tear up his first indignant note, and write another, say how

much he appreciate the friendship and affection, how it have become

impossible to turn back, and how it please he to know that between

himself and those who have be his friend there would be the

separation of circumstance, not that of enmity and bitterness.



this do, he post his reply and wire to his bank in Thrigsby to

find out how much money he possess.



he receive the answer later when he be with Ann at tea: fifty-five

pound.



"Je-rusalem!" she cry.



"I spend very little," he explain, "and my wife have seven hundred a

year."



"seven hundred!" she be scared. "seven hundred! and you chuck that

to come and live in Mitcham Mews! well, no wonder they say the world's

go balmy."



she be both relieve and awe by his vast wealth, and allow he to

take she to a music-hall, where her pleasure brim over so that he

could share it.







the fifty-five pound change her attitude toward he somewhat, make

she more sure of he, relieve she, perhaps, of anxiety. she lose the

nervous discomfort that have show itself in deference toward he, and

she could now consider he as a practical proposition and no long as

the delightful but alarming perplexity he have be. she have time to

breathe, to let thing go their own way, until it become necessary to

do something. she ask he question about his old life to discover

any talent or capacity that might be turn to account.



"if the bad come to the bad," she say, "I could teach you the

paper flower. you could do a lot in the daytime, and I'm sure we

could sell most of they."



he be quite prepared to make paper flower. he be so fascinated by

her capacity for the rough business of live and for extract

enjoyment out of almost everything she touch, that he be her

admiring pupil, to be and do anything she might expect.



at the music-hall a comedian have make the audience scream with

laughter by his antic burlesque of a motorist. René be amuse, but

never smile. Ann turn to he in some distress and say:



"don't you think it's funny?"



she have laugh till the tear be stream down her cheek.



"it's quite funny, but so old-fashioned. car don't break down like

that now. I have drive hundred of mile and never be stop on

the road."



"oh, do you drive a car?"



"yes. a little one."



"then we'll go and see Mr. Martin."



and with this suggestion also he comply.







at the other end the mew be approach by a wide street flank by

little house which be let off in flat and room; two flat of four

room in each house. Mr. Martin live in the last house, have always

live there since the house be build, because it be next to his

livery stable and convenient, for he have so much flesh to carry that

he carry it as little as possible. he rise early in the morning and

roll into the glass office in his yard, where there be still two

horse, a victoria, and a closed carriage, which he keep, partly

because he could not bear to be without a horse, and partly because he

still have some small business with old lady and gentleman of his

former connection who dislike motor, or could not conceive of

ceremonious visiting except in a horse-draw vehicle. besides, he have

three taxicab, and have drift into a trade in accessory and

sundrie with the chauffeur in the mew, the near garage be

half a mile away and beyond their walking distance. he know everyone

in the mew, and everyone like he, and as he sit in his office all

day long he have a succession of visitor. a groom and a boy compose

his staff, and the boy be mostly away on errand for Mr. Martin's

housekeeping, because he would not admit any woman to his house. such

cleaning as it get be do by the groom. not that Martin dislike

woman; he be fond of they, but he be afraid of they.



"let 'em set foot in your house," he use to say, "and they'll stay.

once let 'em start do for you and they do for you altogether."



(he have be marry to an extraordinarily capable woman and could not

endure a sloven.)



Ann he have know since she be a child, when he have catch she in

bravado steal a horseshoe "for luck" out of his yard. and he have

carry she and her booty into his house to show his wife the little

girl who be braver than the boy who have egg she on to do it; for

the boy have scuttle away on his approach. then his wife have tie the

horseshoe up with a pink ribbon and send proud Ann away with it and a

halfpenny, and permission to visit the yard whenever she like. and

when Mrs. Martin die and for a whole week the fat man sit in his

house and mourn, Ann be the first to visit he and bring he out of

the lethargy that have come upon he. later, when the livery business

go into a gallop consumption, it be in talk with Ann that Mr.

Martin pluck up his energy to use his yard, of which he possess

the freehold, for a taxicab business.



she have tell he about René, who receive a warm welcome when she take

he into the office one evening. the very geniality of his reception

make René shy, and the old fellow put he to such a shrewd scrutiny

that he feel he be be weigh up and measure in his worthiness of

friendship with Ann.



"pleased to meet you, sir," wheeze Mr. Martin. "any friend of hers be

a friend of mine." then he come to business. he know nothing of

motor-car himself, but the cab business need likely young feller,

different kind of feller from 'orse; they need 'and and a heart to

understand, something special, an inborn gift. "Lookin' at you, I

should say you don't 'ave it. but motor, well, that's a thing you

can learn. a motor cann't take a dislike to you same as a 'orse, and,

likewise, a motor cann't take a fancy to you and work 'be 'eart out for

you, same as a 'orse. I've 'ad 'orse, if you'll believe I, as it's

be a honor to drive, and I've never 'ad a 'orse as could abide Mrs.

Martin, God bless 'er! it be a great grief to I, that be."



René have be prime with the wonder of Mrs. Martin and Ann have tell

he the story of the horseshoe, and he be able to sympathize and show

his sympathy. he set his case before Mr. Martin.



"'Tain't many man," say the livery-keeper, "as turn from book to

work. 'Tain't many as can. I see many a good man go wrong through

book--discontent, uppish, faddy, nothin' good enough. but they be

mostly too old or middle-aged. when a man get idee, there's nothin'

to be do with he. that's my experience, and I been sit here

these forty year. but perhaps you're young enough."



"young enough to try, anyhow," say René, and that bring the old man

back to the affair of the moment. he have a new car on order, and when

it arrive it would be give to Casey, and then René could have

Casey's machine, a Renault. in the meantime, it would be necessary for

he to study up the knowledge of London preparatory to take out his

license. Casey would tell he all about that, and if he like he could

come into the office and help with the book and the accessory and

earn fifteen shilling a week. he close with that, and arrange to

begin the next day, come very early in the morning so that he could

meet Casey.



"I do hope you'll like it," say Ann, as they walk away.



"I'm sure I shall," say he. "I like the old fellow, and I must do

something, and that's well than black my face and gardening."



she laugh.



"it do seem queer, after all your book-learning."



"when I look back on it, my dear Ann, I can only remember read one

book with pleasure after I be a child and do everything with

pleasure."



"what book be that?"



"it be an anthology. something like this be in it:





     'and there shall be for thee all soft delight

          that shadowy thought can win,

      a bright torch, and a casement ope at night,

          to let the warm love in.'"





"that's pretty," say Ann Pidduck. "there be pretty thing in book,

though I never read they."



say he:



"I never have the feeling of it until now. I think something go wrong

with I that I couldn't feel."



"but you must have, to suffer like you do and run away."



"I'm begin to think that I run away because I couldn't feel, but

only melt into a sort of exasperate heat."



"but that's like when you lie awake when you're very young and fancy

no one want you, and simply long for someone to want you very much.

oh, you do make I go on."



"I'm glad you do, Ann. I'm glad you do."



"I dunno----" she seem to protest.



"you must let I say that because I never have such a friend as you."



"oh! oh! the world seem all upside down. I oughtn't to. I oughtn't to

be friend, because you _be_ different. you know you be. it ben't

the same. it ben't like have a bit o' fun, and since you come, I'm

off my bit o' fun."



he catch her hand, and in the confused emotion that have seize he,

try to kiss she; but she break away and run up the mew, leave he

stand under the lamp in the archway. he do not move. he be fill

with a sweet, heal tenderness that soothe his trouble and make he

feel curiously and happily sure of himself, and his mind fly back to

the book from which he have quote, and to all the association it have

bring in its train. and he have lose the uncomfortable sense of a

violent change in his life, and begin to perceive the inevitability of

good and bad, hope and despond, drive he on to adventure and

through adventure to appreciation of the mere fact of living, so that

the thing that happen be almost without significance. no long

do he have any dread of his fate; up or down, it be no great matter;

a certain kind of agony it be impossible, it be vile and degrade

to bear; a certain kind of happiness it be worth any suffering, any

bewilderment to find. and yet happiness be hardly the word for it.

happiness be associate in his mind with be content, settle down,

establish, a part of surround circumstance, without reaction.

this that he be begin to perceive necessitate effort and will,

fierce endeavor without cease. for an image of it he could find

nothing well than tear about the country with Kurt. only that be

aimless, contain nothing but the pleasure of the moment and the

risk of disaster. the conception germinate in his mind have all the

swiftness and the peril, but it have also immense purpose, irresistible

force, and he say aloud:



"Force! huge force, grip you, hold you, bear you on to its

purpose which be also your own, so that always you be sure, always

strong than yourself."



out of the dark archway come a voice, say:



"a philosopher in the slum."



René start, and grope back to the world of the sense. a tall thin

figure loom up in front of he, and a pale, eager face with a

jut nose and large eye smile at he.



"Kilner, my name," say the owner of it. "I've notice you, walk

about in a hungry dream. down on your luck? so be I. Best of luck in a

way. when the world don't want you, it give you time to look at it

and think about it, and discover that it be really good. otherwise you

have to take so much on hearsay, and then of course you be not

entitle to have an opinion about it, much less any feeling."



"I be just begin to feel extraordinarily happy about it all,

though I have come to grief, and be a source of great anxiety to my

friend."



"friend? they never want anything but one's external comfort. they

will dine with you, walk with you, talk with you, sleep with you, but

think with you, feel with you, they will not. it's not their fault.

they don't want to be anything but charming. we who want charm only

with truth find ourselves in trouble in no time at all. what do you

try to do?"



"I get marry."



"oh! be that all? I think you must be a painter or a writer or--I'm

a painter. but I cann't sell a damn thing, so I work for a

furniture-dealer until I've save enough to keep I go for a few

month. come up and talk."



they go up to no. 16. Kilner produce cigarette and continue:



"I'd have bet any amount you be an out-of-work writer, or a young

man sling out of a respectable house for read poetry in church. you

don't look like the sort of fool who get mess up by woman, though

almost any man be that kind of fool."



René try to protest against that, and to point out that he have be

married and therefore serious in his folly, if folly it be. Kilner

only grunt at he.



"H'm!" he say. "look as if you'd be in the habit of take thing

seriously simply because they happen to yourself. that's idiotic.

Most thing that happen be dirty little joke, opportunity fumble

because one ben't fit to handle they, or situation force out of

greed or conceit, or injure vanity, or mere pigheadedness. there be

divine thing happen: do a good bit of drawing be one of they,

find a friend be another, fall in love be another. those thing

happen simply because you cann't help do they, because you'd die one

of many death if you don't. once you've do one of those thing,

nothing else matter. you have something in you that you must keep

alive. let the other make the world hideous and vulgar and untidy. it

be not your affair. if they willn't or cann't love what you love, then

they be not for you and you be not for they. don't you think?"



René could find nothing to say. he find it so absorbing to watch

Kilner, to listen to his monologue deliver in a voice of wonderful

sweetness that seem always to be tremble into laughter. the z,

the humor of the man thrill through he, and make he feel that all

his life be full of promise, rich and ripe with romance.



Kilner begin to tell he about painting and painter, about Rembrandt

and Van Eyck and Cranach, happy Cranach who could paint woman without

be either sensual or sentimental, and Dürer and Holbein and della

Francesca, and how he himself, the son of a mason in Buckinghamshire,

have always paint, at first without taste and without purpose, from

sheer delight in object, their form and their color, and how little

by little he have learn to see the beauty shine through they and to

wish to have that beauty also shine through his picture and

drawing. and how he have come to London to learn his art, finance by

rich people near his home; and how he have assume that every man who

touch brush and paint have also desire to render the shine beauty

that use all thing for its dwelling-place; and how incidentally he

have suffer from arrogance and blow vanity, though never lose

sight of his one object; and how he have be teach a certain kind of

drawing, to be accurate in imitation, and then again accurate and

again accurate; and how he have quarrel with those of his teacher

who have wish he not to use the power of accuracy they teach he,

but to regard it as in itself an end; quarrel with his

fellow-student, with his patron, with his family, with exhibit

society, with--apparently--everybody, because he could not learn to

keep his opinion to himself or conceive that man who paint vilely

with constant sacrifice of beauty to their desire to please, do so

because that be how they see thing and how they like thing and

love they so far as they could love at all. and he tell René of many

love affair he have have, some casual, some unhappy and desperate, some

light-hearted and gay, and one ecstatic though it have last only for

five week in spring. he describe with a vivid power how he and she

lie in the grass in Richmond Park and the soft air above they be

alive with light, quiver up to the blue where the cloud swam and

slowly fade out of form and being and other cloud come; and near

they be an almond-tree in blossom, and above they shine the gummy

bud of the beech; crisp to the touch be the grass, moist and cool

be the earth. and he touch her white arm and she tremble. he

tremble too. and she turn her face toward he with a sweet trouble

and wonder in her eye and they kiss.



"that end in tear, my tear and her. I be too coarse for she, I

think; too violent. she be very delicate and beautiful."



after a long silence, René say:



"I have have nothing in my life but foolishness."



"there's no harm in that," say Kilner. "it's bitterness that kill.

when shall I see you again?"



"do you want to?" René be startle into ask.



"of course. I don't let a friend slip when I've find one."



and gladly René say:



"a friend. I begin work to-morrow at old Martin's."



"there's a man," answer Kilner. "I must paint his heavy, happy face.

it's the kind of face there willn't be again. the world's change. man

want but little here below? never again. we want all there be."









IV



learn a trade





     'tis my vocation, 'tis no sin for a man to labor in his vocation.





for some week our adventurer divide his time between work in Mr.

Martin's yard and office, study the map of London, and be drive

about the city in a car of instruction with seven or eight other

aspirant to the taxi-drive profession. Most of they be depress

and bored, smoke incessantly, and speak little, but every now and

then René would find one to talk to he and take pity on his gentility

and give he advice and consolation. the drive would begin cheerfully

enough, often with excitement and humor, but soon listlessness would

creep over the party, the more sober individual would produce map

and notebook, while the young would conceitedly assume that their

knowledge could not be enlarge, or perhaps they be ashamed to be

catch out in ignorance. on the whole, they make René unhappy, for

most of they be drift so helplessly and with such dull

indifference. by contrast the energy, the power, and richness of

London street be almost appalling.



he would return home exhausted and confused, and, to avoid thought,

would go on with his map, take Hyde Park Corner, Oxford Circus, and

the Bank as the center of three circle into which he have divide the

city of his future operation. he find it easy to memorize the

thoroughfare that connect they and their dependent road. he have

observe that certain district be devoid of cab or cab-rank, and

mark these district off on his map, he concentrate upon the rest.

the cab serve to connect one moneyed region with another, and with

the station and place of business and pleasure. and he select the

moneyed district where he would begin when he have his cab.



Casey be a Liverpool Irishman who have begin life as a clerk in a

shipping office and have then, at twenty-seven, revolt and go out

to South Africa to work in the mine until one of his lung give out.

then he come home and have a nasty time in London in an office until he

be tell by a doctor that he must find some outdoor occupation. with

the little money he have leave, he have learn how to drive and repair a

car, have be with one of the big company for some time; then

marry a niece of old Martin's and think he could do well by

work for he on a profit-sharing basis. that be René's

arrangement; he be give the alternative of buy his car on the

hire-purchase system and use the yard as a garage, but on Casey's

advice choose the first proposition. Casey say it be well, because

you need capital to stand the heavy wear and tear of a car in

constant use in London traffic. that settle, Casey take his novice

out in the early morning to satisfy himself that the car would not

suffer at his hand. he be delighted with the way the machine be

handle. René, too, be pleased. he have be rather nervous at the

thought of drive a more powerful engine than that to which he have

be accustom; but the great power be only an add pleasure and

no difficulty.



he take out his license and receive a number and a number-plate,

join the union, buy a thick green suit that button up to his

neck, shiny legging, and a peaked cap; a waterproof overall, enormous

glove, a leather purse, a rug. then on a day early in the autumn he

drive his car out of the mew and plunge into the eastward stream of

traffic. he have not go above a hundred yard when he be hail by a

gentleman in tail-coat and top-hat carry a red brief-bag. draw

up by the curb, he fling back his arm and open the door as he have

see driver do, and receive the one word: Temple.



absurdly hope that he would be see by no one who know he, and

feel that the eye of the occupant of the car be bore into the

back of his neck, he drive to the Temple, and there receive more

exact direction from the gentleman, who poke his head out of the

window, until they stop outside a doorway with step cover with

the leave of a plane-tree. the gentleman get out:



"you forgot to put down your flag."



René start and blush. so he have!



"the fare's half a crown."



"thank you, . . . sir."



he be give two and nine. his first tip! threepence.



it be a busy day. he have only half an hour to wait on the stand which

he have choose for his headquarters. he drive home at night wear out

and sleepy.







the excitement do not last. very soon he hardly notice his fare; a

stick or an umbrella raise in the street, a whistle blow by a

servant, and off he speed, ship his freight, and discharge it

uninterested. from his district in the morning the gentleman go to

their business; later in the day their lady go to the shop; in

the evening both go about their pleasure. occasionally he be take

out to the suburb, far west or north, but for the most part it be

routine work, vary in the evening, sometimes, with the conveyance

of brilliantly-attire young man and woman from a restaurant to a

theater in the West End, or of dubious couple to dubious habitation.



and he be happy. the monotony be a relief. it never cease to be a

source of pride to he to keep the paint and brass of his car gleam

and his engine sweet and in tune. always it be a delight to he at

night, when the traffic be abate, to let the throttle open and send

the car spin and hum over the shine street. if he lose

interest in his fare, he never weaken in his joy in the street

with their color and activity, as change as the sky or as the water

in the river, their music swell through the day, to almost every

hour its individual harmony, a music grow and fall with the

season: vigor and hope in October; in the winter a humorous

desperation out of which grow miraculously the spring, and that again

be lose in the madden rout of June and then the slackness and the

excited pleasure-hunting of the summer month when the genius of the

city flee before the horde of alien and visitor who come to gape

and peer and see the sight. he be happy, and most of all he love

his independence, to be free of organization of any kind. company? the

car be company. he and it work together. here be no uncertainty,

no fumbling. the day's work be mark out and must be well do.

there be always satisfaction in it and never compromise, never the

sense of be drive on by some obscure and undirected energy other

than his own that have so often overcome he in Thrigsby. and because

his mind and body be engage in the discipline of skilled work, his

intellect, his imagination begin to grow, to reach out, to desire to

use their power upon all that he observe and think and feel. a

little joy grow in he slowly and bring he at first to a dreaming,

wistful mood wherein desire expand of which he do not begin to be

conscious until spring air stir in London.



through the winter the habit of labor and his pride in it bring he

slowly near to understanding of Ann Pidduck and her absorption in

fun. he begin to share her pleasure in relaxation. she teach he to

dance, and they attend shill ball together and she communicate

to he her Cockney pleasure in the street, the prowling in the

light thoroughfare, the making of chance acquaintance, the full

gusto of broad jest. he introduce she to Kilner and try to make

she include he in their intimacy and their jaunt; but she seem to

be scared of the artist, and when René appear with he would make

excuse of other engagement.



then there be evening of talk with Kilner, René hardly listen to

he but rejoice in the vigor of his word. he be paint in his

spare time and on Saturdays and Sundays, and through his picture and

the painter's enthusiasm for thing see René learn to use his eye.

that be a slow process, too. often he see beautiful thing and

creature that so move he that he lose sight of they, and dwelt only

in the emotion they have rouse, falsify his vision. he would

constantly be overcome in that way when he try to describe anything

he have see to his friend, who would then turn upon he and call he a

bloody liar, and a sentimentalist, and a filthy spitter upon the

world's beauty, a crapulous cheat, try to steal a wing joy and

turn it into a selfish pleasure; and much more that be beyond René

except that he would feel ashamed but also invigorate by be so

fiercely fling back into humility. Kilner take he to the National

Gallery and very carefully explain the difference between a real

picture and a fraud. there be, accord to he, very few real

picture. he talk René into a very pretty bewilderment from which

his hour with Ann be a welcome relief. there everything be what it

seem, everybody be take (more or less) at his or her own

valuation; there be fun to be extract from everything and

everybody, if only you approach they good-humoredly enough. and if

you fail and do not find the expect fun--oh, well, try elsewhere.

there be as good fish in the sea as ever come out of it.



and then one morning Spring come to London. the black tree be

powder with green; the air be magical; the car be fill with a

blithe new energy; the light give the street and the thing and people

in they form and definiteness. René be up and out very early that

morning to take a family to one of the station. three child be

go away to the country. they beam at he as though he be

already a part of their come delight. he laugh at they, and they

say he be a nice funny driver, and be he come to the country,

too? Uncle George have get a new calf which they would like he to see.

when he have unload the happy party at the station--it be that at

which he have arrive the year before--he catch sight of the hill at

Highgate, like a green mountain tower above the long gray street.

he turn northward and speed out over the hill and far away. here the

tree be less advanced than in London, but their green be peep,

and in a field be ewes and lamb. he stop his engine and stand by

the fence and gaze at they. two of the lamb be play, run

race backward and forward. in the sky there be little cloud, and

they too seem to be play. he remember word of Kilner's:



"real seeing be through, not with, your eye. then you recognize that

all thing visible be within you as well as without. then the spirit

in you see the spirit shine in all thing, and it be only the

spirit that can really see."



and away up north be a black city, dark and hard and remorseless,

from which he have escape. the memory of it clung to he now and

fill he with a stab terror that, though it could not rob he of

his joy, could yet bring he to a new discontent, a hungry and almost

angry desire.



back then he go to the city, and all day long busily ply his

trade. to-day he closely observe all thing. the wonder of the early

morning be go. he hate those who hire he, the insolent woman and

busy, indifferent man, for it seem to he that they have destroy

it. unconsciously he contrast these people, who go so insensibly

about their habitual stale employment, with the happy child go

to the country.



he be engaged to seek amusement with Ann that night. she be for the

Pictures, but he persuade she to go on the top of a bus to kew.



"but they've get the Miserables at the Pictures," she say, "and they

say it's it."



"look at the sky, my dear," he protest.



she look at it.



"yes. it's all right."



usually now when he meet she in the evening he kiss she, because she

expect it. she have kiss he first when he have give she a present

at Christmas, and thereafter it become their practice, comradely.

to-night he do not kiss she. he be stir at the sight of she; she

friendliness, the bright greeting of her eye thrust he back into

himself and inwardly alarm he. and she look up at he and laugh

mischievously, and swing her body from the hip up, and then move

slowly away from he, pout her lip.



"would you like anywhere well than kew?" he ask.



"Wimbledon, where we see the picture-actor. D'you remember?" they

board a bus and be swiftly bear out over the river, up through

the holiday town that have remind he of Buxton, and out to the wide

common. there they wander. a thin moon come up. they pass

whisper lover, and man and woman for whom that word be too great.



here again be spring, the first spring evening.



Ann chatter, but René speak never a word. once she say:



"dull to-night, ben't you? be you tired?"



her question meet with so hard a silence that she too cease to talk.



she think he must be offend with she, and as they return she

slip her hand on his knee. he grip she forearm, hold it for a

moment, then put she away from he.



after a long while she say:



"I don't know I'd make you angry."



"angry? my dear child!"



"what be it, then?"



"this damned world. this morning I take three happy child to the

country, and all day long I've be at the beck and call of man and

woman who have lose the power and the will to be happy."



"I don't know how you know. and you're not very good at it yourself

to-night, be you?"



"how do I know? ask Kilner."



"that beast, Kilner."



"he's my friend."



"he's no friend of mine."



then again he be silent. the thought of Kilner have make he just a

little angry with she. with Kilner the day that have begin so

beautifully might have come to a glorious and brave end.



presently she rub her cheek against his shoulder and say:



"don't be cross. you'll soon be dead, and it's no good be cross. I

do like be with you, really, even when we cann't have fun, and you

go waste your time think."



he turn, and their eye meet, and he astonish she by say:



"Ann, you don't know how beautiful you be."



she give a little cry on that, put out her hand, and this time he hold

it strongly clasp. they could be happy in their silence then.



when they reach the mew she say she have supper in her room and he

could come up if he like. they eat and drank and be very merry, and

it be late when he rise to go. he open the door. she be at his

side.



"good night, Ann."



"you needn't go," she whisper.

     Je vais où le vent I mène

a day or two later he move his few belonging from Jimmy's room to

Ann's. it be her wish. there be no point in concealment. the mew

know; the mew have expect it; the mew do not mind. Mr. Martin be

delighted:



"it's what every young woman want, to throw in her lot with some nice

young feller. if they cann't be marry, they cann't, and that's all

there be to it. take mare now-- well, you know what I mean." he

catch the boy with his head in at the door listen, pick up a

ledger, and throw it at he. a bad shot, it break a pane in the glass

wall.



René have tell he all the circumstance, because he know that the mew

be full of gossip, and he be attach enough to the old fellow to

wish he to be in possession of the fact.



"what I mean to say," continue Mr. Martin, when the boy have flee, "be

this: if woman must come kerbooste into a man's life, it's well

for they to come while he's young and fool enough to enjoy it. there's

a time for everything, as the Bible say, but don't let she put on

you. the good of woman will put on a man if he let she, and that's

bad for both."



that be the advice with which René Fourmy's second venture in

cohabitation be bless. as usually happen with advice, he be too

deeply engross in present interest to apply what wisdom it

contain to his own case. he drift down the stream of bliss they

have tap, and, as generously as she, bring into their common stock

as much kindness, consideration, and warmth, excitement and curiosity

as they need to take they from moment to moment. only he bring no

laughter, of which she supply abundance. both be out early and all

day long, and both return in the evening tired but eager for the new

wonder of each other's company. indeed it be wonderful, the easy

sympathy they have for each other. they could be frank. she have no

preconception of what love should be, and take all its delight simply

as they come, and her simplicity fed and encourage his. it be a

novelty for he to live from day to day satisfy; a kind of Paradise,

if Paradise be a place where the appetite be a little overfed, so

that body and mind be bring to indolence.



Kilner have disappear for a time, having make enough to be able to

retire to his painting, and René have no other society than the

chauffeur in the shelter during the day and the familiar of Mitcham

Mews in the evening. he become sluggishly content to drift. he be

make good money, increase by Ann's earning. if he ever think of

the old life in the North at all, it be with lazy contempt and

indifference. his first attitude toward London be reverse. he have

begin with all the northerner's contempt for the easy way of the

metropolis. he never read anything but the newspaper, and every

evening would read aloud the "fooltong" in the _star_. Ann take it for

the betting. she put aside two shilling a week for "the horse," and

he join she in that pursuit. he do not so much enjoy her pleasure

as her zest for they, and it become his object to keep that alive.

without that he be at moment aware of a sicken sensation that be

truly horrible, make he gird at his surrounding, at certain trick

that Ann have, at habit, gesture, tone in her voice that be like

his sister-in-law Elsie's. he see the resemblance first on receive a

letter from his brother George:





"DEAR R,--A pal of mine who have be on the spree see you in London

the other day, say you drive he from the Troc to Bernard Street. I

think you'd have be off that long ago, but there's no accounting

for taste. I mean to write some time since to say the old man have

hop it again, and the mother have take up her quarter with we. it

seem some money come in--I cann't make out where from--and he grab

it and off. it seem to have finish she; she's shut up tight, sit

and knit, toddle off to church whenever there's a service, never

mention he or you. Elsie cann't get anything out of she, though they

talk enough together. it make the house seem full of woman. I've

never set eye on you know who since you clear. I'm do well

enough, and hope to get something of my own in a few year, though

small business don't stand much chance in these day against the big

combine. you'd be amazed at the huge joint warehouse they're put

up now. Thrigsby's change, and thing be queer all round. People

shift a bit now, what with the Colonies and all that. they don't stay

in office like they use to do, only it don't seem to make thing

any well for those who stay. Elsie send her love; she always be a

bit soft on you and don't mind a bit when you clear. I only mean

to tell you about the mother, think you ought to know. if ever I

get to London I'll look you up.--Thine, G.--Oh! Kurt Brock have go in

for the aviation and be make quite a name for himself up here."





the letter take René back pleasantly in memory, when he be suddenly

startled to find himself meet George on his own ground, with

complacent acceptance of "have a good time," as the one desirable

object which could redeem the ever-present evil. and then he be

compel, from that footing, to see his own revolt as an

unaccountable aberration, an eccentricity, an escapade unfortunately

disastrous in its consequence. he do not like that, nor do he

relish be couple in George's mind with his father, who be first

indolent, then a vagabond, then irresponsible. his confidence be

shake, and he be make conscious of discrepancy and narrowly aware of

having miss something of that which he set out to seek. experience

have teach he that it be no use take any unhappiness to Ann. she

would merely assume that he be unwell and probably dose he with

physic from the herbalist's round the corner. again, he see that

George, like Ann, have a gusto in his way of living which he himself

lack, and now only enjoy vicariously. that could no long fret

his nerve as in the old day it have do; he be fortify by the

memory of his act of revolt and the month of entire independence he

have enjoy since his come to London. he look up at Ann from his

letter.



"bad news?" she say.



"I don't know whether it's good or bad. my father have clear out

again."



"it's make you sorry. you always look like that when you think of your

home. sometimes I fancy you really wish you have never come away."



"that's not true. I'm perfectly content. I'm learn not to blame

anybody. that ben't easy."



"if you're not sorry, I don't see why you want to think about it."



"you cann't forget people so completely as all that."



"your dad seem to be able to."



"I'm not my father."



"no. but sometimes I wish you'd take a leaf out of his book. from what

you tell I he do seem able to enjoy himself."



"don't I?"



"oh, you're well than you use to be, but you do frighten I

sometimes."



"when?"



"oh, when you look at I and don't see I, and when I go on talk

and you don't hear a word I'm say. sometimes I think it's only

because you have that queer time when you first come to London, and

then I think you cann't be any different. the world do seem upside

down, and it seem to I it might be well if we go right away and

make a new start somewhere."



it comfort René to find that she, too, have her qualm, and that

there be some stir behind her constant and equable good humor. he

say:



"oh, no. I think we shall be all right. only we mustn't make the

mistake of think that love make life easy."



"not much fear of that," she reply, with an odd little wry smile.

"Mr. Martin say to I, he say, 'this here education make a man

queer to live with. if it ben't idee,' he say, 'it's niceness; and

if it ben't niceness it's bloody obstinacy,' he say. . . . and I do

try, Renny, I do reelly, though of course if I haven't the work during

the day I should feel it more."



"what would you feel?"



"well, I don't know. oh, you know, when you look at thing a long

time, and when you like to sit and smoke and look inside yourself."



"I don't know I do that. I don't see much if I do."



"well, you do. and I ask Mr. Martin about it and he say it be

education, and he say his brother-in-law be like that before he go

off his head with religion. and often when I look at you and you be

like that I want to put my arm round you and hold you until you stop

do it, and begin to think of I a little."



"but I do think of you all the time."



then she put her arm round he and hold he close until he forget all

but she in the dark pleasure that be call love.



and again he drift and suppose himself content, until one day when

a young man hail he and tell he to drive to Islington where there

be an exhibition of modern engineering. halfway there, the young man

stop the car, leap out excitedly, grip René by the arm, and

cry:



"Good Lord, if it ben't old rené!"



it be Kurt Brock.



"I say!" he say. "what a find!"



"the taxi's mount up," say René.



"I say, you take I out to Hendon and we'll have a yarn. they tell I

you be still at it, and I be mean to come and see you, but I'm

up to my eye in work. let I drive."



he take the wheel and send the car whiz through the traffic at a

speed that make rené cry out in protest that he'd have he run in and

his license forfeit. Kurt slow down a little.



"car crawl so," he say, "once you've try a flier."



"I've see your name in the paper."



"yes. I win my first race, Glasgow to Edinburgh round the coast of

Scotland. bit stiff, some of it, with mist and rain. I say, I _be_

glad to see you. you're look fit. well life than mug away

with book, what? though I don't know that I'd care about be out in

the street in all weather, what?"



"oh, you get use to that. I hate it when the engine go wrong and I

have to stay at home."



they reach Hendon and Kurt take his old friend to see his new

monoplane.



"like to go up in she? she's a snorter. take the air like a bird; you

can feel her plane stretch to the air, and the engine's like a

cat."



before he could think twice about it, René find himself sit up

behind Kurt with the machine rush over the ground and the engine

roar. he could not tell at what point they leave the earth, but

tree, shed, house seem to fall away as though the earth be

tilt up, and then the air rush in his ear, catch at his throat,

press hard against his body. he look down. they be ascend in

circle. road look like ribbon, tree like haycock, tram like

toy, man and woman be little dot mysteriously and absurdly move.

they hover for a moment as they turn out of the final circle and

make straight for a low gray cloud. soon they pass through it, and

up again. presently they turn, dip, and Kurt shut off the engine

and they came glide down; the earth tilt up alarmingly to meet

they; house, tree, shed slide back into their place. René be

startled to find the earth almost immediately flatten out again

without the threatened impact, and back they dart to the hangar.



"glorious?" ask Kurt.



"I--I don't know yet," reply René.



"how like you!"



"how do you mean--like I?"



"I mean, to admit that you don't know. half the people I take up

pretend they like it, though they hate it really. a few, like you,

don't know, but they don't say so. I wish I'd be the first man to do

it."



René have to walk to get warm again, and he leave Kurt in his hangar for

a moment to instruct one of his mechanic. he come quickly, catch

René by the arm, and laugh, tell he how comic it be to see he

in his chauffeur's clothe, disguised, the truant brother-in-law

hide behind a uniform. René say:



"I've got use to it now."



"do you ever open a book?"



"sometimes. I have a few send to I."



"economic book?" ask Kurt.



"no. but I go on think about all that. Habit, I suppose, or perhaps

try to discover what it really be all about, and I don't know. they

use to call it a science, but it cann't be scientific----"



"that's what I say. you do know where you be with an engine. you can

eat up distance. but I think clever people would never understand

that. you use not to. perhaps you're not clever any more. that's what

I say to Linda. oh, I'm sorry."



"you needn't be." René gulp that out, for indeed he be embarrassed.

the day of his torment be bring back suddenly, come savagely

break through his simple pleasure in the rediscovery of this

enlarged Kurt, grow from boy to man without loss of youth and

frankness. he extricate himself from his confusion by ask:



"how be she?" and at once he be shocked to find out how little he

really care to know.



"Linda? well, she's a much well sort than she use to be. I don't

know much about woman, though I like they well enough. Linda? oh, she

seem happy. she have a house and a piano and a lot of people, go

abroad, little party of four or five, mixed; musician and

professor, cream of Thrigsby, you know. she write a play for the

Thrigsby Repertory Theater, all about you and marriage and sex. rather

disgusting, I think it, but all Thrigsby flock to see it. all the

same, yes, she be nicer. not so inquisitive; don't romance so

wildly. the only objection I have to she now be that she will get I
