unable to do that, and she fancy I know something about you that she

don't, though I've tell she over and over again that I don't pretend

to understand you or anybody else. I do tell she that you make I

feel that what I want to do ben't necessarily a thing to be ashame

of."



"I do that?"



"well, it be only after you come that I be able to tell the mater

that I don't want to do as she wish and couldn't. . . . where be

you live?"



René describe Ann's two room.



"do you like it? I mean, ben't they rather grubby and piggy?"



René think it over with a clear picture in his mind of Ann's room

and Jimmy's and Kilner's, and the woman stand at the door and

lean out of the window, and the child play in the muck. for

he it be all color emotionally. moment of distaste he could

remember, but nothing like the offend fastidiousness express in

Kurt's tone.



"well, yes. untidy and careless. one day's work slop over into the

next day. but, you know, my home be not so very unlike that. I use

to hate it at home when I get back at night to find my bed unmade.

that use to happen."



"can I come and see you? I'm here for a fortnight. my business be up

north. got a factory now. you must come and see it if ever you

are----"



"I don't think I'm likely to go north again. I feel that's finish. I

don't know why. it ben't that I have any hatred for it, or any

bitterness about what happen. only I feel on firm ground here, as

though I have take root."



"I'll come along then. any night?"



"almost any night."



"I'll take my chance."



they shake hand, Kurt with a grip that squeeze René's knuckle

together until the pain be horrible.



"'Member our smash?" ask Kurt.



René grin at the recollection. he be very pleased and comfortable.

to have establish a connection with the past through Kurt be to

have it make without shock of shame or injury to vanity. through

Kurt's frank mind it be clean and shape for he, present to he

so that he must make the necessary effort to strike out of himself the

light which should reveal it, the light of humor. it be a very faint

gleam that come out of he, but it be enough to serve and to imprint

the picture on his mind, give he possession of it, and deliver he

from the anguish which attend all his dark contemplation.



"oh, yes," he say, "and I remember how I lecture you. and now the

position be reverse."



"I don't see that."



an elegant young man in a gray suit come up, with a beautiful woman of

a loveliness and charm that take René's breath away.



"how do, Kurt?" say the young man, step in front of he. "Lady

Clewer wish to be----"



Kurt shake hand with the beautiful lady and with she and her

companion walk away toward the knot of brilliant person gather

round a biplane that have just come to earth.



flushed and tingle at the hurt, René rush away, savagely wound up

his engine, and glide back into the city, to the narrow place where

he have till now live in comfort and the pleasure of simplicity.

small and confine he see it now, mean and untidy. but it have be and

be still his refuge. he have be happy, and the world have ignore his

happiness and snatch it away from he. he be actively angry and

jealous.



he frighten Ann by the hungry affection with which he greet she

when she come home, after work overtime to keep pace with a rush of

work at her factory. she like it too. it be exciting. yet she could

not conceal her fear. she be more than his match in exuberance, but

here be a demand upon she that she could not recognize and very soon

she be in tear; not her happy tear that have so often reconcile he

and make he gleeful and proud. he be humble and acutely conscious

of separation from she, though they clung together. for a few moment

the whole weight of their relationship be throw upon their loyalty,

and it do not yield. she sleep at last, her hand in his, but he lie

awake stare back into the past, fascinate as the light grow in

he show it up in continually sharpen relief--his parting from

his father; he he could see very clearly; but his mother be in

shadow, sitting, head down, hand busy, never stir, in acceptance.

and Linda? he could see she at that absurd tea-party when his father

have show she his picture. she walk into his life then. they sit by

the tulip and she be go. he could remember his own desire and

after, only its horrible, inexplicable disappearance.









VI



KILNER





     could I find a place to be alone with Heaven

     I would speak my heart out.





the next night Ann go out alone. she insist that it must be alone,

though she give he her most happy smile to reassure he.



he sit read a copy of _extract from Browning_ which he have buy

for twopence from old Lunt. the book be against his temper, but he

find a certain pleasure in make himself read from page to page. at

nine o'clock Kilner come in. he be gaunt and haggard, and his collar

be dirty. he nod, produce a pipe, and sink, as he light it, into

the wicker chair opposite René's.



"you're comfortable in here," he say. "Snug. I suppose once you're

settle in here of a night you don't give a blast what go on in the

world outside. one don't when one have get what one want."



René lay his book down.



"have you get what you want?"



"I? no. I never---- I be go to say I never have. I don't suppose I

ever shall. that make I hate all the people who settle down in

comfort and pretend there be nothing more to want. and as that be

nearly everybody, you can imagine the hate part of I be keep pretty

busy. that again be a nuisance, because it get between I and what I

want, and make I waste energy in analyze myself, my enemy,

patron (when I have any), friend. my relation give I up as a bad

job long ago. they make all sort of sacrifice because they be lead

to believe that my talent would in the end make I more comfortable

than they have ever be. when they find that I prefer discomfort

and penury and starvation to what seem to they the simple expedient

of paint what I be expect to paint (they cann't understand

anybody want to paint anything else), then they shrink away from

I. they could make no more sacrifice. People don't sacrifice for

something they don't see, and their eye close just when mine begin to

open. we both console ourselves with hatred. I hate what they worship:

the capacity for comfort. they hate my incapacity. it be very stupid.

I would give almost anything to be able to live without hatred. it

seem barely possible, though you come as near to it as any man I ever

know. the pity of it be that you arrive at it by do and want

nothing."



"that's hardly fair," reply René. "I'm out and about all day. every

day I clean and oil the car. often I spend hour on it."



"you do nothing that could not be do by a less intelligent man than

yourself. you may do it more conscientiously, but at its good it be

not good enough for your good."



"but surely that apply to every trade and profession?"



"do it? I'm certainly not go to generalize. what's true of you be

probably true of thousand of man. I'm not interested in they as I be

in you."



"it be even more true of the work I do before," say René. "I do feel

now that I be do something. there be money earn at the end of

every day, really earn by be useful. but I don't know that I

think about it much. it have become a habit, like everything else."



"all right, say it have become a habit. say that a certain amount of

your energy be draw off in habit, what of the rest? that's what I'm

drive at. what of the rest?"



"I read, amuse myself, and Ann----"



"and you be go on forever, work out of habit, read and

amusing yourself, and a woman who----"



"I'll trouble you not to say anything against Ann."



"I'm not say anything against she. she have a perfect right to be

herself, but if be herself interfere with I, I have a perfect

right to fight for what I want."



"what do you want?"



"your friendship."



"you have it," reply René, in the tone of one squash an argument.



"yes," say Kilner, "comfortably. you try to make room for I in your

little circle of comfort, and, bad still, to use I as a comfort. I

cann't stand that. she know it. that's why she keep you away from

I."



René protest:



"she don't."



"she do. you watch her eye when she come in and find I here."



René look up at he uneasily. Kilner pounce on that:



"you be uneasy already. I don't want to make trouble between you two.

you can make quite enough for yourself, but I mean to dig out of you

what I need. I mean to try anyhow until I be satisfied that what I

need be not there."



there be a challenge in this, and René have the surprise of find

himself meet it. indeed it be brace to feel the painter's

vigorous mind search his own and throw aside all that he

dislike or condemn.



"ever since," say René, "ever since our first meeting under the

archway, I have feel that there be something in you that I desire to

understand, something that, without my understanding it, have make more

difference than any other thing in my life."



Kilner lean forward.



"now," he say, "now we know where we be. Most man pretend with I

that they keep the emotional side of their nature for woman. they

don't give it they, God know what they do with it. Most man also

confuse their emotion with their imagination. I think that be why

they spend their life in the uncomfortable search after comfort."



"and woman?" ask René.



"you and I be not concerned for the present with woman. it seem to

I that you and I be in this queer place for much the same reason,

because we be incapable of let our life run along the line

lay down for they. I don't know what you be after; perhaps you don't

know yourself, but I want to tell you what I be after. I'm not a great

reader of book. some of they may have say what I'm try to say.

. . . as long as I can remember I have have the intense joy through

my eye. I think I've say that before. it don't matter. I see

thing. at first it be just the crude pleasure of form. one thing

after another, I let the whole world unroll before my eye until I be

drunk with delight in it and nearly mad. then form begin to have a

meaning and to melt into each other. I begin to see relation between

different form. Beauty begin to sing in color. with form and color

the world be so rich that the strain upon my sight be an agony. my

greed bring I to seek consolation which unfortunately do not

console. if I accept comfort, then I lose my delight in form and

color and be not comfortable. I find that the way out of that be to

select and concentrate. I could only select in a certain passionate

mood. in an ecstasy I feel truly that I could recognize the object in

the contemplation of which I could find the great joy, a joy equal

to that of human love, and have this advantage over it that it need

not be express in physical experience. but, once feel, it must be

express. I do my good in paint, but it always seem

impossible--except when I be actually work. when I look at what I

have do, then I know that it be impossible. one can give a little

singing hint of it and no more. and then again, turn from that to

life, one be disgusted. everywhere such coarseness, such greed, such

meanness, such conceit. yet to nurse that disgust be to feel the joy

fade away, to hear the song of it die down. there be no justice then,

no kindness, and the world be so horrible that the soul take refuge

in a sorry silence. youth be then a heated torment from which there be

no escape, but in a kind of death that bring decay and poison love.

. . . there, if you can understand that, you can understand I. I

cannot surrender my vision either to comfort or to my own disgust."



they be silent for some moment. then René say:



"in here," he touch his breast, "I know that you be right. I have

be try all this time to understand you with my brain, but now

that seem only to be a sieve through which to pass what you have

say. you see, I have never try to express anything, but there have

be time in my life when I have be move enough to understand

faintly what you mean. disgust? I know that too. almost everything I

have ever do seem to I now to have be the result of disgust. I

suppose that be why I be what I be. but I'm glad you come in to-night.

I be go through another crisis of disgust; I go from one to

another."



"I know," say Kilner. "a man do when he seek to find love only in

woman."



René wince. his friend laugh at he:



"oh, you be not the only one. it begin very early. woman exploit

their motherhood as they have exploit their womanhood to get we. it

be not their fault. man have keep their joy from they and preserve

their brutishness. there be an even more bitter disgust lie in wait

for those who seek to find love only outside woman."



Ann come in on that. she stop inside the door, and glower at the

painter.



"oh, so you've come back?"



"yes," say Kilner, rise. "like a bad penny."



"don't get up. I ben't no lady. you been talk?"



"yes," say René. "shall I make some tea? have a good evening?"



"no. rotten." she have not move from the door. her eye come back to

Kilner. "you can go on talk. I'm off to my bed."



and she slip from the door into the bedroom. René meet his friend's

eye. they be grimly ironical.









VII



OLD lunt





     the glass be full, and now my glass be run:

     and now I live, and now my life be do.





OLD Lunt be a dirty old man who wear a crack bowler hat ram down

on his head, a frock-coat green with age, trouser that hang in loop

and fold about his lean shank, and boot hold together with leather

lace and bit of string. he have one room at the corner of the mew,

and he live God know how. Ann always say that he would stand on the

doorstep of a butcher's shop and sniff like a dog, and stay there

until they fling he a scrap of meat. on a Saturday night he be to be

see prowl about the shop, feel the rabbit and fowl, and then

shuffle away as though his appetite have be satisfied through his

finger. he never shave, but clip his beard close. the skin hang

so loose on his jaw that shaving would have be perilous. his eye

be gray, watery, and red-rimmed, and he have ear like red rosette.



he use to watch for René to come out, and then wait by his own door

to see if the car leave the yard. if it do not, then he would come

shambling along and stand at the gate of the yard. and if René be

work on his car he would edge near and near until he could peer

into the engine. often he would stand quite silent, and go away

without a word. occasionally he would talk and mumble.



"I remember when there warn't no railway, and my brother Philip drive

his horse from Glossop to Sheffle. they use to say there wouldn't be

no engine. but there be engine. then they say there wouldn't be no

engine on the road. but there be engine on the road. and thing grow

bad and bad for poetry."



with variation, that be his customary address.



about once a month he would sidle up to René and beg for the loan of

one shilling, and ten day or a fortnight later he would return a

penny or twopence.



"interest, interest. time bad. I must ask you to extend the loan."



sometimes he would give the copper wrap up in old ballad tell

of murder and hanging, shipwreck, battle, national event, some in

print, some in writing, all dirty. in this way René become possess

of an ode to the Albert Memorial:





     Proud monument, thou Christmas cake in stone!

     the thing thou mean never yet have grow

     in english soil, a virtue not content

     to be its own reward, a virtue bent

     on cheat life of man and man of life.

     we English have rejoicèd in the strife

     of be, till that virtue chill our blood

     and have we hypnotize and nip in bud

     our aspiration. we of Shakespeare's line

     have in our living make our life divine

     Till, as we grow accustomed to look at you,

     we worship man transform into a statue.





this poem be write on the inside of a grocer's bag, and when it be

hand to René it contain threepence. it be sign Jethro Lunt, and

date April 4, 1887.



one day Old Lunt extend his usual observation, and end by ask

morosely:



"do you--do you read my poem?"



"why, yes," answer René, "all of they."



"have you really now? no one have read my poem for thirty year. it's

only the old ballad I sell now, and they not often. the newspaper do

all the murder and hanging. till the halfpenny newspaper come in, I

could sell a murder or two in certain street. I have one about Charley

Peace:





     Charles Peace, he play the violin.

     Music excite he to sin

     like drink with other man.





maybe you never hear that?"



"no. I never hear that."



"no. I think you wouldn't have. you'd hardly be bear then. hard it

be to remember that there be some so young they might almost have

be bear into another world."



he fumble about in the tail of his coat, hum and croon to

himself, and presently he produce a litter of paper and hold they

out diffidently, and so shyly that he turn his head away as René put

out his hand for they.



"there's forty year' work there," he say. "forty year. I be

thirty-five when I begin it, thirty-five, and hopeful, and I finish

it five year ago. I want to know if you think there's any chance of

its be publish in a book. I'd like to leave a book behind I.

I've be forget. I'd like someone to be remind of I. I've be

mortally afraid of the young one till you. there's something lucky

about your face, something that shine in it. there be many face

like yours in my young day, but there be no golden statue in the

Gardens then, and this must have be meadow down to the river side."



he press his lip together and mumble. René ask he if he could

do with a shilling, but he refuse, seem so hurt that he shrivel

and go away.



René keep the manuscript and read it during his off hour on the

stand. it begin nobly on foolscap, in a bold, spiky hand, and end

pitifully on old envelope and leave tear out of penny account book

or yellowing sheet from ancient volume. thirty line be write on

the back of the title page of a copy of _the City of Dreadful Night_.

it be some time before he could find his way through the manuscript.

the sheet be not number, and they be in no sort of order.

slowly he piece the poem together, and perceive that it be an epic

in ten canto, blank verse varied with ode. it be call _Lucifer on

Earth, or the Rise and fall of British Industry_, and it be many day

before its first reader could make anything out of its confusion. the

Gods change: it be difficult to make anything in this century of the

God of 1860. clearly Jethro Lunt hate that God. in fierce rhetoric he

denounce his claim to omnipotence, but where exactly his grievance

lay, it be impossible to discover. lucifer in the poem struggle out

of Hell, and, catch the Almighty in a moment of boredom, unseat

he and send he down to the Infernal Regions for a space to see how

he would do there, and afterward, in his spleen, command he to

dwell on earth. so God arrive one day in a village in Derbyshire,

and, act upon the commercial principle always employ in his

dealing with man, get the inhabitant to apply the mental process

till then only use in the practice of religion, to their everyday

life. then the community become possessed of a horrid energy, set love

of gain above love of life, and soon the old, quiet society of squire,

farmer, and laborer be break up, mill be build in the village,

their great stack belch forth smoke over the hill so that the

heather be dirty to lie upon; the woman leave their home to work in

the mill, and child be take to help they. and wherever God

go, the same thing happen.



meanwhile Lucifer be enrage to find that he be not worship as he

have hope. the church also have go into business. in Hell he have

take some pleasure in the sin of the flesh, but these have now become

so mean, so grubby, and so stealthy that his proud spirit be revolt

by they, and he say that if man like to fritter away their substance

in such trumpery they might do so for all he care, and to occupy

himself, he begin to investigate the divine power which sustain

Heaven and Earth. then he perceive that God have usurp this power

and abuse it. he set himself to master it, and when he have do so,

wait until man's love of gain have bring they to an intolerable

strain so that they must release the spirit in themselves or perish.

then he go down upon the earth and engage God in mortal combat so

that they both perish, and man be leave alone to work out his own

salvation, for to such desperate issue have God bring they in his

mischief. upon the earth there be singer bear of sorrowful woman

leave in anguish by the evil of war and peace, not know which be

the bad. slowly their song come to the ear of man, and then in

fierce conflict they work upon God's perdition until they have make

it shine in the likeness of beauty.



that, so far as René could make out, be the outline of Old Lunt's

poem. interspersed be ode in condemnation of Queen Victoria, the

Duke of Wellington, Lord Tennyson, Gladstone, Sir Edward Burne-Jones,

Augustus Harris, Bulwer Lytton, and Thackeray; in praise of

Beaconsfield, George Meredith, Charles Darwin, Cobden, Bradlaugh,

General Booth, and Charles Stewart Parnell.



no critic of verse, René be unable to judge of the work's poetic

merit, though he have a shrewd idea that it be small. historically, it

be very valuable to he. the picture be horrible, of an England

dot with community screw up in their own vileness, of an energy

turn in upon itself, desperately strive to satisfy a demand itself

have create. the tension must have be terrific, and the most pitiful

part of the poem be its revelation of the author's gradual yielding

to it, the slow ruin of his hope, the grow repulsion from a world

in which he refuse to live except upon his own term. it be possible

to mark the exact moment of his plunge into despair, for two-third of

the way through he suddenly drop from verse (grow more and more

halting) into prose:



"art be a world of beauty where there be a logic not of this world,

but until I have see beauty here how can I hope to reach it? I must

have wing, and if my soul can find neither love nor friendship, how

can it ever be fledge for flight? hatre? that would be something. I

cannot hate mediocrity. I can only let it wither I."



and he let himself be wither, though in that agony there be

moment when the word pour melodiously from his brain.



the last sheet be terrible. it contain only a brief description of

his room, the grubby ceiling, the sack on which he lay, the peel

paper on the wall, the crack window stuff with rag.



"I lick my lip," he write in a savage scrawl. "bitter!" then he have

make a blot thus:



     [image: a black circle approximately 5 mm in diameter]



and against it he have write: "my world."



twice after René have read the manuscript do Old Lunt appear in the

yard, but he creep away as soon as there seem any danger of his

be accost. and then he do not come again.



a busy time follow, and he be forget except that, to please he,

René have order a typewritten copy of the poem to be make--that be

the near possible approach to the book of his desire. this copy

come home at last. Ann be ask to bind it, and do so neatly with

the green cloth she have for flower stalk. then, a night or two later,

it be take to Kilner, for he to decorate the cover. he have be

tell of it, try to read it, but could not. however, he design a

decoration for the cover and print the title and the author's name

in bold letter, and beneath each he place a blot. that part of the

manuscript appeal to he more than all the rest.



"that," he say, "be what the world be to all your comfortable people,

behind the charm and excitement with which they cover and disguise it.

the only difference between they and your old man be that he fight to

get some light on it and lose. I would rather be he than they. he do

take his world with he; theirs they leave behind, catch in the

mesh of their factitious moral and convention."



"but," say René, "ben't he leave his world all write out?"



"no, the tale of how he sink beneath its weight. it be true enough,

anyhow, to have stir you into a desire to give he pleasure. he have

rouse you exactly as I have be try to do these last month."



"that's true. I do keep try to get light on that little black

world, but I say to myself that after all the sun's light be quite

enough."



"it's enough for beast and tree. it ben't enough for man unless they

will consent to live like beast, at the mercy of their instinct, in

competition with the beast, and have a very nasty time of it. no. no.

the light your friend be after be the light of the imagination. let

your light so shine. he have never have it, never more than the will to

have it. probably he drink or take to some other form of vice to

console himself in his more difficult moment. you'll never know.

probably we all know that be worth know. young man often make blot

like that because life be such an infernal long time in beginning; but

for an old man--well, it look like a sober conclusion, as though he

really have face a fact, and have the sense of humor to go on live in

spite of it. there!"



he have finish the cover.



"I hope he'll like it."







René take it that same evening to Old Lunt's room. it be behind a

stable and harness room use by a grocer as a store. its one window

look out on a blank wall of yellow brick. for the rest the room be

exactly as the old man have describe it; not a stick of furniture in

it; sack throw in a corner, and on these Old Lunt be lie with his

leg cross, his hand under his head, smile up into the dim light.

the set sun strike the yellow wall outside the window, and the

upper part of the room be fill with an apricot-color glow. dust

dance in the light. the room be fill with an acrid sweetish smell.



Manuscript in hand, René step forward.



"good evening, sir," he say, "I think you----"



he stop, for he know that the old man be dead. he have know it

before he begin to speak, but the sound of his voice bring home to

he the mockery of word. raise the cold right hand, he lay _the

Rise and fall of British Industry_ beneath it.



the light die down. the glow sink into the gloom. he creep away, tell

the woman next door that Lunt be dead, and she say she would go at

once to the crowner's office.









VIII



RITA and JOE





     and it seem the very door-hinge pity

     all that be leave of a woman once,

     hold at least its tongue for the nonce.





ANN have always know Old Lunt. as far back as she could remember the

mew have be her playground, and the old man come and go have

be a part of the scene.



she seem to connect the silence that visit her mate after his

death with he, for she fill it with reminiscence and story about

he. he use to sing queer old song, and sometimes he could be

persuade to tell about the country where he come from and flower and

bird; yarn about his father's farm and the happiness he have have on

it until it come into his brother's hand, and his brother have go

into the manufacturing. then there be no home for he in the old

stone house.



for all her talk Ann could not break in upon René's silence, and his

eye would implore she to cease, yet she could not cease. she go on

and on talk, for she dread his silence as she dread his

solemnity. they make life heavy and evil for she. if a man be

unhappy, there be plenty of distraction and consolation. everybody

be unhappy at time, but no one in his sense clung to his

unhappiness the way Renny do. it be an exasperation to she to have

he like this--"mooning and dither to himself"--because he have be

so much more complacent and docile than she have expect. she have

look for trouble, but he have slip into her way, and share she

pleasure with an astonishing ease and grace, so much so that she have

have the mortification of hear two woman in the mew argue about

he:



"garn! 'E ben't no scholard."



"'struth. 'E's a college gent."



"'Im! they might come to see a work girl, but they wouldn't take up

with 'er."



the trouble she have look for should have be between herself and

he, and she be prepared to tackle it so soon as it show its head,

but this trouble he keep to himself, outside she. and though she

call it unhappiness, she know well enough that he be not unhappy.



indeed, it be a joy to he to find himself more and more alive to the

world, the little, grubby, amusing corner of it in Mitcham Mews, and

the great roar whirlpool outside in which lie his work. his

pleasure in London be no long purely emotional; no long do he,

as it be, implore London to let he be a part of it. he be work

in it, contribute to its life, to its bustle and noise; but since

his talk with Kilner and his reading of the poetical work of the old

ragamuffin, he have be able little by little to detach himself from

it and watch all that be go on. truly there be never a more

amusing city! everything be on show. everybody have the air of

expect to be look at and admire; though everybody pretend also

that he or she have no such expectation. when provincial arrive in

London they seem to feel all this and to wince before it, but soon

they perk up their head and behave as though all eye be upon

they. and they go to the show-place, those of which there have be

talk in their home from their early recollection. but everything

else also be a show to they. more and more the shop tend to become

show. government office be be pull down and rebuild to make

more show. exalted personage be bent on make a show of their

common humanity. even in the city, the office in which londoner

work--the counting-house behind the shop--be be raze to the

ground to give place to colossal palace of ferro-concrete and marble

and plate-glass. motor-car be grow more and more garish and

glossy; the advertisement on the hoarding be more and more crudely

color. for whom be the show? for whom be all the outpouring and

display of wealth? hardly, think René, for Mitcham Mews, that sink

of the submerge and those who could only just hold their head above

water. he think he could find the answer in the mile and mile of

little house like the house in Hog Lane, six room, attic, and

cellar, constantly stretch out to the west and to the east; the

uncease expansion of mediocrity, a flooring of concrete, warrant

fireproof, to keep the fantastic creation of wealth uncontaminate by

the source from which wealth sprang.



these be no general speculation. as he detach himself from the

spectacle of London, and observe and bring humor and charity to

bear on his observation, it become more and more clear to he that in

this fantastic atmosphere he could not live. he be conscious of

energy within himself. upward from Mitcham Mews lead to the mediocrity

of the little house, to those who live in the dazzlement of the

show, forget life, forget death. downward? there be no

downward without sink into the disgusting vice which repel he.

beyond the mediocrity be only the show where everything be

sterilize, think castrate, art hermaphrodite. (Kilner know too

much of that.) at the same time, he feel that his present mode of life

could not go on much long. there would certainly be a move from

Mitcham Mews, but he want it also to be a decision, not a mere

change of house.



Ann return to her idea of try a new country, and for a time he

play with the idea. it have its seduction. the long voyage: the

indolent life on board ship; the possibility as they slip away from

existence in England of shed those element in themselves which

prevent the full sympathy desire by their affection; the settling

in a country where class difference be not so acute. but, he feel

rather than see, that would mean isolation with Ann, and his feeling

be against it. when she try to discuss it with he, to get he to

consider the respective merit of Canada or Australia, he be evasive

in his reply and soon force she to drop it. she would show a little

disappointment, but would reassure herself by say:



"there's no place like old England," or: "Sally Wade's in Canada, and

she do miss dear old London."







he be so absorb in his thought and his grow certainty that he

do not notice how few of his evening he spend with she. because she

be cheerful, he imagine that she must be find her own amusement

and satisfaction. he see a great deal of Kilner, and when the painter

be otherwise engage, like to be out in the street on duty. without

know why, he have begin to desire to save money. every shilling put

by add to his sense of independence and potential freedom. he have

commence with a money-box, but find Ann one day shake coin out

of it, he open an account with the Post Office Savings Bank. he say

nothing to she at the moment and be angry with himself for let it

pass, but it be impossible to reopen the subject later. he tell

himself that Mitcham Mews be no harbor of strict moral, that its

inhabitant do more or less what they want to do, and therefore

make it enjoyable for he to live among they. (that be the reason

Kilner have give he for live among the very poor. they have the same

liberty as the very rich, with none of their pretension or false

responsibility.) he have dismiss the matter from his mind when it

be bring home to he one night on his return late from work.



Rita and her husband live opposite Martin's yard. as he come out of

it, René be confront by Ann leave their house with a basin under

her arm.



"I've be see Rita," she say. "Joe's be out of work since the

coal strike, and he's go on the drink. her time's come, and

someone's get to do for she. it be for she I take the money."



"I--I beg your pardon, Ann. why don't you say so before?"



"it be the way you look, Renny, dear. you do frighten I so."



"I'm sorry. can I do anything to help?"



"it may be to-morrow. anyway, soon. would you mind keep Joe away?

he's not your sort, I know, but he must be keep away."



"all right. he shall be keep away. be she in for a bad time?"



"I'm afraid she be. Work's be so skeery of Joe these time that it's

be all she's be able to do to feed the child."



"that's bad. but she ought to have think of herself."



"sometimes," say Ann, "there ben't room for everybody to be think

of. if you can get through a day or two it's as much as you can manage

without think what's go to happen in a month's time."



"don't you ever look ahead, Ann?"



"no. what's the good? whenever I do, it only frighten I."



"be you frightened of anything now?"



"a little."



they have reach their room and she have begin to wriggle out of her

clothe.



"I don't like your be frightened, my dear. there's nothing can hurt

we, and be hurt be no great thing."



"all in the day's work, eh? oh, well. some thing. but, don't you see,

I think I'm go to be like Rita."



"Ann!"



she look at he queerly, almost maliciously.



"what do y'expect? make I so fond of you?"



he say lamely:



"I--I haven't think of it."



she be sting into silence. presently she creep into bed and lie with

her face to the wall. in a tone of almost petulant disappointment she

say at length:



"I fancy that be why you be put by all that money. I be

pleased about that, I be."



René sit on gloomily in the outer room, listen, wait for she to

go to sleep. he be full of resentment against he know not what. she

almost cynical practicality? her acceptance without wonder of the new

fact? as with the rest of his life, so now he be able to detach

himself from she. she have be pleased with he because he have begin

to make provision, as she think, against the probable event. she have

announce the event as one regret the pleasantness of the past,

almost as one diffidently present a bill--commercialization.

horribly their relationship be strip of their individuality;

they be just a man and a woman separate by that which they have

together create. they have know kindness and fellowship, mutual

forbearance and gratitude, and now they be despoiled of these good

thing. he be leave impotent while she bow to the disagreeable fact

and be absorb in it. and he begin to see that they have long be

bear toward this separation, and to escape from the pain of it he have

turn to Kilner and the thing of the mind, while she have comfort

herself with the thing of the flesh, the suffering of the

child-ride Rita, who now seem to he typical of the life of the

mew, a creature crush by circumstance, by responsibility which

she could not face, a house which she could not clean, child whom

she could neither feed nor clothe, a husband whom she be unable to

keep from deterioration. and to think that for one moment he have see

beauty in she, when she have appear almost as a symbol of maternity,

which must be--must it not?--always and invariably beautiful and to be

worship. his idealism came crumble down as he could not away with

the knowledge that Ann have lose in beauty for he.



it be no revulsion, no withering of his feeling for she; rather it

be that the brutal fact have a burn quality to peel away the

trimming from what he feel.



he find himself grope back in his life before Ann come into it.

nothing quite the same have happen to he before. the perishing of

his young desire have leave he in a whirling excitement which contain

less torture than this obsession of cold realization. bereft now of

all that have make his life good and pleasant and amusing, he could

only appreciate Ann and the experience that lie before she,

appreciate, but not understand. that be too horrible. she have be so

dear to he; such a good, kind, true, brave little soul. the

resentment that he could not altogether escape he visit on Rita, as

Ann have from the first visit her on Kilner.



why should Kilner on the one hand, and Rita on the other, draw they

apart? why have they create nothing that could be share outside

themselves? why should that which they have create destroy that which

they have value in their life together? why--and he come firmly back

to his real obsession--why should they have so isolate themselves

that the natural consequence of their love, if love it be, should be

an intrusion, a shock great than they could bear?



he listen again. Ann's breathing seem to tell that she be asleep.

he creep in to she. she be awake. after what seem an age, she say

in a dry, weary voice:



"I keep try to think what kind of a house you live in."



he describe Hog Lane West.



"no. the other one, I mean."



"oh, that?" he tell she it be like a little house in some Gardens not

far away.



then in the same dry, weary voice she say:



"I have be try to think what she feel when you leave she."



"for God's sake," cry he, "for God's sake keep that out of it."



"I do try to, Renny, dear. but I cann't help think about she

sometimes when you're like that----"



"don't talk about it, Ann, don't talk about it. go to sleep."



"kiss I, then. I couldn't go to sleep till you'd kiss I. not

to-night. it _be_ all right, ben't it?"



"oh, yes. it's all right, bless you."



"I don't want to be a drag on you, Renny, dear. it _be_ a blessing

we're not married, ben't it?"



"that don't matter."



"that's what I say. if it's right it cann't stop, can it? if it's

wrong, it must."



he kiss she to stop she talk. she sigh contentedly, slide she

arm into his and press her face against his shoulder.



"good night. we _have_ be happy."



and in two minute she be asleep. he too be glad of the happiness

they have. he be a little infected with her fatalism. if there be to

be calamity, there have be store of frank pleasure and true

delight to draw upon in defense against they.







by kill off an imaginary grandmother, Ann procure a half-day off

from her work and spend the afternoon with Rita, who be weak and

dispirit by the great heat which fill the mew with stale air and

bring old fume and stench from the stable. there have be

thunder and storm, and the two young child be down with

colic. Joe have disappear with Click and Billy, who, to Rita's great

distress, have begin to seek her husband's company and to give he

money--at least she suppose they do, for he have nowhere else to get
