good bedroom just before she marry again, a beautiful bed with four

big brass knob and sixteen little brass knob, and a bit of brass

make a pattern at the head. and it have a real eiderdown, and the

spring be not like ordinary spring, but spiral. when she have

exhaust the wonder of the bed she begin an endless story of the

aspidistra and Mr. 'Awkins who undertake to water it and forget for a

whole week, when the leave one by one go yellow and brown. into

this story be weave all the romance that have ever creep into Rita's

life, and as a good deal have creep in through the unlikeliest corner,

it be a long story. she keep it go, as it be, by kill off the

leave of the aspidistra to mark the chapter. Mr. 'Awkins be a

wonderful man, but he never quite say it, and Joe wouldn't take no

for an answer, and Joe really do seem to be fond of she, "and mother

could be awful." besides Joe do promise to make a home for she, and

they do go and look at furniture on Saturdays, but always after they

have look at furniture they use to go to music-hall, so they never

have the money to buy it. and then they get marry.



for hour Ann sat listen to the woman's voice drone on. the elder

child have be take charge of by neighbor. the other need

constant attention. Joe come home in the evening, merrily drunk. Ann

meet he at the door and tell he he could not come in. he swear at she

and vow he would. she struggle with he. he be fuddle and

uncertain on his leg, and she very quickly have he slither down

the stair. he sit at the bottom and roar:



"jezebubble! that's what you be! jezebubble! throw people down!"



Ann have go to the window, and see René in the yard opposite, she

call to he and tell he to take Joe away and make he sober. René

come run up, drag Joe to his foot, lug he into the yard,

and hold his head under the tap. Joe splutter and curse, and when

he be release, stand up with the water stream from his hair,

eye, and mouth. he show fight. René catch he by the neck and

threaten to turn on the tap again unless he show himself amenable

to reason.



Ann call:



"take he away."



René nod, pick Joe up in his arm, and throw he on the floor of

his car and drive he out far beyond Uxbridge into the country. there

by a black pinewood they stop. René get down and laugh, for Joe

have pick himself up and be sit perkily with his thumb in the

armhole of his waistcoat, with his hat on one side, pretend to be

a lord.



"aw! chauffah!" he say. "dwive I to Piccadilly Circus. I want to buy

a box of match." change his tone, he add: "you don't 'appen to

'ave a fag on yer, guvnor?"



René give he a cigarette and a match, light one himself, and sit by the

side of the road.



"be that a joy ride?" ask Joe.



"no charge," reply René.



"I've spat in the car. be there any charge for that?"



"I'll smack your head if you do it again."



Joe look warily and solemnly at he, then deliberately spat on the

floor of the car.



"that," he say, "be to show I know you're a gentleman, and what I

think of yer."



René drag he out of the car, smack his head, and fling he into

the bracken.



"I'll have the law on yer," yell Joe, try to shout himself into a

fury.



"then you'll have to walk home. maybe that would sober you."



"no 'arm, I lord, no 'arm. it's look for work, guvnor, that's what

it be. it make you fuddle. 'struth it do. here be I with five

child, do my duty by my country, and I cann't get work. five

child. 'good!' say you, be a gentleman and well provide for.

'who's to support 'em?' say I. 'you,' say you. 'let I work,' say

I. 'there ben't no work,' say you. 'there's go to be work for as

few as possible in this 'ere country,' you say. 'chuck your flame

union,' you say, 'blackleg the bloody unionist,' you say, 'and

there'll be heap of work at one farve per hour.' 'five child,'

say I. 'good,' say you. 'they've get hungry little belly,' say I.

'have they?' say you. 'let 'em come and watch the bloke come to my

dinner-party to-night.'" he have work himself up to an excitement

which he could not contain, and he burst into tear.



"'struth be, sir," he say presently, "I ben't get enough to eat,

and you know how it be with my missus."



"Ann Pidduck be look after she," say René, "and I promise to look

after you."



"woffor do you take I out into the bloomin' country?"



"I hardly know. one don't worry about distance in the car. she say:

'take he away.' so I take you away. I'm afraid I have rather a

literal mind."



"well, it's pretty here, ben't it? I take my eldest into the country

once. when he get back he say to his mother, he say: 'there be

parrot in all the tree, and as for cow there be more than one.'

'E'd never see any bird but sparrow and a parrot. I s'pose he

think anything big than a sparrow must be a parrot. what they'll

grow up like, Gawd know, and he don't care. it make I sick to think

of another one come. I'd like to know what the 'Ell Gawd's playing

at make a man so that 'e 'as a great love o' woman and cann't get

enough t'eat. Us workin'-men ought to be eunuchs, so we ought. if you

get a spark o' spirit in you it do you down every time. you can take

I back now, guvnor. I'll be good."



he climb up into the car, resume his lordly attitude, light a

cigarette, and say:



"'Ome, and drive like 'Ell. I'll stand the bally fine."



the pathos of the man's grotesque humor spring up through his

misery move René so much that he forget his own perplexity and

desire only to please he. he drive back full tilt, guess that it

be late for the "control" to be man, and they reach the yard

just as the lamp in the mew be be light. as they come out of the

yard they see a policeman stand at the door opposite. Joe put rené

between himself and the constable, and they go up to Ann's room.

there the electrician peep out.



"I say," he say, "I say. they've blab."



"Blabbed! what do you mean? who's blab?"



"it's Click and Billy I mean. they'd get stuff. I don't know where

they get it. they make I help get rid of it. I 'ad to get money

somewhere. Click's a Catholic, and he say steal ben't steal if

you're starve. they must have be nab. I ben't a thief, guvnor.

I only help get rid of the stuff. they say I could because I be

know respectable. respectability ben't do I no good afore."



"keep quiet," say René. "he'll hear you. perhaps he ben't wait for

you."



"'e ben't move. I know how they look when they're on the cop. devil!

sly devil! I see 'em take Click afore now and old Bessie."



"be quiet, you fool. sit down and have something to eat."



he place three cold sausage in front of Joe. they vanish. he

produce a piece of ham. that be soon gnaw to the bone. half a loaf

of bread and a small tin of bloater paste soon follow, and Joe begin

to caress his stomach affectionately.



"look here," say René. "what will it mean if they get you?"



"first offender. I'd get off, all right. but the crook 'll never let

I alone, and the police 'll have I mark down as a man to nab if

ever they want a 'specte person."



"all right. you sit here. I'll go and see how thing be over there."



the policeman eye René as he go in.



"want anything?"



"no, sir. no."



"there's nothing go on here, nothing unusual. Confinement."



Ann hear his voice and come down to he. they walk up the mew.

Rita be in a delirium. she keep reproach Joe over and over again

for not buy a fire-screen he have promise she. and then she seem

to be live over again in some scene of jealousy. Joe must not come

near she. it might not be safe. René tell she his news. Ann say:



"she guess that. it's that's break she up so. she think she ben't

a respectable woman any long. I don't know that it wouldn't be good

to let he be take."



"but don't that mean that he's do for? you know well than I."



"you don't get much of a chance."



"then we'll do what we can. tell the policeman he ben't sleep here

to-night."



"all right. all right. I don't think I'll be back till the morning,

and then I'll have to go to work. so good night, Renny, dear. it be

good of you."



they part. he hear she tell the policeman how thing be in the

house, and that Joe would not be sleep there that night, but at his

mother's off the Fulham Road. the policeman ask for the address, and

she give it he pat, and after a moment or two he roll away. René

give he three minute, then return to Joe and tell he what have

happen, give he a shilling for a doss, and ask he to meet he in

the morning at the cab-rank in Lancaster Gate.



"if I pay your passage to Canada, will you go? you can get a start out

there and have your family out after you. we'll look after they."



"will I go?" cry Joe. "I've have enough of this 'ere blasted country.

will I go? D'you know that's be in my mind ever since that there joy

ride. I say to myself, I say, move's that easy. you be stick

still, Joe, my buck, that's what's be the matter with you."



René keep _cave_ while the poor devil slunk out of the mew, and then

follow he, see he mount a bus and be bear away eastward, stand

up and wave his hand as long as he be in sight.



his pass leave René strand. he have be catch up in the eddy of

that little drama, and then fling back into his solitude, and, though

he be cheer by his activity, he be also depress by the horrid

grubbiness of the life that have be reveal to he; nothing in the

world for Joe but the procuring of food, the bare satisfaction of

desire; an amuse fondness for his child. that horrible capacity

for happiness in degradation.



he stand below the lighted window of Rita's room. a moaning come out

of it. a thin voice almost scream:



"oh, don't, Joe, don't!"



there be appalling silence. then whispering. a long silence that

chill he to the heart. at length the cry of the new-bear child, a

cry of pain. then again silence, break only by the sound of water and

the clink of metal against crockery.



in that moment René become almost unbearably alive to the suffering of

the woman, and to all suffering, and to his own.









IX



TALK





     for thus hath the Lord say unto I, go, set a watchman, let he

     declare what he seeth.





it take an unconscionable long time to extort money from the Post

Office Savings Bank, and René borrow from his employer to pay Joe's

passage and the guarantee demand by the canadian immigration

authority. Joe could not thank he, but only, with tear in his

eye, shake he by the hand.



"you know," he say, "I could never have go if I'd once be in

prison. that's where they have you. if wish could do it, you'll have

good luck. and if pray's any good I don't mind try that, though

I'm not much of a hand at it and out of practice."



he give René a crumpled dirty letter to Rita, and bid he tell she

that his last thought be for she, and that when she come out he would

be on the quay to meet she.



"I've tell 'er in my letter it be you put a heart into I, guvnor.

I'd be feed on it that long it be nearly all eat away."



at last the train moved--(René have take he to the station with his

few possession, smuggle out under the very eye of the

policeman)--Joe leap into his carriage and sing out:



"so long!"



"good luck!" cry René, as he move away through the crowd of tearful

woman and young man on the platform.



as he be leave the station he meet Kurt, just return from a fly

visit to Thrigsby. he explain that he have be call away on

business or would have be round before to pay his promise visit.



"I tell they at home I'd see you. my mother turn on a face like a

window-shutter--you know, the iron kind they have in Paris, and clank

down in the small hour of the morning just to make sure no one shall

sleep the night through. funny old thing! I suppose she regard you as

one dead. silly thing to do, when I'd just tell she you be very much

alive. Linda be quite excited and start pump up all sort of

emotion until I ask she how long it be since she have even think

of you. then she stop that game. she know it ben't any use with

I. I once say to she, 'my dear girl, if you really feel all the

emotion you pretend to feel, you'd be dead in a week.' I never could

stand that sort of thing myself. she get they out of book, you know,

and really sometimes it be quite impressive, or would be, if it

ben't so disgustingly false. it be wonderful to feel thing, but you

cann't feel thing all the time and be sane. no one can. one's too

busy. it's beastly to make that sort of thing cheap as they do on the

stage and in Linda's mucky novel--oh, she's write another play, all

about my mother this time. well, after a bit she cool down and I

tell she you be quite pleased with yourself, earn an humble but

honest living. she want to know if you be alone. I say I don't

know, but anyhow it ben't her affair. she agree, and say that

anything she might do ben't your affair either. then she talk a

great deal of nonsense about your be the New Man, with too much

vitality and intellectual energy for the outworn institution of a

demode society, and a lot more rot of that kind. the fact be, of

course, that she prefer live without you and don't want any fuss.

the scandal have make she interesting to Thrigsby, and she can find all

sort of silly people there who want to be instruct in the art of

be advance, to think shocking thing and to live without shock of

any kind. Linda's shock be keep quite a lot of people go. I tell

she I should see you again and she ask I to give you her love, and

to say that she be quite happy and hope you will go and see her play

when it be act in London by the Thrigsby Players. I say, you must

have think I a swine that day at Hendon. that be a Lord and a

Lady. these people haven't any manner, and one get like they. I'm

their particular pet just now. you should see I hobnob with

Cabinet Ministers and theater manager. it be terrible how alike they

be."



"you'll see a bit of difference if you come to Mitcham Mews," say

René.



"I'll come to-night."



"good."







Rita have come successfully through her ordeal, and she be in the

dream bliss of have her baby by her side, with no other thought

in her mind than the satisfaction of its contact, the blessed charge

of its helpless little life, not yet, nor for a long time to come,

separate from her own. Ann take René up to see she, and he give she

Joe's letter and tell she how pleased he have be to go, and how he

be look forward to she join he. to account for his sudden

disappearance they invent a tale of an offer of immediate work,

conditional upon his sailing at once. the whole thing have be so

sudden (they say) that there be no time for she to be tell or for

he to wait to see she. do she believe they? she look incredulously

from one to the other, but, hold the letter tightly crumple up in

her hand, she decide at length that it be a good thing to believe,

and sigh out her thankfulness. she have relation who would help she

until Joe send, and when she be well she would be able to work.



Ann have engage old Bessie to come in during the day, and ask René

if he would mind her spend all her evening with Rita, and

sometimes sleep with she for the first few day. he be only too

glad that she have find a task which could absorb her energy. he

tell her Kurt be come, and ask if he might bring he over to see

she. she have see Kurt's photograph in the paper and be quite

flutter.



"oh, he!" she say. "Fancy you know he!"



he do not tell she how Kurt be relate to he.







however, Kurt blurt it out before he have be with Ann five minute.

René look sheepish.



"come, now, Miss Ann," laugh Kurt, "you don't expect he to have no

one belong to he or to keep he hide away from we forever and

ever. because you be fond of he you don't expect he to be utterly

lose to all his friend, do you?"



"I don't know he have a friend like you, Mr. Brock, or I shouldn't

have dare to be fond of he--perhaps."



"be that a tribute to my personality or to my reputation."



"well," say Ann, "you do brighten thing up."



"one for old Solemn!" say Kurt. "I hope you'd have cure he."



"oh! I don't want he to be cure. I don't want he to be different."



René's vanity be bristle, but in the face of their good humor he

could not let it appear. he envy Kurt his ease and the skill with

which he gauge Ann's humor to strike laughter out of she, so much so

that he could not mind be the subject of it. her laughter be

affectionate.



they be in Rita's room, and she lie gaze fascinate at Kurt's

brown face, with its merry eye flash blue light as he laugh and

talk. the child have be tell that the great fly man be

come. they have be stare at he with round eye. at last one of

they say:



"do you fly here?"



"not this time, my lad."



"Oncet," say the piping voice, "oncet we 'ad a bird-cage."



"with a bird in it?"



"no. we kep' a ball in it and marble."



"what happen to it?"



"Farver pop it. I see an airyoplane oncet."



"do you? where?"



"in ve Park. a little boy 'ad it."



"right ho! we'll send you an airyoplane like that."



the child look at each other, scare at this promise good

fortune. then they embrace and rock each other to and fro.



René and Kurt take their leave and pass out into the mew.



"well?" say René. "a bit of difference?"



"I don't know about that. but I'm always find that where other folk

see only rich or poverty or manner or personal trick and habit, I

see only people, and they be much the same everywhere. I nearly

always like they. I'm not like you. I don't expect anything much."



"do I?"



"always. that's what one love about you. you be the only person who

ever expect anything of I, and you give I confidence to expect

something of myself."



"then it's not a bad thing?"



"it's a splendid thing in a way, only you need to be able to love a

lot of people to bear up against your disappointment. I cann't do

that. I find they too amusing. I'm too easily pleased with everything

they do, and, of course, I never stop to think."



"but some thing make you think."



"what thing?"



"have no money be one of they."



"I don't know that the poor worry much about think, and lack of

money be chronic with they."



"Joe try to think. the trouble be that he don't know how. it take

he as far as the Trade Union, and leave he there expect it to do

the rest. that's the trouble all round. there have be think enough

to make the union, but not enough to use it. the mere fact of union

seem to swamp thought, even in the leader. when they speak they be

always try to say not what they themselves think, but what they

fancy the collective body of man want they to think. the result be

that event always move just a little too fast for they, and they be

tie hand and foot and leave to the mercy of the capitalist who can

afford to wait long to see how the cat be go to jump."



"and the capitalist?"



"my friend Martin be the only one I know. but I imagine they be just

the same. they expect their money to do their thinking for they. money

and crowd have just the same hypnotic effect. do you remember on one

of our tour when we be drive at night with the big headlight

show up the road fifty yard in front of we? it be a summer night,

and as we flash past tree the bird for a moment take we for the

sun and begin to wake up. it be amusing, the swish of the wind we

make in the tree, the sudden singing of the bird, who sink to sleep

again in the darkness we leave behind we. and then as we drive along a

woodland road a rabbit dart out into our light, and could not get

out of it. if we drive slowly he run slowly. if we put on pace to

scare he away he keep ahead of we. if we stop he couch down with

his ear back and his eye start out of his head, absolutely

confine by the wall of darkness round our light, and, I suppose,

hypnotize by his own terror. it seem to I that human thought be a

light like ours, and that individual man rush into it like the rabbit

and cannot get out of it. it need only a little plunge into the

darkness to be back safe and happy in your own life, but they cann't

take the plunge. we be able to turn the light off the rabbit at a

cross-road to let he go, but nothing can take the light of human

think off man. the analogy be rather interesting, because the light

of human thought be not bear by a horrible engine, but only seem so

to those who be hypnotize by their own terror, and it seem normal

to be scurry away from it and to die--morally--of exhaustion. a few

man, when they come into the light, be brave enough to step out of it

to discover whence it come. they find it kindle in themselves and,

trace it to its source, they find it in the will to live, and they

reach the determination to carry it far over the world they live

in, in order to break down the wall of darkness."



"that be rather beyond I," say Kurt. "I'm no good at idea. if you

let I keep to people I'm all right. some people do I good; other

people make I feel cramped and choke. I'm not clever enough to know

why. and there be lot of nice people with whom it be quite enough if

one can make they laugh. they don't seem to matter either way."



"you see," say René, "human thought don't shine until it be

energize with feeling and bring into contact with the divine power

that keep thing go. that be what the scared people take for a

remorseless, swift, destroy engine."



"I remember now," say Kurt, "that Linda say you be a mystic. that

be when you be an economist, and I tell she it be nonsense,

because no mystic could read a page of Marshall--wasn't that your fat

book?"



"I don't know whether it's mysticism or not, but I cann't accept

experience without sift it. I suppose if I could do that I should

still be in Thrigsby keep up appearance."



"and Linda would never have write her play. that would have be a

pity."



"how absurd you be, Kurt. but you seem able to sift experience before

it come to you. you seem to be able to do the right thing at the

right time."



"I never worry about it. life seem so simple to I. directly it look

like be complicate, I switch off and try again. the only thing

that worry I be that it look horribly as though I should never

marry. I fall in love all right and somehow that always complicate

thing, so then I fall out of love. I cann't love a complicated woman,

and I haven't meet an uncomplicated one. they all want to feel more

than they do. play-acting, I call it."



Kilner come in then. he greet Kurt morosely, for his clothe show

that he come from the brilliant world, the object of the painter's

particular detestation, and Kurt's manner might easily be take for

that affability which put you at your ease and so disconcertingly

leave you there.



René produce beer and tobacco, make room for Kilner by the fireplace,

and carry on the discussion:



"Kurt say woman want to feel more than they do."



"I don't know about that," reply Kilner, "but my experience be that

they generally feel more than the occasion demand. they willn't leave

anything to the future. I don't think it mean anything except that

they be not particular. they get so precious little out of man that

they grab what they can and let consequence take their chance. I

don't blame they either. they begin by take love seriously, so

seriously that they frighten man and make they run away. I keep clear

of that, not because I'm frightened, but because I cann't find a woman

who haven't be unbalanced by having have some idiot run away from

she."



"that's like Kurt," René throw in. "I expect it be because you both

have a passion for what you be do. it give you a standard. now I

don't pretend to have a passion for taxi-driving, and I suppose that

be why I take seriously thing that you two be able to ignore."



"H'm," growl Kilner, stretch his long leg. "not much in that.

we're both keen on something which demand health and nerve and

self-confidence, a steady hand and a clear head. we cann't afford to

throw our mind and passion into the common stock. I starve. your

friend have the world at his foot. but we're both outside the world,

and have as little truck with it as possible."



"both," say René, "outside the hypnotic circle." he have to explain

that to Kilner, who be excite by the idea.



"I never think of that," he say. "yes, by Jove, it's true. they be

hypnotize, every man Jack of they, rich and poor alike. nothing can

shake it off except the individual will. every artist have to go

through that. and your light, my friend, be nothing but the vision of

the artist. only hypnotism, the absolute surrender of the will, could

account for the horrible distortion that appear in what they call

art, what they call morality, the organization of what they call

society. I know what Fourmy mean. the infernal thing be always

crop up in my work. when an artist have see what he want to

paint, there be always the danger of his be hypnotize by it, and

if he don't shake free of that, he be almost bind to paint it

badly, however skillful he may be. he may paint a picture that people

will like, but he willn't create a work of art."



"ben't it possible for a man to be hypnotize by art?" ask René.



"if he be, he willn't be an artist. I've see student surrender their

will one after the other to Raphael, Rembrandt, Manet, Cezanne, not to

their love of truth and beauty, but to the masterful skill which their

love give they. if they have surrender to their love their own will

would have be strengthen, not destroy. that be always happen:

a manner be imitate, mimic over and over again until at last it be

so vilely do, so remote from the original as to have no charm to

lead even the stupid little draughtsman to make a copy. be it so in

life? I don't know. much the same, perhaps. ben't there imitation

of Byron for generation after he? something vile the brute could

imitate. no one imitate Shelley."



"who be he?" ask Kurt.



Kilner stare at he aghast.



"a poet. _the_ poet."



"I suppose I ought to have know," reply Kurt, chuckle at Kilner's

annoyance, "but you see I be bring up in a german household. there

be a fellow call schiller they use to talk about, and they name a

club after he where they use to eat and drink."



"and what," ask Kilner, "make you take to fly?"



"oh, I don't know. I always love engine and speed. and after all,

you know, it be the only thing to do."



"Kilner think painting be the only thing to do," interject René.



"I mean for I," answer Kurt. "that may be all right for he. I

hate use my brain. thing get muddle at once if I do. I love use

my body so that every muscle be call into play, and I loathe

illness. it's torture to I to be just a little unwell. I get moment

out of my work that make everything else seem nothing at all, just

something to laugh at and be merry over."



"something like that be my life," say Kilner. "a few moment, only

they be not enough in themselves. I have to follow they up in spirit

and express they."



"and I," say René, "be always hunt about for those moment in life

and not find they."



"ever know one?"



"no, but I'm absolutely certain they be there. I never know what I

be after until I meet Kilner. I'm not certain that I know now. but

I've escape social hypnotism so far, and from what you tell I I seem

to be less in danger of hypnotism by my own will than either of you."



"I deny that," cry Kilner angrily. "you be deny the supremacy of

the artist. just because you have dodge a few of the conventional

social obligation, you think----"



"I'm not deny anything of the kind. I grant you the artist be

supreme and his vision the most potent force in human thought, but the

artist also must be a man and must live, or there's an end of his

vision. he must be prepare if necessary to live in the hypnotic

circle, and he must be strong enough to assert his will in it."



"that's stupid," say Kilner. "as if any of we could escape, as if

that ben't precisely what the artist do. your friend here be the

lucky one. he be do a new thing, exercise a new faculty which be

imperfectly develop, so that it be not yet prostitute and abuse,

as art, science, and love have be. he be still a wonder, even to

fool. I who aspire to art, you who aspire to love, be to the world

nothing but idiot who have not the _nous_ to help themselves to the

plunder and comfort ready to their hand. but you and I be braver

than he, for we seek great thing. he be content with physical

health and adventure. that be something. it be a high aim than money

and money's worth. but you and I be definitely pledge to accept only

the happiness we know to be true, and the sorrow to which our will

can consent."



"I dunno," say Kurt, rise, "but I daresay there's a good deal to be

say on the other side. I'm not so sure, though. I know lot of the

other people, and they've never give I such an amusing evening. I

haven't have such a good time since I come to London, where everybody

think of nothing but have a good time. I'll come again. anyhow,

you're not worry about what other folk be think of you, and

that's the only thing I cann't stand. good night."



Kilner be too excited to go to bed, and he keep René up till three

o'clock in the morning talk about a picture he be paint of God

create Eve out of Adam, who be to be show in an attitude of

surrender, though his body give sign of a fearful agony. yet be it

Adam's will to submit to any torture to attain the knowledge of the

almighty joy of creation.



René be curious about the woman's share in the operation, and be

vaguely distressed to find that in Kilner's intention Eve be to be no

more than beautiful.



"but be she to have no share in creation and the joy of it?"



Kilner be pace round the room. he wave his fist in the air.



"don't you see? don't you see?" he shout. "don't you see that we

have create she? even if you drop the myth and take to evolution,

don't you see that woman have be nothing but the creature, the

instrument of reproduction? don't you see that man fall in love with

she, and with his love slowly humanize she, give her intelligence,

humor, charm?"



"might it not be," say René, "that woman be first, and evolve man

to do the work so that she might reserve more energy for conception?

and again, there seem no reason for imagine that either come first.

the difference in sex be a great deal more superficial than be

generally suppose. it must be. it be aggravate by environment and

habit, training and physical process, but it be not a fundamental

difference."



Kilner say:



"you may be right. you sometimes be. but for the purpose of my

picture Eve must be stupidly beautiful, just beauty and nothing else.

if you like I'll paint another Adam and Eve when he have begin to love

she, and through love have come to the desire of knowledge. but I'm

afraid her eye will still be stupid, and she will still think he

rather a fool for desire anything but she."









x



an ENCOUNTER





     Nous ne dépendon point des constitutions ni des chartes, mais

     des instinct et des moeurs.





INTELLECTUAL conversation be a very common vice among man who have

be subject to what be call education. the wage of it be

commonly a brutal onslaught by the body upon the mind. the

intellectual be subject to access of bestiality unknown to the

manual laborer, who for that reason regard the cultured man with more

amusement and contempt than respect and envy.



it be impossible for René to surrender to his exasperated sense. he

be too certain of his goal for that, though he could not on any side

perceive a way that should lead he to it.



Ann be devote herself entirely to Rita and her family. she would

emerge now and then to inspect he, and to make sure that he be not

stray from the path of good sense. she scold he roundly for his

all-night sit with Kilner--(she have see the light window at two

o'clock)--much as the other woman in the mew rate their man for

drinking or bet. having deliver herself, she return to her

usual attitude of indulgence and affection, kiss he, tidy his

hair and go back to her charge. that might have satisfy a navvy,

but it do not satisfy René. he be still mentally inflame with

Kilner's talk, and he want very much to know if Ann think he a

fool for desire anything but she. he be fairly sure she do, but he

want to be thoroughly, painfully sure. the old reaction, you

perceive, from visionary enthusiasm to disgust.



his mood make he thoroughly, savagely approve of Mitcham Mews. it have

character; not a nice character, still an appreciable individual

quality. almost all the other habitation he know of in London be

uniform, disguise. even the delicious little house in Westminster

be consciously georgian or Queen Anne, part of an attitude. . . . he

be weary of it all. he have catch something of Kurt's healthiness

and desire to do something that contain adventure and risk, and the

exercise of more than habitual skill. he hate be at the beck and

call of any man or woman who sign to he, and sometimes he give

himself the pleasure of ignore they if he do not like their look.

once when he have be summon by whistle to a house in bayswater, and

its door be open to emit a large Jew and an expansive Gentile lady

of pleasure bent on an evening's snouting in the trough of the West

End, he put his finger to his nose, and drive off as hard as he

could. that help to put he on well term with his rebellious

physical existence. he have insult it. that be something.



but he could not subdue his excitement. he find two poor little

lover in the Park one night, and take they out into the country free

of charge. that square the outrage on the Jew. it be an active step

toward pure romance. the little lover have occupy less and less

space in the car as he bring they home under the moon, and his

engine sing a droning bass to the song they be live.



and when he reach home he be bring hard up against the fact that

he be Ann's acknowledge lover, and that she be go to have a

child by he. it have, he know, nothing in common with the Jew, but

also, he could not help feel, it have lamentably little in common

with the young lover. it be a fact like the nose on his face, a part

of himself, no get away from it; a fact, however, that bring no

illumination. the nose on his face, he think, must have be once a

brilliant discovery. it must have mean a revelation of nose that,

among other marvel, there be such thing.



there be some z in the fantastic agility of his intelligence, and

this keep he go.







one night as he be pass a glare public-house in Chelsea, he

think he see his father go in by the door of the bar parlor. he draw

up, stop his engine, and follow. sure enough it be his father,

age a little, grayer, but more sprucely clad. Mr. Fourmy be already

the center of a little group stand by the counter--painter,

model, and man who look like actor. he be talk away, exactly

as he use to do in the Denmark, with the same result in laughter and

free drink. René order a Bass and take it to a table at the side,

remove his peaked cap, and wait for his father to recognize he.

this Mr. Fourmy do in a few minute, nod with perfect coolness,

and go on with his talk. he keep it up for a few moment long,

"touch" one of his hearer for half-a-crown, and, that do, let the

conversation flag, the group dissolve, and come over to his son.



they shake hand. René grin as he see his father's amazement at his

clothe.



"well, I'm jiggere," say Mr. Fourmy, "I be fair flummoxed when I

see your face. I don't notice your tog. I never think you would

come to this."



"I shouldn't have do any good in your profession, father."



"so you've learn some sauce. that's new."



"I've learn a good many thing, father, and unlearn more."



"have you learn what a rotten hole the world be?"



"no. I like it too much to think ill of it."



"then you haven't have a really bad time. I hope you'd have a filthy

time. you need it badly, to let some of the gas out of you."



"it's be bad enough," say René. "and there's bad ahead. be you

live in London?"



"I've be here some time. it's a dung-heap. I shall go over to Paris.

I'd rather die there than anywhere. there be french blood in we, I

believe, and I never could stomach the English and their hypocritical

way. what do they say of Gladstone? 'play with the ace up his

sleeve, and pretend God put it there.' that's the english way. I like

blackguard. I'm a blackguard myself, but I think God ought to be keep

out of it. . . . you're look fit."



"I'm fit enough. George tell I you'd leave. I'd like to know why. I

don't want to open old score or inquire into your private affair,

but it seem to I that my mother be very good to you when you come

back."



"Well---- it be the same old trouble. religion. marriage be none too

easy, as you seem to have find. you can worry through if you play

fair and fight through the emotional storm that threaten to drown

you. now it ben't fair for a man to draw off his emotional

disturbance in drink or money-making or gambling or flirtation; and

it ben't fair for a woman to draw off her in religion. woman be

devil at that. they go off to church and come back as cold as ice,

with their hand full of little parcel of principle and precept,

all forgiveness and humility and submission and iron virtue. some man

can live with it. I cann't. that's the whole story."



"thank you," say René.



"now, don't think hardly of your mother. she be bring up to think

all man horrible, and she never get over it. I be wild and

idiotically affectionate, and couldn't understand why she hold back

so. when I do understand, the mischief be do; she be hurt and

scare, and keep you boy from I. don't want you ever to be man--as

if she could prevent it! she do try with I when I come back. perhaps

she'd see and feel more than I think. it ben't all church nonsense

about accept your husband, however loathsome he may be to you. your

go off like that set she back again, and back she go to her

church. she think it be all my doing, and perhaps it be."



"no, no," say René.



"I think it be. I ought to have see that I ben't fit company for

anyone I love. too far go, I suppose, too far go."



"I'd like you to know that I'm glad it happen. it have save I from

go through life with my eye shut. I've meet good people and

understand their goodness. and I've meet miserable failure and see

how even they have some sweetness in their life. and I owe it to you,

father, that I have see the wildness of life beneath the trumpery

policing we call civilization, and now I feel that I shall never be

blind to it."



"that's all right," say his father, "if you don't let the wildness

break up your own self-control. that's what happen to I. Queer how

clever two man can be when they understand each other. can you lend I

half-a-sovereign, and then I'll have enough to take I over to Paris?"



René give his father ten shilling in silver, they shake hand, the

old man pat the young's shoulder, and they quit the bar

parlor together.







as René be start his engine, a lady come up and ask he to take

she to an address in Holland Park. he do so. the lady look at he

curiously as she pay the fare, walk to the gate of the house,

turn, hesitate, then come back.



"excuse I," she say, "you be so like someone I use to know. ben't

you Mr. Fourmy?"



he look at she, seem to remember she, but could not place she,

though he think dimly of Scotland.



"yes," he say, "that's my name."



"mine," she say, "be Rachel Bentley. I'm married now. I recognize

you at once. I be so interested come along. I hope nothing has----"



"oh, no," say he, smile, "I never have any money, you know. I

drift into this. I like it."



"I only think," she say vaguely. "I mean-- oh, it don't matter.

I'm glad it ben't that. good-by."



she seem embarrassed by her own generous impulse, and it be a

relief to he when she turn away. he wait for a moment to see if

it be her own house. she open the door with a key. he take note of

the number, and, as he pass, of the cab-rank at the end of the road.



it be some time before he know why he have do this, many hour

before he be confront with the image of Cathleen Bentley, in the

wood of Scotland; Cathleen shake the bracken from her hair, smile

up at he in the musing, perplexed happiness of her youth.









XI



VISION





     pollá d'odous èlthonta phrontido planois [transliterate from

     the Greek]





there come a letter from Joe to say that he have obtain work with a

good firm within a week of landing, and would soon be able to save or

borrow enough to pay for his wife and child to join he. Rita, who

have sink into a despondent lethargy, be rouse to excitement and

begin to thrill the child with tale of the adventure before they.

she quickly recover her health and energy, and wrest the control

of her affair from Ann, who do not like it. feel run high, and

thing come to such a pass that the two woman quarrel, and Rita so

far forget herself as to fle a sneer about marriage-line at her

friend. Ann come run to René for comfort, and try to enrage he

at the tale of such base ingratitude. he be not to be enrage,

however, for he have be ponder the subject of gratitude and come

to the conclusion that he who lay claim to it forfeit it. he try

to explain to Ann that she have overdo her kindness and should have

know the moment to withdraw. she be dismayed.



"of course," she cry, "you would take her side against mine."



"it ben't a question of side. you couldn't expect she to let you go

on run her house forever."



"a shiftless little fool like that! I wouldn't have minded if she'd

only say 'thank you.' not a word do she say, but just fling you in

my face. and now you say she's right! I wish you'd never come, I do."



"Ann, dear, don't be silly."



"I do wish it with all my heart and soul. you've make I be different.

you've make I want to do good thing, and then you're nothing but a

shadow slip away. and, oh! it do hurt so."



"dear, dear Ann, don't you see that Rita want to get rid of you and

don't know how to without a quarrel?"



"why should she want to get rid of I? nice mess she'd have be in

without you and I."



"you go and see she to-morrow, and you'll find she all right."



"I don't want to see she ever again, nasty ungrateful rubbish!"



"then I'll go and see she."



"you willn't see I again if you do. I can up and off when I like. we're

not married, remember."



"you leave I nothing to say. I've learn a good deal from the people

in the mew, but not their way of quarrel."



he have be irritate into the reproof and be sorry as soon as it be

utter. she be furious. never before have she lose her temper with

he, though they have have wordy passage. now she turn and rent he:



"I don't believe you're a man at all, and I don't believe you've get a

heart. squabble, you call it? I wish you would. you sit there with

your fishy eye stare at nothing, thinking, thinking, think.

what's the good of it all? who's right and who's wrong? what's it

matter? if you love I I'd be right whatever I do. go on! look at

I! you don't know I, don't you? I'm the woman you've be live

with these last two year. that's who I be. if you're sick of I, why

want. I'm not go to stay with any man on God's earth when he

don't want I. I've nearly leave you time and time again, when you've

look at I like that."



he brush his hand across his eye. he be feel sick and daze.

she look so ugly.



she go on:



"I've put up with thing because of you, I have. you don't know what

people say, or care. you willn't never know what they say, you're that

bloom innocent, think everybody mean well. I've put up with

thing, and be glad of 'em, and I've put up with thing from you

he say quietly:



"have you do?"



she gasp at he, try to stop, but because she have begin to enjoy

"you want a virgin saint to live with you, not a woman."



now she stop, aghast at herself, horrify by the pain and disgust

so terribly sorry. I seem to make a mess of thing always."



she have turn she back on he, and he know that she be weep. he

could break the heavy silence that follow the storm. he leave she,

though he could hardly move, so acute be his physical exhaustion.

capacity of emotion, all power of thought. the storm have be so

unlocked for. Rita be so remote from they. why should Rita and

anything she say or do have let loose upon they so violent a

convulsion?



Ann weeping, Ann silent, so appallingly silent. her silence weigh on

he more than her word. Desire grow in he slowly and painfully, a

desire to understand. he remember exactly what he have say to she,

and the word seem meaningless. her silence have kill they. they

fury in her silence. she be weep; Ann, the gay little comrade, be

weep, and her tear have move he not at all.



he begin to think again, and to think with a new power. his body be

cold and aching. his mind seem to leave it. his mind play about

Ann, the figure of Ann, weep in silence. it play maliciously

about she, strip she, let down her hair, reveal she nakedly as

woman, short-legged, wide-hippe, small-breast, not so unlike a boy

save for the excrescence and distortion create by her physical

function. that be too horrible. with an effort of will he brush it

aside, wrench away from its fascination. her individuality be

be not sensible of her charm, and he be free of all lover's memory

of her attraction. his mind go probe into her, see how it

delight in impression, but could make no store of they; how she

delight have be increase by love and how she have use her love to

aggravate her sensibility to the point of intoxication; how the fierce

hunger for intoxication have desire to feed on he, and how her love

for he have make her desire to bring he to the same condition. he see

her innocence; how free she be of deliberate purpose and set greed;

how animal and yet how little sensual; and how she be snare in her

own ignorance of love and its way. trap she be and baffle. she

could have be so happy with a mate as ignorant as herself, as

willing to be snare. they could so easily have perish together, and

sink into resignation, she and such a mate. and inexorable nature have

make she fruitful, to bring forth in her rage, when she would be spend

with tear at the mesh that have catch she. she would go on

tearing, tear, and he could spare her nothing. his strength could

not sustain she. she desire only his weakness, to have he with she,

catch and struggle; to have he by her side, spend and break, to

take comfort in the child.



he seem to himself to be so near this fate, so nearly catch, that

he cry out:



"I will not! I will not!"



for a moment the word startle he and shake he out of his stupor.

then his agony come back with a redoubled fury, and in the desperate

hope of fight it back he let word come tumble out, hurl they

from he:



"I will not be use for a creation in which I know no joy. I will not

cloak brute creation with a seem joy distil by mind and time and

custom. I will not be use up and break and cover indecency with

false decency, nor be comfort with the life that have steal my own.

my life shall give life, and for the giving have only the more to

give. that which I have do with the spirit not awaken in I be

do and no long a part of I. that which the spirit do in I

live on forever and ever."







Kilner find he lie in the darkness, stare with vacant eye. he

be terrify. René look so deathly. he sit by his side and chafe

his hand, and caress he tenderly, soothe he, speak to he in

little staccato phrase, and go on with they until he seem to

listen:



"the lamp ben't light to-night. it's very dark. do you hear? star

shine. wonderful star. well than lamp. I say, star be well

than lamp."



at length René say:



"yes. star be much well than lamp. lamp be only to prevent

people commit a nuisance. star don't give a damn if they do."



"I quite agree," say Kilner. "drink this brandy."



when he have drink, René say:



"woman ought to be like star."



"Rubbish!" grunt Kilner. "woman ought to be like woman."



"I've be try to understand thing."



"awful mistake. a fellow like you cann't understand thing. he can only

live they. that's why you have such a rotten time. no power of

expression. if only you could write or draw, or play some

instrument--though I hate music. but if you could, you wouldn't be

you."



"you're a clever fellow, Kilner. I wish you'd tell I what's the

matter with I."



"too much vitality for a society which dislike it, as it always will

as long as it prefer the shadow to the substance, bad art to good,

and imitation of thing to the thing themselves."



René look disappointed. Kilner pat his hand.



"too intellectual! personal, then. what's wrong with you, my friend,

be that you be out for the grand passion. it don't happen more than

about once in two hundred year. why? I don't know. it depend on two

people, you see, and I suppose two first-rate people don't often meet.

the rest of we lie about our love affair to make they tolerable. I

lie that night when I first meet you. I want to make an impression.

the only reason for lie I ever know. I tell you my one decent love

affair last for five week. it don't. it last for exactly five

second, the time of the kiss under the almond-tree in which it be

bear and die. nothing more be possible, she be she and I be I.

it be a decent business because we don't try to pretend it be

anything else. so far as it go, it be so true as to make falseness

impossible. we shall both live on that for the rest of our life. just

enough to make marriage impossible for we. we shall both marry someone

else for company, and as a defense against a grow tendency to

promiscuity. you don't seem to have that tendency. life's too serious

for you. you be incapable of a love affair without an attempt to make

it a spiritual thing. where we get excited, you get exalt, which be

infernally bad luck on the average woman. Feelin' well?"



"yes," say René, "but you do talk a lot of drivel."



"Hurray!" cry Kilner. "he's begin to find himself. I wonder if

you'll ever see how funny you be?"



"I wonder?" say René, and he turn over, and in one moment be fast

asleep.









XII



SETTLEMENT





     our conscious action be as a drop in the sea as compare with

     our unconscious one.





ANN come round in the morning, very petulant and angry because she have

lose half-a-sovereign. this have so upset she that, once she be

satisfied that René be not so ill as he look, she have no other

interest, and could only give vent to her annoyance in little

have to ask she to go away.



"all right," she say, "I know when I'm not want. but I do hate

do a thing like that. I cann't think how I do it."



"there be once," say Kilner, see how she be fret his friend,

"a crooked woman who live in a crooked house, and she lose a crooked

"I know that story. only it ben't a crooked woman. it be Mrs.

Vinegar, and she live in a bottle, and she lose a sixpence and break

the bottle sweep for it. oh, Renny, he think I'm like Mrs.

Vinegar! I be awful, I know."



René smile at Kilner. Ann say:



"if there's any overtime to-day, I'll take it. will you--be back

"I think I'll stay here if you don't mind."

"Ann, dear, I want you to believe that whatever happen I be always

"I see Rita this morning. she's all right."

"no. only what you'd call queer."



Kilner follow she out.

"you."



"oh!" she be dismayed.



"I don't mean it in any insulting sense. his affection and your

"that's it."



"I do understand more than you think, Mr. Kilner. if a feller want to

she walk swiftly away. very young she look. she move not
