not for want of money or a help hand. if your heart's set on he, I

cann't do nothing there; but, Lor' bless you, heart ben't everything."



"good for you, Mr. Martin," say Kilner.



"oh, I know a thing or two." the fat man wink. "you don't have to do

with 'orse for nothing. I have a 'orse once take a uncommon fancy to a

goat there be in the mew. have to see it every day. the goat be

sell, and that there 'orse pine away. I keep on a-telling of he that

no goat in the world be worth lose a feed of oats for, and at last

he get so precious hungry he believe it, and I never do see a 'orse

so glad to eat. fancy come and go, but your belly let you know it's

there till you die. will you come in, too, Mr. Kilner?"



"no, thank. I must get to bed early. work in the morning."



when Kilner have go, old Martin say to Ann with an affectionate

touch on her arm:



"that young man have a 'ead screw on his shoulder."



"he's all head," say Ann, "and I hate he."



"Lor'! there's talk. how woman do like to make a man wriggle. I

never be much in the wriggling line myself, not be the build for

it. but a 'ead's worth have, too. I never have much 'ead myself. too

affectionate myself. what a pretty little thing you be, to be sure.

feel it bad, my pretty?"



"hellish bad," reply Ann.



"there, there."



"I never think I'd feel anything so bad. I want to hate he, but I

cann't. I do hate that Kilner. I'd like to see he dead."



"there, there. 'orse have wunnerful strong dislike, too."



Ann say:



"it's enough to make a woman scream, the way man talk."



Old Martin's huge face expand in astonishment. he reach out his

hand for a pipe, fill it, convey it to his mouth, and sink into a

brooding silence. he break it at length to say:



"woman have a great scorn o' man, and I don't know but what they

deserve it."



"if there's one thing I hate," say Ann, "it's be dish. I suppose

I always know it couldn't last. it be too wonderful. you don't know

how kind he be in his way, never want anything you don't want

yourself. and that be awful, too, because it make you afraid to want

anything. it seem to shame you. he be always shame I, and I do

feel awful sometimes. but it be lovely when we go for ride on top

of bus."



this appreciation of René's quality as a housemate seem to bore

old Martin, for he take up a newspaper and begin make note and

calculation from the bet column.



"Hullo!" he say. "this must be some connection of his. 'Miss Janet

Fourmy of Elgin, N.B.' 'Miss Fourmy,' it say, 'be a distinguished

german and italian scholar, a Goethe translator, a contributor to the

scottish Encyclo--' what you may call it. 'in her youth she be

familiar with the famous Edinburgh circle which gather round _maga_

and do much valuable philological work, and be for a time governess

to the late Archbishop of Canterbury who never cease to express his

admiration for her intellect and gift. she have many friendship with

the interesting figure of her day, and it be believe that she have

leave some record of they.'"



"he tell I about she," say Ann. "he use to go and stay with she,

and she use to read an italian book call Dante, with the page

upside down. she be very old, but good to he, and she think Lord

John Russell be in love with she."



"Lord who?"



"I don't know who he be, but that's the name. Renny say it be her

weakness. she live all alone, and it's very dreary in the winter in

Scotland. she have meet a lot of lord in her time, and she like to

remember more than she'd meet. and she'd never marry, and Renny say

she think it sound well to account for it by say that Lord John

Russell be in love with she. it ben't always him----"



"well! the thing woman do think of. I shall say I remain a widower

because of Madame Tussaud."



"she be fond of Renny," say Ann, and that seem on her lip the

noble possible epitaph for old Janet. she add:



"perhaps that's where he's go."



"I shouldn't think so. it cost a pile o' money to go to Elgin, N.B.

it's a good deal north o' Bedford, which be the farth I ever go

with the 'orse. that be in eighteen-eighty-four."



he settle down for a story. fortunately for Ann, he be allow to

get no far than clear his throat, when he be cut short by the

entry of Casey.



"evening, miss," say he. "I see your young man in the neighborhood

of Holland Park, stand on a street corner. I nod to he, but he

look clean through I. very queer, I think. we've be good pal.

when I come back an hour later he be still there. I be empty that

time. so I stop. 'keep the pavement warm,' I say, cheerful

like. 'try to warm myself,' say he. 'draughty weather to be do

that in the street,' I say. 'you go home, Casey,' he say. 'oh,

well,' I think, 'we're all fool, and every fool to his own folly.'

so I leave he. I come home that way just now and he'd go."



"we been talk about he all evening," say Martin, "I and Annie

here."



"he's one of the good hand at an engine that ever I see. and that

bring I to what I want to talk to you about, guvnor. I be to see

the doctor again, and he say London's do I a bit of no good, and

if I go on with it, it'll do I in. now I've get an idea. leastway it

ben't all my idea but mostly hisn, young Fourmy's."



"if you know about 'orse, there's a good livery at Barnet."



Casey persist:



"my idea be this: there's just a few want motor in London.

something's happen in the place. well, one night in the cab-rank

young Fourmy, Young Earnest, as we call he, take out the map of fifty

mile round, and he point out how the railway go out of London like

spoke of a wheel. between the spoke, he say, be where London be

go to live if it be make possible, and motor ought to make it

possible. he say if you choose your place properly, so as to link up

the main road and two railway, you'd be bind to make a living.

there's enough house already. soon there'll be factory and work

out there. then there'll be more house. I don't believe it at first.

I say: 'but if all the people live out there, what's to become of

dear old London?' 'London,' he say, 'will be a clearing-house and

capital, a real center.' I don't understand altogether what he be

talk about, but I've be out to see for myself, and what he say

be happen. all the little country town have cinema and new shop,

and in the suburb there be whole street of house empty. I'm no

good for the West End traffic, and I want to try my luck at the other,

if I can get hold of any capital."



"ah! Capital!" say Martin. "that want a bit of get, capital

do."



clearly he have not understand a word of what Casey be talk about.

he have his own idea of London, and be not go to change it or admit

the possibility of change. from one year's end to the other he never

leave the mew. his yard might actually be fill with motor-car, but

for he it be really a sanctuary of the 'orse. their smell still

clung about it. the one horse he have leave have little else to do but

provide the smell.



however, he like Casey, and be distressed to find he take to

idea:



"don't you go worry your head about what be and be not, Casey.

head ben't make for that. head be make to have eye in, and

mouth, the same as 'orse. all you get to do, all any man's get to

do, be to earn his keep and pay his shot, same as a 'orse. when he's

do that, 'e's get to behave nice to they as be in stable with he.

and every now and then he get his little canter and may be turn out

to grass."



"I'm no Nebuchadnezzar," retort Casey, "and I want to be on my own."



"no man can be on his own if he ben't get no capital."



"that's what I've be say."



"ah!" say Martin mysteriously, to baffle Casey's obstinacy. "ah! that

want get, that do. if it be 'orse now----"



Casey see that it be hopeless. nothing would budge the fat man from

his yard. car! they be a necessary evil, not to be encourage

beyond the limit of necessity.



Ann want to know more about René, but Casey could tell she nothing.

he repeat his eulogy of young Fourmy's skill as a driver, and add:



"we've get have-be gentleman on the rank, score of they. but

they're not like he. it's a treat to hear he talk, it be. they

want he, a lot of they do, to pitch into the union, but he don't

seem to think much of trade-union. he say they cann't do anything

yet, in the way of fight I mean, because they want to make we all

middle-class, and that ben't good enough. if I could get he to go

along with I!"



Ann say:



"he haven't be home all day. don't he say anything to you?"



"he do say one day: 'I'm get sick of this, cart man and woman

like cattle.' it seem to have get on his nerve a bit. too good for

it, I suppose."



"it would be a good thing," say she, "if we go into the country,

though I don't know what I'd do. I do love London and all the light

and that, and the shop."



say casey:



"you should see the night in Africa. some part you can walk a

hundred mile and never see a light. nothing but star, and few of

they than we have here. flat and empty as the sea some of the country,

go on forever and ever in the darkness."



Ann shiver:



"Ugh!" she say. "it make I think of Renny. I don't know why. he'd

like it, I think."



"yes. I think he like big thing."



it be late. near twelve o'clock. the lamp in the mew flicker as

Ann return to her room. the post have bring a note from René,

post in the north of London. he say: "please tell old Martin I

shall be away three day. I will come back then. I think I have it all

settle in my mind. I want to get it clear for you, too. you have be

so good to I, my dear, and I owe you so much.--R."



there be also a letter for he. she struggle against the desire to

open it, and conquer it for that night. the next morning, however,

the temptation be too strong for she, and she steam it open. it be

from a writer to the Signet in Edinburgh to say that the late Miss

Janet Fourmy have leave René the residue of her estate, which, after

certain small legacy have be pay, would amount to nearly four

thousand pound. the house in Scotland would also be his, and all the

deceased lady's personal effect.



Ann go to her work that day shiver with excitement. René's

enormous wealth frighten she. she could put up a fight against his

intelligence, his brooding, his silence; but against this she feel

powerless, and know within her heart that her battle be already lose.



she be a forewoman now, and she give the girl under her the bad

day they ever remember.









BOOK three



CATHLEEN BENTLEY









     so between they love do shine

       that the truth see his right

       flaming in the phoenix' sight,

     either be the other's mine.



     property be thus appal

       that the self be not the same;

       single nature's double name

     neither two nor one be call.









I



meeting



     he trieth the sea after many shipwreck; and beat still on that

     door which he never saw open.



when Ann and Kilner leave René, he be fill with anger against they,

first of all, fleetingly, with the petulance of a sick man at be

leave alone without his having express a wish for it, and then at

their treat he as a sick man when he be nothing of the kind, but

only pass through a crisis in which not even sympathy could help

he much. Kilner be so cocksure just because he have a peculiar

delight in put paint on canvas; and Ann--poor dear little

Ann!--she love to have thing and people at her mercy and to keep

they there. and she could make no attempt to understand they, because

if she do so, that would be to believe in they and let they be free

to work out their own destiny. he know how little freedom she would

even grant himself, and his mind, spur by revolt into high

activity, go straight to its mark, the place where freedom most

clearly promise--absurdly, the door through which he have see Rachel

Bentley pass. that lead to his clear and most beautiful memory, the

day in Scotland, the happy boyhood when delight have grow from year

to year, to flower at last in the coming of Cathleen. very vivid be

his recollection of their first meeting in his aunt's house: himself

very coltish and shy, she charmingly self-conscious and alert. it be

the first year the Bentleys have take the big house, and she have come

round by the road. his aunt have ask he to show Cathleen the short

cut through the wood. she chatter until his shyness overcome he,

and then they walk in a miserable silence. he comfort himself by

regard she as a little girl, which to his young prudishness make

his involuntary adoration of her beauty legitimate. he could never

take his eye off she, and she begin to amuse herself with he and try

her coquetry upon his oversensitiveness. he suffer terribly. she

be catch in her own wile, and she too suffer. it be a relief to

both when, the first year, they part.



the next year she be not so lovely, and have lose or disguise she

wildness. it be not long before he discover that he could rouse it

in she. then begin their meeting in the wood.



at the thought of she now his affection for Ann, his warm regard for

Kilner fade away. they be meaningless without she. he know not

where she be. his only clue be Rachel. Cathleen, too, might go to

that house. he would wait until she come. if the bad come to the

bad, he would ask Rachel. he must satisfy himself that he be not

cover that sweet past with illusion. the meeting with Rachel have

bring it all flood back to bring he to acute discontent with the

present. it be one thing to sigh sentimentally over happy day. to do

that be to obscure they. it be quite another thing to have happy

day demand egress through his life, grow through the thick-set

year like a tree through a wall.



he steal away directly Kilner and Ann be out of sight, find he have

only a sovereign, and turn into the tobacconist's round the corner

for change. it be also a news-agent's, and he buy a newspaper and,

as he be bear along by the bus, read of his aunt's death. strange,

he think, that all his thought should be cluster round her house

just then. the wise old woman, with her dear foible: what have she

long life be? the end of it be sweet and true and full of grace.

not only his mother have be help in her trouble. that he know. the

old lady's meager income become supple and elastic under the touch of

generous charity that never spoil its gift with the demand for

gratitude. she once say to rené: "well be ungrateful than cramp

with gratitude." Read Dante upside down she might in her old age, but

she could quote he from her heart:





     Ed io a lui: Io mi son un che, quando

     Amor mi spira, noto, ed a quel modo

     che ditta dentro, vo significando.





she have make René learn a little italian and get that by heart. it

begin now to have a meaning for he, and he repeat it to himself as

he come near the road in which stand Rachel's house.



he take up his stand at the corner and wait. he have be there

nearly an hour when a car drive up and a spruce, middle-aged gentleman

get out, walk up the path, and admit himself with a key. Rachel's

husband? far too old for she.



another hour's waiting. a young woman come along the road. René

think for a moment it be she, and his heart leap. she do not see

he. she turn in at the gate, knock at the door, and be admit.

no, he decide, that be not Cathleen.



then he tell himself he be a fool, that only by the unlikeliest

chance would she be there to-day. he walk away, but be back again

in ten minute. in another twenty the door open and the young woman

come out. she stand for a moment at the gate. it could not be

Cathleen, she be too tall and slender. in his eager hope and

curiosity he move toward she. he be not a yard away from she when

she turn and their eye meet. neither stir. they be still by

the wonder of it. a spell be on they, and slowly in both grow the

dreadful knowledge that a word or a gesture would break it. in his

heart René pray: "oh! let it break into happiness," and his will

leap into be and decide that it must be so and he laugh. she

say:



"oh! rené!"



it be no echo of the old cry, but the same fill with a new music.



their hand meet in the conventional salute. she say:



"I have be think of you so much."



"much?" say he. "I have be think of nothing else. and I be not

sure that it be you when you go in just now."



"I see you, but I don't recognize you. Rachel tell I she have meet

you."



"do she tell you where?"



"I have to dig it out of she. she be very hushed and secret. Rachel be

funny. I've be look at taxi-driver ever since. they be a very

plain lot of man."



"where do you live now?"



"in Bloomsbury. I be work for my living, you know."



"I'm glad of that, but I shouldn't have think it necessary."



"my father die."



"I hear that."



"he leave nearly all his money to another woman: another family. I

suppose he like they well than we. I have a row with my mother over

it. it appear she know all about it and never minded. only when it

come to she have less money than she think, she develop a horrid

conscience and denounce my father to we. I haven't think about such

thing, but I be fond of my father, and it ben't fair to vilify he

after his death. I don't understand it in the very least, but I stand

up for he, and of course I say a lot of stupid, cruel thing. I go

to see the other woman. she be quite old, old than mother, rather

vulgar, but jolly and warm-hearted and kind, and, from the way she

talk, I could see she really do love my father and be very proud

of he. you know, he make his own way. his father be a barber in

Rickham, in Hertfordshire. she come from there, too. I tell mother I

have see she, and she be furious, and say I be too young to know

anything about such thing. I point out that she have tell I, and

she declare she never imagine that I would understand. then she put

it all down to my taste for low company, mean you. that annoy I,

and I tell she you be a very learned and brilliant person. she say

Thrigsby ben't a real university, and its degree do not count. you

ben't a gentleman, and it be terrible how all the profession be

be invade by little whipper-snapper with a thin coating of book

knowledge. so I ask her point-blank why she marry my father, and

she say he be extremely successful. Father have leave we each two

hundred pound. I ask for that, and say I would earn my own living.

I should have a year in which to look round. she say no one would

ever marry I if I work. I tell she that the little I have learn of

her life don't make I anxious to be marry. she become very solemn

on that, and tell I I couldn't possibly remain unmarried, because I

be too pretty. I say I think woman could look after themselves,

and obviously other arrangement be possible, and sometimes more

profitable. that be an odious thing to say, but we have irritate each

other out of all decency, and for vulgarity the other woman be an

angel to we. I couldn't stay with my mother; I have say too much. she

know if I stay it would make it hard for she to play the devoted

widow; and also, if she could be the break-hearted parent, it would

give she a good start. she pounce on that, and let I go with she

most lugubrious blessing and most ghoulish doubt. she prophesy

almost gleefully that I would go to the bad, and help I along by

treat I as if I have already do so. then I plunge into the

wicked world. it be very disappointing. I have be lead to suppose

that no woman be safe alone. the wicked world have absolutely

disregard I. occasionally some miserable little man or pale-face

boy have sidle up to I in the street and say, 'excuse I, miss'--or

'haven't we meet before?' they don't alarm I. I say I willn't excuse

they or that I haven't meet they, and they look very comically cast

down, and say 'Beg pardon' and shuffle off. sometimes I be so sorry

for they that I feel inclined to run after they and tell they to cheer

up, because it's quite easy to find affection if you only set about it

the right way. they think it's adventure they want, but it ben't. it's

only affection, some sort of human contact. I understand that, because

I too be lonely. but those poor little man be so dull. I cann't bear

be dull, and I hate to see it in other; I hate to see they

settle down to it. that's what mother want I to do. I might have

do it, too, if father haven't die. you know it seem quite pleasant

to flirt and spend money, and find a husband and go on flirt and

spend money. I'd never see anyone die before, and it do make I

feel ashamed. all of we be change by it for a little. we become

very shy of each other, and want to be nice, and begin to talk about

the thing we really think and feel inside ourselves. then all that

slip away, and we be just the same as before until we talk

about father's money, and then we be all angry. I suppose I haven't

quite recover from the strain of his death, because all that hurt

I, and I could only think that I have really love he, and might have

love he much more if thing have be somehow different. and then

when I see that kind, common woman it open up another kind of life

go on apart from money and position and amusement, all the thing

we be so proud of. it horrify I at first, of course. it be

dreadful because it be secret. in itself-- well, anyhow, the only

other thing in my life that be the least bit like it and could stand

against it be my absurd little affair with you in Scotland. so you

see, I have begin to think of you even before Rachel meet you."



"Absurd!" René wince at the word.



"ben't it? I couldn't have go on with it, you know. it make I feel

so helpless, and I feel so mean, let you care so much. your

letter use to frighten I."



"but you care for I?"



"yes, yes; with one eye on you and the other on my mother."



René think that over uneasily. he be disconcert by this cool

young woman. the enchantment of their meeting have rouse and

invigorate he, and, as usual, he have surrender to the emotional

flux of the encounter and be prepare for wonder, which, as usual,

do not come, or, at least, be not palpable. his eye never leave she

face. it be light with a smile of happiness, an incommunicable joy.



Unconscious of their surrounding they have reach Kensington Gardens,

and stand by the railing outside the Palace look over the Round

Pond. a gray October day: the tree gaunt and shabby; the heavy cloud

tumble and rag. a cold northw wind be blow. René's ungloved

hand be blue.



he grip Cathleen's arm, and she turn her happy eye on he.



"that's good," she say. "you be so strong then."



"Cathleen, I mustn't lose sight of you again. you make I forget

everything that have be, though that ben't quite what I want to

say."



"I shalln't lose sight of you, my dear. it don't matter what happen

to either of we."



René say:



"a good deal have happen to I."



"tell I."



he tell she. she receive his story in silence. at last she say:



"if you have a friend, it don't matter what he do. all the same,

it's a nuisance."



"what be?"



"the nuisance be that I'm a woman and you're a man. can friendship get

over that?"



"love," say René, "can master everything. I love you. shall we start

with that? that's clear, anyhow."



"clear? oh, yes; but it mean be very certain about it and

definite. some of the charm of love go. it be go already from I."



"I'm sorry."



"don't. I'm try not to pity you. oh, René, my foolish dear, I only

want to love you and help you."



"it be you who be strong," he say.



she move close to he, so that she could just touch he.



"we shall need all the strength we can get if we be not to be

break--strength and patience."



"I have a friend," say he, "who think that all the confusion come

from sloth and fear."



"I should like to meet that friend."









II



happiness





     human lack of power in moderate and check the emotion I

     call servitude. for a man who be submissive to his emotion be

     not in power over himself, but in the hand of fortune to such an

     extent that he be often constrain, although he may see what be

     well for he, to follow what be worse.--SPINOZA'S _Ethics_.





CATHLEEN live in Bloomsbury with a friend of her, a Miss Cleethorpe,

who manage a hostel for young woman, clerk, schoolmistress, shop

girl. René take she there after their long conversation in Kensington

Gardens, and then, feel the impossibility of go back to Mitcham

Mews, go up to Kentish Town to see his friend the sandy-haire

railway porter. he have visit he once before, about a year ago, and

could think of no one else with whom he might take refuge. the little

man be delighted to see he:



"it's the sleeper!" he cry. "Lord! I've often wonder if you'd go

off again, and when you tell I you be in the taxi-driving, I say

to myself: 'well, that'll keep he awake.'"



yes. he would be glad to let he have a bed. want to sleep, eh? he

often feel like that himself: day after day, day after day, working,

and the suburban traffic grow so fast that they couldn't put on

enough train, and the station morning and evening be like Bedlam.



"London," he say, "be not what it be when I first come to it. I use

to know all the regular gentleman. but now--well, I tell you, they

don't have a nod for anyone. a bee-line for the city in the morning,

and a bee-line for home in the evening. it make you feel small, it

do."



René sympathize with he. his day also have be devote to

impersonal service, and he have know the humiliation of it.



now his only desire be to see Cathleen again. to taste once more the

vigor and keen energy with which her presence fill he. the thought

of she be not enough. it rouse a flood of emotion too strong for his

unpracticed control. he warm to the idea of her beauty. when he be

with she her beauty be axiomatic, food for rejoice without

disturbance, a mere accident, one to be thankful for, yet no more than

a light bidding to the thrilling pursuit of her elusiveness.



he have arrange to see she the next day in the evening. she work as

secretary in an Art School and be not free until after five. he spend

the day in happy brood over the come delight of see she, and

prepare with boyish dandyism for it. he have his hair cut and his

chin shave (he have grow a mustache), and he buy a clean shirt and

collar. in a book shop he see the anthology from which they have read

together and could not resist go in and buy it. he be ashamed

of himself when he have do that, and hide it away among the railway

porter's rather strange collection of book--More's _Utopia_, _the

Master Christian_, _Marcus Aurelius_, some book of Edward

Carpenter's, _Foxe's Book of Martyrs_, and _Arsène Lupin_.



Cathleen receive he in her little bed-sit-room at the top of the

big grim house, which smell of food, ink, and washing. she have make

her den very pretty, and he recognize a picture he have give she long

ago, and one or two trinket that her mother have have in her boudoir in

Scotland. the wall be of plain brown paper, and there be

gay-colored stuff by the window and on the sofa.



she take in his spruceness at a glance, be pleased by it, and

laugh.



"I must give you a buttonhole," she say, "as I use to do. you look

so wonderfully the same."



René tremble as she come to he and pin a flower in his coat.



"sit down," she say. "I think we can talk well here."



René sit awkwardly on the sofa, she by the fire, which she stir

with the poker.



"well," she say, "I feel rather a beast. I couldn't help flirt

with you a little yesterday. that's get to stop."



"be you--flirt?"



"I be."



"I think you be glad to see I--as glad as I be to see you."



"I be glad. I'd be have a foolishly miserable time. live in

this house be rather terrible with nothing but woman, unmarried woman.

you don't know. they come here young, many of they from the country.

then they go out to work in the day and come in in the evening. they

haven't enough money to pay for amusement. they're too respectable to

look for fun in the street. they hardly dare have a man-friend, the

other be so jealous, so rigid, so uncomprehending."



René say:



"I have a feeling that my presence here be an offense."



Cathleen laugh:



"that's why I ask you. I think it would do they good to see you.

it do I so much good. I think I be getting infect by it. Lotta,

my friend, escape into the country now and then. she have a cottage. I

go too sometimes, but her consolation be not mine. she have a garden

and make jam and fruit-wine. I want something more than that. I

don't want to console myself until I have to. if I be go to do

that I might just as well have stay with my mother. on the other

hand, I don't want to flirt with you, my friend. it wouldn't be fair

to you."



"what do you want, then?"



"I want to be able to assume that we love each other. we can be frank

then. it sound uncomfortably intellectual, I know, but that will be

less disastrous than be uncomfortably emotional. you use to think

about these thing. you make I think. you haven't stop?"



"no. no. but I have such a longing for simplicity. I don't know why

there be all this fuss make about love."



"because people will exploit the first excitement of it. Blake say:





     he who catch a joy as it fly

     life in eternity's sunrise."





"I don't know about that," say René. "all I know be that I don't want

to let you go."



"but you may have to. we have a wonderful thing yesterday. we may not

be able to rise to it again."



"I don't care about that. I want _you_."



"only because we have that moment yesterday."



"I don't know why it be."



"but I know and I care, and I want to keep the memory of it. I don't

mind it's be darken by circumstance, if it must be, but I do

mind it's be spoil by our own weakness. man be always gird at

woman for care about nothing but love. they may gird fairly when we

be untrue to love and let man belittle it with their impatience and

arrogance. I ought not to say that to you, because you have try, and

I have do nothing but argue with myself."



"I think you have find something which I have not even begin to see."



"and argue about it."



"I don't see what else you could do."



Cathleen thrust silently at the fire and say savagely:



"oh! don't you? I think I be go to be so free with my two

hundred pound. free, to do what? walk in suffrage procession, break

window, insult policeman. I be free to do what I like, but I like

nothing very much. I be too fastidious and could not take what come.

thing do come. they lack this or that necessary for my

satisfaction. when my money be go I have to creep into shelter away

from the freedom I do not know how to use, and ask for work to keep

myself alive, just like the girl and woman in this house, who keep

themselves alive for nothing, so far as I can see, except the pleasure

of be tired and bored and malicious. I be in a bad way, René, when

I meet you. I use to go to Rachel, who be the only one of the family

who will have anything to do with I, and sometimes I envy she in

her stupid, unhappy comfort. she don't get on with her husband, but

she have a nice house and two child who alternately infuriate and

amuse she. that be impossible for I. I'd hate it, just live with a

man to keep a household together. but then even now I've hate the

alternative I have arrive at, this being huddle away with a lot of

useless woman. work woman! a genteel occupation to support a

genteel existence. the selfishness of it! People like to pretend that

motherhood solve everything for a woman. it may give occupation to a

dependent woman, but why should it destroy her selfishness any more

than another physical fact? if she insist on it too much, it cannot

do anything but accentuate her selfishness. woman can be just as

greedy about motherhood as about eat or drinking or love, and they

can just as easily spoil it with overindulgence. don't look so

unhappy, René. I'm not argue with you. I've have to think so much,

and for month I haven't have a soul to talk to like this. even Lotta

have her world so shaped and trim (she's efficient, you see) that all

my doubt and wondering be just an annoyance to she, though no one

could be kind. I don't know what I should have do without she. it

be such a comfort to find a woman work really well, without

insist that hers be the only way of living, and do good without

want to be thankful for it. she make I patient. when you have

decide what you do not wish to do, you be apt to think anything

different must be well. you're not sorry you make the ordinary

career impossible for yourself?"



"sorry?" say René, puzzle. "it be never a thing to be sorry about

or glad about. it just happen and I feel well. and now I have meet

you and everything be change again. I don't go to my home last

night."



"no?"



"I go to an old friend of mine who live happily and contentedly. I

want to see happiness and contentment. somehow you have make I sure

of myself, and I feel that everything be change. but the change be

in myself. in nearly everybody I have be more conscious of the

thing they lack than of the thing they have. I have be bolster

myself up with contempt--for myself as well as everything else. it be

that or be sorry for myself. always a struggle. I cann't see it

clearly yet: like right without weapon and without a cause. I have

no desire to live irregularly and uncomfortably or to come in conflict

with accepted opinion as to conduct. but I don't see why opinion

should be antagonistic to a man's private affair. I ben't

antagonistic. I be only do confusedly what I feel very clearly and

have always feel to be right. I feel certain now that I ought to have

do so long before. I'd like to explain that to all sort of people,

except that honestly I cann't take much interest in it. I have a vague

sickening feeling that the end of the world have come, but that be

only because I could not see an inch before I. the end of the world

do not come, neither for I nor for--she. it seem stupid to be

explain all this to you. I know you will not think I be excuse

myself, because I be sure you accept I as I am----"



"theoretically," say Cathleen, look up at he with a quick smile.

"you see, I have live on theory, not my own, either; Lotta's. and I

don't know whether my theory can hold out against your practice, any

more than my sentimental girlish fiction could. you upset they, you

know, and you be just as disconcerting as ever. shall you go on with

your work?"



"I cann't think of anything else I should like so well."



"and that girl?"



"that's what we have both be think about all the time."



"yes."



Cathleen rise and walk over to the window and look out. she stand

then for so long that René follow she and lay his hand on her

shoulder. the window give on to a row of back garden with a few

tree, black and bare. Opposite be a lighted window through which

could be see four girl sewing--stitch, stitch, stitch.



"I have often watch they," say Cathleen, "and wonder what might

be in their life. Desire? religion? love? what be it make it

possible for they to work so mechanically and so happily."



"Fun," say René. "they want fun, spice with the risk of have to

pay for it."



"be she like that?"



"she be. but there be something more."



"there would be," say Cathleen. "she couldn't love you without be

move out of herself and the habit of her class. that be why I be

sorry for she. be you go back to she?"



"not yet."



"I think you ought to write to she."



"I be wait until I have see you again, and make quite sure----



"and you be sure now?"



"I feel now that we shall always be together, gaze out on the

world."



"and find it so wonderful."



they be silent then, and in each for other be the same song of life

and love, a music pass thought and understanding. so they remain

for a time that be no time, hardly conscious of their body whose

slight contact give they strength for flight. easily they range back

in spirit to their youth, and catch up its sweetness and melody.







they be break in upon by Miss Cleethorpe, a pale, gray-haired lady

whose eye smile kindly amusement at their helplessness. bring

help to the helpless and force they to help themselves be the whole

practice of her life. lover, dog, indigent young woman, be the

material in which she work.



she be present to René, and give he a grip of the hand that

startle he with its vigor. turn to Cathleen, she say:



"the girl have send up a deputation to I to say you have have a man

in your room for the last two hour, that it be against the rule, and

that it be not quite proper. ten minute they could have overlook. I

say that Mr. Fourmy be a very old friend, and that I know all about

it, but they insist that I must come and chaperone you, and here I

be. speak well for my authority, don't it?"



René be so distressed at the thought of the young woman contemn

Cathleen that he be almost speechless. he mutter that he must go.



"you mustn't go," say Lotta, "before I have thank you for what you

have do for Cathleen. she come home last night look perfectly

radiant--and look at she now." (she have turn up the light.)



Cathleen be stand with her hand lightly clasp in front of she,

her head throw back, her lip part, and in her eye a golden

tenderness. she smile and shake her head slowly, and come to her

friend and kiss she. Lotta put her arm round she and hug she.



"you two poor silly," she say, "what a heavy burden you have

shoulder."



René grin:



"I don't feel the weight of it," he say.



Lotta gaze full at he. he meet her eye, search he.



"be you go back to your stable?" she ask.



"I want two more day of this."



"would you like to take it down to the country? there's a west wind

blow over my hill, and winter be come in."



like child, René and Cathleen gaze at each other in surprised

delight.









III



the WEST WIND





     day, that in spite

     of darkness, by the light

     of a clear mind be day all night





NORTHWEST of London there be hill, where the air be eager and the

upper wind be catch in wood as they come cloud-bearing from the

wild sky. often the wind fle cloud about the hill and leave they

entangle in the wood. such a cloud they have leave on the Saturday

morning when Lotta Cleethorpe bring René and Cathleen to her

retreat, an old white cottage on the border of a long common brown

with dead heather, orange with wet wither bracken, olive-green with

the gorse and the close-crop grass under the gray mist. out of

this, as they drive from the station, loomed tree and haystack and

house. a public-house and a church stand at the end of the common.

soon they pass a blacksmith's shop with the bellow in full blast,

the spark fly and the smith's huge arm and swart face light up by

the red glow. there come out the merry clink of hammer on the anvil,

and then the hiss of the red-hot metal plunge into water.



René say:



"the beginning of it all."



"of what?" ask Lotta.



"modern life." and he find himself think of Kurt, who have just

add to his laurel the first prize in a race to Berlin.



they reach Lotta's cottage. Apple-tree stand by the gate, a clip

box-tree by the door. a sheepdog came bound along the road, clear

the gate, and paw frantically at Lotta until she crouch and he

could lay his foreleg on her shoulder and lick her face in a

frenzied greeting.



"he live at the public-house when I be not here, but he refuse to

regard it as anything but lodging. down, Sammy! you know Cathleen.

say how do to Mr. Fourmy."



Sammy cock his head, look the other way, and lift his paw. René

shake it. the dog return to his mistress, who say:



"I cann't keep my hand off the garden. it have get into such a dreadful

state. you two have well go for a walk. you'll find toadstool in the

wood and there may be a few blackberry leave."



she give they a basket and send they forth.



when they come to the wood, René say:



"it want only the river and I could believe that we have never lose

each other for a single day. there be just such mist then: the same

drip in the tree, the same mysterious shrouding of the life of the

wood."



they wander for mile, happy, hardly conscious of each other in the

joy they share. the mist clung about their hair, their eyebrow, and

whip up the color in their cheek and make their eye to shine.

each new path they come to be a promise of adventure, and always in

color and mystery and the play of light the wood fulfil that

promise. René jump all the stile and tease Cathleen because she

be only a woman and could not do the same, and she point out that

man need to do extravagant thing like jump stile or they become

flabby, whereas woman have a more instinctive economy and be

physically more subtle.



"woman," say René, "be ridiculous."



"from a man's point of view. no more ridiculous than a man from a

woman's point of view. the absurdity disappear when they love each

other. then male extravagance and feminine subtlety be only

incidentals----"



"wise young woman."



"I'm a fraud really, René. it's pure Lotta. she be train as a

doctor, you know, and really have watch people. I only guess."



"that's my trouble, too. I only feel quite sure when I reach a certain

stage of emotion."



"I never feel quite sure. nor do Lotta. how can anyone? she say she

have observe certain thing. she say man and woman only make love to

each other as a rule because they love each other so little that they

have nothing else in common."



"and you and I----?"



"have everything."



René laugh.



"except the power to jump stile."



"oh! I love to see you do it."



"and I love to see your inability."



"we both get over it. that be all that matter."



"that's a hard, common-sensible woman."



they reach a place where the tree--beech, pine, and larch--came

march up a steep hill, so steep that they could see over the top

of the tree out to the plain beneath. the mist wreathe and break. a

pale blue sky shine through they, and the sun cast pale yellow light.

Cathleen begin to sing as they plunge down the hill. René start to

run, could not stop himself, and go tear down, shout like mad

until he be bring up by a wide ditch. there he turn and watch

Cathleen thread her way through the tree, singe. the wind come

roaring, whispering and mutter through the leave, and the tree

sway and moan. Cathleen come run the last few yard, and he

catch she. she hold up her laugh face and they kiss, and the

wind seem to sweep through they and set they sway like the tree.

their blood race in their glee.



on the way back they gather blackberry, and in a green clearing in

the wood they find mushroom. happy they be to take such treasure

back to Lotta, their friend, who have make such wonder possible for

they.



she have supper ready for they, the lamp and the fire light, the curtain

draw in the cozy kitchen. after they have eat, they sit with

cigarette and coffee and peppermint round the fire.



Lotta say:



"I know you would find what you want here. I think all lover should

bring their love to the earth and let the wind know that it be there.

how can you love in street and house? they drive the sweetness out

of it and keep it unnaturally excited. I have see so much of that.

woman especially be so house-conscious. they hate everything in love

which threaten their pride of possession and position. they live so

jealously that they want jealousy even in their love----"



"thank you," say René.



"for what?"



"for be so frank. I never be in a house before where there be no

oppression in the atmosphere."



"the house be so much happy since I come to it. it be occupy

before by an old woman who never set foot outside the door for thirty

year. we talk abusively about life in London, but life in village be

even more sordid. country people live even more meanly and graspingly

than townsfolk. there be more stagnation. they be all inbreed. the

people here be all married to cousin, and they be queer in the head

and abnormal. personally, I think the great town grow out of the

necessity for break all that up. english life be far too like a

novel by Emily Brontë. it have to be break up and readjust. it be

much more that than the desire for money. you be both such child

that you have hardly have time to realize the kind of life in which you

be bring up. you have both shake free of it with the violence

that make one so hopeful of the young generation. when you be as

old as I be, you will be able to realize far more than I have do.

the readjustment will be more nearly complete. the reaction from the

evil of industrial life will be even more violent than the reaction

from those of agrarian life. you will know how rare love be, and you

will rejoice that it be give to you to feel it, even though, as it

must not, it be to end to-night." she turn to René and smile at

he with her soft eye. "Cathleen have tell I."



"yes," he say. "I seem to have flounder into be force to live

my own life in my own way."



"Cathleen too. you can only do it together. neither of you could put

up with a mate who desire less and regard every emotion as a bond

instead of a liberation. love be the release of the spirit or it be

not love."



"and if other be to be unhappy?"



"that be their affair. you don't seem to have let that worry you much

until now."



"I never see thing so clearly before. there come a crisis, and I just

plunge blindly. I have a horror of do that again."



"but I don't think you'll ever mind make a fool of yourself, René.

you never do," say Cathleen.



"perhaps not, my dear, but I should hate to make a fool of you."



"everyone," say Lotta, "make mistake. it ben't everyone who will

admit they. once they be admit they often turn out extremely

profitable. really I don't see that you two need have any but

financial anxiety, and that be easily surmount. marriage? neither of

you have a scrap of conventional religion. you cann't possibly be

worry by scruple. really the marriage law of this country be in

such a mess that it have become almost a duty for decent people to

transgress they. they willn't be alter in our time, so there be

nothing for it but to disregard they. you have quite enough real

difficulty to face without trouble yourself about artificial

one. a few virtuous people willn't know you? what be they to you or

you to they?"



"it all come back," say Cathleen, "to that girl."



"she take her risk. she know that. they have courage, some of those

girl."



"be courage," ask René, "all that be necessary?"



"I think so. it be only lack of courage that have make rule of conduct

and religious maxim and precept--crutch and prop. we're all very

stupid at conduct, but if we live by rule and habit there be no hope

of our get any well."



"but you have rule for your hostel."



"I always allow they to be break when there be anything to be gain

by it. I love defiance, but I hate slyness. rule must be break, they

must not be evade. but we be begin to talk for the sake of

talk, and Cathleen be nearly asleep. I'm glad you have have a good

day."



"such a day," he say, "as I never have. I seem to have find that for

which I have always be search, and it have make everything

valuable, even those thing that I have most hate."



"I hope," say Lotta, "that you don't think you have arrive at any

conclusion. it be impossible to decide anything about life. it be

possible only to live--sometimes."



they go to bed very early. the wind have rise to. a gale and

scream in the chimney and the eave.



hardly have rené sink into sleep, the quick easy slumber of health and

peace, than he be rouse by a fearful din. leap out of bed, he run

to the window and open it. the wind came rush in upon his bare

chest and make he gasp for breath. out on the road be a crowd of man

arm with rattle, tin can, kettle, bath, which they bang and

whirl in the air as they march solemnly up the road to the next

cottage. there they move slowly up and down, make a terrible noise

and chant:





     there's evil enough between wind and water

     without your tumbling of the farmer's daughter.

     do you hear Billy bow behind the door?

     there's no honest girl shall be a whore,

              with a billy, billy, billy,

              Billy blow.





they keep this up for a couple of hour in the wind and the rain,

until at last with three groan and hoot they break up and trail

off into the darkness.



René ask Lotta next morning what they might be do, and she tell

he that the man in the cottage be an unpopular character who have

be annoying and molest a girl in the village.



"that be public opinion. they wouldn't have minded if he have be a

popular man, or a rich man. they would have blame the girl in that

case."







Lotta be stay on for a day or two. René take Cathleen back to

London. he tell she he be go to his work and Mitcham Mews and Ann.



"you hear what Lotta say?"



"about the noise last night and the girl?"



"yes. I think it's true. Ann will be blame by her own class."



"would you like I to go and see she?"



"I don't know. I'll tell you that when I have get thing straight with

she--if I ever do."



"I can wait, René," she say. "Time don't seem to matter now. ben't

Lotta splendid?"



"Splendid!"



they shake hand as they part, and each promise to write.









IV



EXPLANATION





     Mais, hélas! quelle raison

     Te fait quitter la maison? . . .

     Et qu'est-ce que je puis faire

     Que je ne fasse pour toi?





during the three day of René's absence Ann do not speak to a soul.

she find the comfort of mortification in read the attorney's

letter from Edinburgh. it make she feel hardly use, and that be

pleasant. René have creep into her life under pretext of be at an

end of his resource when he be incredibly rich. it be not fair: it

be abominable. the grievance become such an obsession as to obscure

her real dread and anxiety. in she almost crazy desire to defend

herself against the alien power that be come to he she tear up the

letter and burn it. he would not know. she would keep he. she would

get he to take she away. it be a good idea of Casey's. they would

all go down into the country. Casey say there be cinema in the

country. through the whole of the last night she sit brood in the

darkness. every now and then she would pretend that he be there in

the next room, in the bed, and she would cling to this pretense until

she have deceive herself and could almost believe that she hear he

there. yes. he be stir in his sleep as he often do. she would go

into the room and run her hand over the pillow. and she

disappointment be a relief. it would have be terrible to have find

he there when she know he be away. where be he? whom be he with?

why don't that beast Kilner know, since it be all that beast's

do, that sly hulk with his sarcastic way of speak and his eye

that look at you as if you be some sort of animal. it must be

Kilner who have get he away. she brood herself into hatred.



in the morning she watch the painter go out, and spat after he.

then she take a knife, go up to his room, find the picture on which

he be work, and slash it to ribbon.



"naked woman!" she cry as she cut away at the canvas. "naked woman!

that'll teach the filthy brute."







it chance that she be out when René return, and he go up to

Kilner's room in the hope of find he. he see the havoc that have

be work, and understand who have do it. when the painter

return René be still try to piece the canvas together. without a

word Kilner take it in his hand, and sit finger it. he say:



"what luck! what infernal luck! I think it be go to put I on my

foot. one of the Professors have be down to see it and be excite

about it. he think he could get it sell for I. there's month of

work in it."



"I shouldn't have thought----"



"I tell you she hate I. I don't think she'd be clever enough to

know how to get back at I. oh! they be clever, these woman, in their

own mean little way. drudge, they be, and drab. it's man like you,

Fourmy, keep they so, ask they for love and take the much they

choose to give you, and when you sicken of it they take their revenge

where they can."



"I never thought----"



"no. damn you! you never do think. by God, I'd rather be the sort of

fool to whom a woman be only a meal or a dinner. there's less mischief

in that. what's the good of your emotion if you cann't control they?

you'd much well give it up like the rest of the world, shut yourself

up in marriage to keep yourself out of harm's way. who the devil be

you, that you should claim in life the freedom an artist hope to get

in his art?"



there be enough truth in Kilner's denunciation to enrage René. he have

feel so clear and confident, so sure of master the event of his

evil, and all this bitterness have he once more throbbing and

confuse.



"what," he cry, "what do a work of art more or less matter? you

cann't expect the rest of we to live in filthiness so that you may

paint picture of a beauty that be never see."



to have sting René into a hot fury seem to appease the artist

somewhat. he grunt and say:



"in a way you're right, and honestly I don't care a hang about the

picture. I can paint it again and well. but I think I be go to

make some money with it, enough to get out of this forever, and it be

almost more than I can bear to know that the harm have come through

you. it don't matter. I'll paint it again. I'll get the fierce

little spark of intelligence burn in Eve. I'd leave that out. I'll

paint her feel half confident of her superiority to both God and

Adam, and ashamed of have to submit to their fatuous pretense of

creation, their old theatrical trick. art and religion! they stink of

the harem and aphrodisiac, the abominable East, the god of lust and

self-mortification. what have your trumpery idealism to say to that?"



he fling the tattered remain of the picture on the fire and hold it

down. the flame consume the paint greedily and roar in the

chimney.



"so much for that," say Kilner. "finish! I'll start again

to-morrow. let's go and see your little vixen and annoy she by show

that she haven't hurt we in the least."



"that's vindictive."



"Ho! have you turn Christian?"



"I'm not go to have Ann moithere."



"and why not? she must learn her lesson."



"let I find out why she do it first."



"I know why she do it. because she think I have take you away from

she."



"she cann't have be jealous of you."



"woman be always jealous of a man's man friend. they know his

feeling can be just as strong for they without be weaken by sex.

and they hate that-- now, a feeling fortify by sex--ah! but that

don't happen."



"that," say René, "be exactly what have happen."



"eh? to you?"



René nod, and he tell Kilner something of the walk in the west

wind, the meeting with Cathleen, the deliverance it have bring to

both of they.



"do she know? Ann, I mean."



"no. I haven't see she."



"she must have feel it. poor little devil! no, I'll not see she. it's

between you two--my rotten picture, Ann's rotten little dream of

happiness, both destroy. you look like a destroyer, my friend. it's

in your eye, your gesture, and movement. absolute purpose, absolute

desire. there's nothing else worth have."



"how absurd you be, Kilner. you turn everything into a picture as

soon as you be interested in it at all. purpose! I feel like a little

schoolboy who have to interview his headmaster. I feel just the same

once when I have be amuse myself with throw paper out of the

window. the headmaster see it, but not the culprit. then I be away

from school ill, and the whole form get into trouble because no one

would own up."



Kilner shout with laughter:



"what a picture of the young Fourmy. do just what he want to do

and evade the consequence by luck. I bet it have all blow over by

the time you get back."



"oh, yes," say René, "but I confess, and no one be very annoyed."



he go round to Ann's room with a sinking at his heart. she must be

tell, she must be make to understand, and she never would. he feel

immeasurably old than she, responsible for she, and rather helpless.

she be out. he gaze round at the room and be touch by its poverty

and thriftlessness, the cheap little ornament on the mantelshelf, the

souvenir of Margate and Southend, the cigarette card pin to the

wall, to make, with a mirror, its only ornament. here they have sit,

so many evening, he and she, in a kind of play at happiness. here

they have quarrel. between quarrel and laugh they have spend all

their day, laugh into quarrel, quarrel into tear, and out of

they again laugh: the happy life of the poor, the worker, the

thoughtless, whom no care could subdue, no joy uplift. what a relief

that life have be to he when he have turn from that other life,

where all his quality be exploit and thought and power of

expression be use only to sneak advantage, and even love and

wed happiness be value only as possession! how it have strip

he of all arrogance and cupidity of mind! the simple innocence of

those who sell themselves for bread, and know nothing of the business

for which they be use, and more despise than envy the show in the

production of which more than half their effort be expend. Ann's

scorn of "lady," believe they all to be light woman, her hatred of

charity organization inspector (she have route they more than once

when they meddle with Rita), Insurance Cards, and Old Age Pensions.

she resent be underpaid, but even more she loathe the spirit

which try to supplement the underpayment with instruction in virtue

make impossible by it, with dole and callous assistance. it have not

escape she that the motor-car in the mew cost more to maintain than

the income of any one of the family who live above they. but she

love her little bare room, and if she be allow to keep they and

the happiness that fill they she ask no more. the brave

independence: that be what René have prize in she, what be express

in her room. he have contribute nothing to it but a little comfort, an

easy chair, a few book, and his pleasure in she. he know that she

treasure that above everything in the world, and he must take it from

she. he be shake with cowardice and dread and pity--by pity most of

all. that bind he to she, drag he down. he have not expect it,

so clear have everything seem in the light of his healthful

experience. and, he know, pity from he would be to she of all thing

the most hateful. he could not shake free of it, and it absorb he.



he hear her footstep on the short flight of stair. he be fill

with a longing to escape. with her hand on the door he lose his head

and flee into the inner room. he hear she go to the fireplace and sit

in the easy-chair. she sit silently brooding. then she hear he in

the inner room. she have hear that before, and he be never there.

slowly she come into the inner room, and he could just see she

smooth the pillow with her hand. she catch the sound of his

breathing and stand stock-still. he could not move. she come toward

he grope with her hand. she touch he.



"Renny, dear."



she be press close to he. her arm go round his neck.



"I know you'd come back."



he caress she soothingly, gently, consume and burn in his pity

for she, and his terror lest she should discover it too suddenly.



he try to draw she into the outer room, but she cling to he and

keep he in the darkness, force he to feel her animal possession of

he and hunger for he. rage and the desire for self-preservation

thrust back his pity and he carry she back to the outer room.



then it be some moment before she could recover herself. she stand

giggle and laugh nervously, almost hysterically.



"Renny, dear," she say, "you do say once we'd go off together. I

want to. I want to. I'm sorry I go on working. I oughtn't to have

do that. we ought to have have a house and I look after it."



"you would have be even more unhappy."



"I'm not unhappy, Renny, dear. you've come back. and there's that

coming----"



("she must be keep off that," he think.)



"Old Martin's be that kind," she say. "he say he'll see we through

if it's money."



"I can make enough money," he reply, and then stop, puzzle and

startle by the malicious pleasure that come into her eye. he lean

forward the well to see she, for the gas jet be flicker, and she

turn away with a half smile that be exasperatingly silly.



"it ben't money," he say, "and you know it. I've see Kilner."



she be instantly defiant on that.



"well, and what have he to say for himself?"



"nothing you would understand."



"Heuh! Clever, ben't you, you two, when you get your head together."



she begin to lay supper. "I'm hungry," she say. "I've not feel like

eat while you've be away. where you been?"



"away," he answer. "out of London."



"to your home?"



"no."



"I think you'd have go to your home."



"there's nothing to take I there. I've be with friend."



"woman?"



"yes."



she have nothing to say to that. he go on:



"one of they I know year ago, when I be a boy."



"that's not so long ago. a lady?"



"yes."



"a lady wouldn't take up with you now."



"she work for her living."



"the same as I?"



"the same as you."



"well. what of it?"



"we go down into the country, she and I and her friend."



"I don't want to know about that."



"but I want to tell you."



she stand by the table and her finger draw pattern on the cloth.



"what be it you want to tell I?"



"I'm in love with she."



Ann's lip set in a hard line, and her eye narrow and her brow

scowl.



"do you come back to tell I that?"



"yes."



"why? do you think I'd want to know?"



"I'm so sorry."



"sorry, you devil? you come down to torment I. you'd well go, d'ye

hear."



René could not move. he be fascinate by the suffering in the little

creature, melt and weaken by his pity for she.



"you'd well go," she repeat. "and tell she you leave a poor girl

hate you, and see how she'll like that. sorry! that's what you say

when you step on a fellow's foot in a bus. sorry! when you get a girl

body and soul, and you throw she away like dirt."



"I come back."



"yes. to tell I that. to tell I I be dirt, to throw I down for she

to walk on so's she shalln't get her foot wet."



she change her tone and ask quietly:



"you know she before I?"



"long year before."



"before that other one as you marry?"



"before that."



"and she's pretty and have pretty thing?"



"I've tell she about you."



"oh! and she send you back! thank you for nothing."



"she do not. I come of my own accord. I couldn't leave you like

that."



"I'd rather you do. I'd rather you do. my Christ! I cann't bear to

see you sit there and talk and talking----"



he rise to his foot: "I cann't leave you, Ann. I couldn't leave you

like you be. . . ."



she lean across the table and put out her hand.



"look here, Renny. D'you love I?"



"yes."



"Heh!" she give a snarl of incredulity. "heh! see here! D'you want

I!"



her eye be stare at he cunningly, invitingly. he see that she

half believe his weakness would lead he to evasion or consent to she

will. he wait, and make she repeat her question.



"D'you want I?"



"I want your happiness," he say. "I don't believe you will find it in

I."



she be inarticulate. her eye close and she sway. she jerk she

head toward the door. he take that for a sign that he be to go, and

move round the table. she be before he, crouch, bar the way.

strangle sob sound come from her throat. he stretch out his

hand to implore she, to tell she of his almost intolerable pity. she

spring at he. she have a knife in her hand. he see it flash, feel a

burn pain in his breast, and fall. he could see her face twist in

an agony of fear close to his. spittle from her lip fall upon his

cheek. her hand be busy at his breast. he lose consciousness.









V



THRIGSBY





            nothing I'll bear from thee

     but nakedness, thou detestable town!





that be an appalling night. René lie with his wound roughly

staunch. Ann crouch in the darkness by the bedside, fondle his

hand, cling to he, occasionally weep. both watch the light

come creep over the roof and chimney. neither could say a word.

their eye meet, and hers be fix hungrily on his face like a dog's

that have be whip for fight. she look so scared that he

desire only to reassure she.



"Ann," he say.



she kiss his hand and fondle it, and press it to her cheek, and

bathe it in her tear and kiss away the tear.



"you'd well fetch Kilner," he say. "he'll know what to do."



"don't let he know how it happen. don't let he know I do it."



"no. go and fetch he."



"oh! I think you be dead. I think you be dead. oh! Renny, dear,

what should I ha' do if you'd be dead, my dear?"



"go and fetch Kilner. he'll tell we what to do."



she bring Kilner and leave they together. René make a clumsy attempt

to shield Ann in a very incoherent account of the affair. Kilner see

through it but acquiesce in the intention.



"can you move?" he ask.



"I think so."



"can you walk to a doctor's? there's one just round the corner. well

than have he here. some doctor talk. you'll be well out of

this."



lean on Kilner's arm, René manage to reach the doctor's, but there

he faint. Kilner invent a story of an early morning street attack,

and the doctor, who be not interested, swallow it. he patch rené

up, give he a prescription, and tell he to call again that day. René

dislike the man so much that he refuse inwardly ever to go near he

again. between they they have half the fee, and promise to send round

the rest.



Kilner make René comfortable in his room and be then send off to find

Miss Cleethorpe.



Lotta come at once. she and Kilner like each other. Kilner have begin

to see the affair in a humorous light. anything to do with René be to

he never very far short of absurdity.



"I wish I'd think of it like that before," he say. "I'd never have

let he go to she. I might have know he would make a mess of it. he

be simply burst with exaltation, and when he's like that it never

occur to he that other people may have a different view. I half

believe he expect Ann to share his enthusiasm for the other

lady----"



Lotta could not help laugh, though she protest: "what a shame!"



"I cann't help it," say Kilner, "other people's love affair always

be comic, and Fourmy--well, he be simply inappropriate in a community

of creature who live by cunning."



"you've hit it," reply Lotta. "I've be try to understand what

it be make he so exceptional. creature who live by cunning----

thank you, Mr. Kilner."



"all artist be like that. Cunning be no use in the pursuit of art.

but they be insulate by their work as ordinary people be by

convention and habit. no artist take personal relationship

seriously. they happen. he handle they well or make a mess of they.

it do not greatly matter. the ordinary being cannot appreciate any

personal relationship until it be conventionalize and strip of its

vigor and value. well--you have see your Fourmy in action."



"and well worth see too."



Kilner tell she what he could make of the new disaster, and how Ann

have hate he and destroy his work.



"I imagine," he say, "that the same blind instinct operate against

Fourmy. he's creative also in a way. my picture, his life, his

precious romantic life, be both thing slowly shape out of chaos,

and the creative process in a man be absolutely indifferent to the

stupid security most woman value. Ann do she ridiculous little good

to stop it in both of we."



"poor girl," say Lotta, "I can imagine the two of you drive she

distracted. after all, what she be go through be important to

she."



"but only to she. she want it to be important for he. it couldn't

be: it be quite meaningless."



"nature be cruelly indifferent."



"if she ben't," say Kilner, "we should never have develop

intelligence, let alone imagination."



"what be we to do with they?"



"I'll look after Fourmy if you'll take charge of Ann. only, remember,

you be not suppose to know that she do it, and, please, I have tell

you nothing about my picture."







the caution be unnecessary, for Ann tumble out a full confession as

she sink into the comfort of Lotta's kindness. she guess at once who

Lotta be, but be too exhausted for resentment. she have drag

herself off to her work in order to fill in the creep hour.



Lotta say she be a friend of René's, and wish to help, and ask

if there be anything she could do. Ann burst into tear and roll

her head from side to side, and cry:



"oh! I wish I be dead, I do. I nearly do myself in last night when

he lie there in the dark not say a word. I wish I have--I wish I

have. I never be so miserable. . . ."



Lotta comfort she as well she could, clumsily drop a word in

here and there as Ann pour out her confused narrative.



Ann keep on say:



"he ought to have go if he want to go."



"but he couldn't leave you like that----"



"it be see he again do it. I couldn't bear it, see he and

know he be want to go."



"he be want you to feel that--that he be not go out of

indifference to you."



"he don't want I. he say that."



"my dear child, you mustn't think about it like that. you must see

that it be end now."



"I'll never care for anybody again--not like that."



"don't make thing hard for yourself. how do you know?"



"you're only young once."



"love be strong when youth be go."



Ann believe that. she want to believe in Lotta, and she sit very

quietly, almost like a child, while the quiet, gentle woman try to

explain to she that René have take nothing away, that their love must

die for all it have lack, that there be no disgrace in a failure to

bring a love to life, that it be happen everywhere, every day, and

that a dead love be the most horrible of prison. and, say Lotta, if

a child be to be bear, it be well not to bring it into such

captivity, well not to have the joy and beauty of motherhood spoil

by jealousy and disappointment in the failure of love. ann weep anew.

People be so kind, she say: there be Old Martin, and now there be

Lotta; and she have only dread her loneliness of be leave alone to

face "that." Lotta say there be no question of be leave alone. if

Ann like, she could come to her hostel as maid, and when her time

come she could go out to the country.



"I think," say Lotta, "that all child ought to be bear and breed in

the country. don't you?"



"the mew," reply Ann, "be not much of a place for they."



she do not quite like the idea of be "in service," but Lotta

explain that it do not necessarily mean for always. once the baby

be bear and provide for, Ann could go back to her factory and take

up her life, if she wish, where it be before René come into it.



"but I'll always want to hear about he," say Ann.



"of course. he'll always want to hear about you."



"and see he."



"he'll want to see you too."



so it be arrange, and Ann promise to be at the hostel next morning.







when Lotta have go, she sit down and write:





"DEAR RENNY,--I do want you to forgive I. I have be awful, but not

without excuse. I do like Miss Lotta. she's be an angel to I and

make I feel awfuller. I'm go to she. a letter for you to say you

'ad come into some money. I tear it up when I first begin to feel bad

toward you. I don't feel bad any more.--Your love ANN."





this confession reach René at the same time as a letter from his

brother George conveying the same news. the attorney in Edinburgh have

write to say he have no reply from Mr. René Fourmy, and to ask for

information as to his whereabouts. "this," say George, "have be a

bit of a shock to we. we'd count on something from the old lady.

however, it make a difference to you. if you feel inclined to come up

and see we I'll be glad to have you. I suppose you'll give up the

street-slogging. the old man have be in London. do you see he?"



René announce his intention of go to Thrigsby. his mind be go

back and back over his life in the attempt to understand it. if he

could see George and his mother, he feel and hope that he might be

able to follow up the thread place in his hand by his chance

encounter with his father.







a day or two later see he arrive at the Albert Station with his arm

in a sling. George be there to meet he.



"Hullo, old sport," he say, "be in the war?"



René tell the lie invent by Kilner for the doctor.



"by Jove," say George, "you have be rough it. I'll tell that to

the youngster in our office when they get dotty about Canada and the

Wild West. Wild West of London, eh?" and he chuckle at his own joke.



"Elsie's quite excited," he say, as they board the Hog Lane tram.



"and mother?" ask René.



"well. Hum. you'll find a difference in the mother."



René be strike by many change. new warehouse, new row of shop,

some attempt to bring distinction into the architecture of the city,

though, for the most part, nothing but ostentation be attain. they

pass the university. there be new building there, more like an

insurance office than ever. street that he remember as respectable

and prosperous have become slum swarm with grimy child. a great

piece have be take out of Potter's Park for the building of a

hideous art gallery. the tram now pass down Hog Lane West, with the

result that most of the house have apartment card in their fanlight.

George have move from the Nest into 168. he could get a large house

for the same rent. his house be exactly the same as their old home.

it give René a depressing idea that nothing have change. George be

fat: Elsie thinner. they have four child.



George be in the same office, and, as he say, have fling away

ambition: too many child to take risk, and after all there be

nothing in the small firm now. the one or two connection you depend

on might go bust any day. it need enormous capital to stand the

fluctuation of price. he have get a rise by pretend to go and be

quite content. he play bowl in the summer and bridge in the winter.

and Elsie? what with the house and the mother she have plenty to do,

plenty to do.



as René walk along the passage he feel uncannily certain that he

would find his mother sit by the fireplace knitting. and it be

so. she raise her eye and look at he with timid anxiety, hold out

her cheek to be kiss, go on knitting, and say:



"now sit down and give an account of yourself."



he edit his experience, and she listen without interest. Most of

his talk be of Kilner.



"artist be very immoral man, ben't they?"



René shrug.



"it depend," he say, "on what you mean by morality."



"there _be_ rule," say she, "and commandment."



"my friend have rule," he reply, "rather good one. he dislikes

do anything which interfere with his power to paint."



"to I that sound very selfish."



"I don't think we can argue about that, mother."



"no. I suppose you make very little money."



"three pound a week."



"I suppose you could do with that, with only yourself to keep. though

it seem a pity, consider the amount of time and money spend on

your education."



be it his mother speak? what have happen to she? whence have come

the dry hardness in her voice? why be her eye so dead? they use to

steal quick little glance when she speak. now she only stare

listlessly. a home-come? this for a home? in the house next door

there have be some stirring of life: the night when he have return

home from Scotland: the strange day after his father's restoration.



the window of the room be shut. René feel stifle. he make some

excuse and go away out of the house, and roam through the familiar

street. there be many house empty: the garden, some of which have

once be trim, be now unkempt. the whole district be dismal and

devitalize. only the red trams clang and clank down the cobble

street make any stir and gaiety.



he find himself presently in Galt's Park. the little pink brick

house have invade it. many of the big house be pull down: other

be be demolish, and only jagged wall and gape window be

leave. on the site of the Brocks' house stand a little red-brick chapel

outside which be announcement in Welsh and English. that give he a

shock. some of the past life have be brush away. he dislike the

idea of its room be usurp by a chapel, a place of Christian

worship. he do not know why he dislike this idea so much, but it be

connect vaguely with the image of his mother sit in that room,

knitting and talk in an empty voice, and cling obstinately to

rule of conduct.



at the other end of Galt's Park he come on a new street, flung

straight across what he remember as field. follow its dreary

completely shut in with little pink brick house. he turn in at the

look very mysterious. he take a hat down, seize René by the arm

and lead he out into the street.



"well, well," he say. "I'm glad to see you, glad to see you. how be

you?"



"very well." René feel inclined to laugh. clearly the Professor be

try not to hurt his feeling and to disguise the fact that he do

not think he fit to enter his house, that temple of domesticity.



"tell I about yourself. one don't lose interest, you know."



this time René do not edit his experience.



"I have hear story," say the Professor. "I be reluctant to believe

they."



"why?"



"well--er-- you know--one expects----"



"that every man will do his duty."



"it be hardly a subject for satire," say the Professor.



René explode:



"good God! what else be it a subject for? England expect? do the

whole duty of man consist in self-mutilation? why, then, the noble

man be he who shirk every responsibility, let his mind rot and his

feeling wither, so that he can attain a devilish efficiency at the

job into which he tumble before he have begin to develop enough to

know what he can do. these be your successful man, your pundit, your

Lord Mayors, your merchant prince, your politicians----"



"my dear Fourmy, I think you should recollect that you hardly give

yourself time to recognize what Thrigsby stand for, the great

industrial center in the world."



"I have time enough to realize what it have do for my father, my

mother, my brother, and myself."



"two wrong do not make a right, and I do not think you set about

remedy matter in the right way. you have every opportunity here.

you have escape the pressure of industrialism. you have good brain."



"brain!" cry René. "I have escape from industrialism only to talk

about it."



"we be do useful work. the defect of the system be slowly be

"cann't you realize they without investigation? ben't they as plain as

the nose on your face?"



"you cann't find a remedy without investigation. that lead to mere

sentimental socialism. but why need we quarrel about that? you don't

like the work. I hope you find more satisfaction in your
