spend money: not that I see much of that except from the outside;

still I do see all sort and kind of people, and you can make rough

conclusion about they."



"you don't mind my suggesting that you be hardly in a condition to

make impartial observation?"



"we don't seem able to use the same term. you still think I be a

fool not to stay in my nice little home, with my nice little job and

my nice little income."



"I don't judge you. I only say that if everybody be to do the

same----"



"I only wish more people would. there'd soon be an end of congestion.

I only come round to-night because I couldn't stand the sight of my

brother settle down to his nice little home and my mother fast

should enter and pollute your nice little home. I tell you, what I

have see to-day have settle I. I come up here in a muddle about it

all, half feeling that I have make an ass of myself, but I'm absolutely

certain now----"

unjust. I have no fear of your disturb my household. we should be

only too glad to see you, only it happen, if you must know, that my

wife be expect Linda Brock. she use her own name now."



René give a shout of laughter.



"but I'd like to see she. how be she?"



"her mother die six month ago and leave she a great deal of money, a

fortune. we have no idea she be so rich. Linda write some play, you

know. she have buy the theater and present it to the Players. I be

one of the trustee. Thrigsby be very proud of the theater----



"it use to be music when I be young," say René, "and the orchestra

be always in debt."



"art," say the Professor, "cannot be expect to pay for itself. we

be run the theater to a certain extent in connection with the

he have assume the voice in which he lecture. René cut he short:



"I'd like to see Linda. will you take I back with you."



"I--er----"



"you needn't thrust I on she. just ask she if she'd like to see I,

and come out and tell I: yes or no. after all, if it come to that,

we're still married. I believe, by the brutal law of the country, I

could insist on see she whether she like it or not. you might tell

she that I have come into some money also."



"really? I'm so glad."



"Hurrah!" cry René, "you think I'll have to live up to it and settle

down."



"it would certainly be a splendid thing if----"



the Professor's whole attitude toward he be change. already, it be

clear, he be begin to plan a grand scene of reconciliation, a

background, symbolical of impregnable Matrimony. René catch the hint

and his mind play with it and blow it out into a grotesque. it give

he so much pleasure that he chuckle and say:



"it willn't do, you know. we couldn't come together again without a

scandal."



the Professor be so intent on his own thought that he do not notice

the savage irony of the remark. he say:



"it would soon die down."



"soon than the other?"



"Well----!"



"I've get you there," observe René. "it ben't fair though. I haven't

the slight intention of do any such thing."



"why, then----?"



"why do I want to see she? I don't know. I want to. ben't that reason

enough?"



they have return to the house.



"you just ask she. tell she I'm in Thrigsby for a few day and would

like to see she. if she don't wish it, don't worry. I'll wait ten

minute."



"very well," say the Professor, not altogether give up hope, "I'll

tell she, but the way you talk of it seem to I almost indecent."



he let himself in at the front door, and in ten minute be out again.



"very well," he say, "she will see you. . . . if you don't mind, my

wife have go up to her room."



"I wonder," think René, "what they would make of Ann. they wouldn't

mind my leave she."



he feel rather nervous as he reach the threshold of the study, but

stiffen himself for the plunge. the door open and he find himself

explain how he come to be in Thrigsby. Linda be noticeably

think she unsuitably dress, tactlessly, provincially. on the

whole, he like she. the handshake be firm, her eye be frank.



"it be nice of you to come and see I," she say. "so much well to

have no nonsense about it."



"if you like," say the Professor, "I--i--will----"



Linda appeal to René.



"oh, no. I've nothing to say. I only want to know that there be to

be no nonsense between we. I'm very glad. I wish we could have arrive

at that soon, but I suppose that be impossible."



Linda smile:



"you've change, René. that would have be blasphemy to you a few

year ago. you hate come to your sense."



"I should think so," say the Professor.



"you're not go to stay in Thrigsby?" ask Linda.



"no. that's impossible, even if I want to. we should be cross

each other's track. not that I should mind that, but---- well, it

wouldn't do, would it?"



"no. I prefer be without a husband. really, for an active woman it

seem to I to be the ideal condition. she have a status and no risk."



the Professor sit bolt upright:



"what _do_ you mean, Linda?"



"I willn't insist on the advantage if it shock you, Phil. René

understand I."



"oh, yes," say René, "Linda mean she can lose her head without any

danger of getting marry."



the Professor explode:



"I never hear of anything so--so abominable."



"but I do mean that," say Linda. "woman do lose their head, you

know, even when they be married. ask Freda. don't look so hurt. she

and I be talk it over yesterday, and we agree that the law be

so horrid that all I could do be to disregard it. and if René be

willing that be what I propose do. you shall represent the world at

large. you do represent its opinion. you know----"



"I do not."



Linda pass over the interruption:



"you be the world at large and I say to you: 'this man be no long

my husband.' no more than that should be necessary. you don't want any

more than that, do you, René?"



"even that seem to I a needless statement of fact, but perhaps I'm

extreme."



the Professor rise and stand with his back to the fireplace: "all

this," he say, "be extremely distasteful. you be make a mock of

marriage."



Said René:



"we know more about it than you. we've try disruption and you

haven't. we're both the well for it. the fact be, there be no such

thing as marriage. there be marriage, and precious few of they.

your, no doubt, be one of the few."



the Professor be mollify, swallow the harangue he have prepare,

and sit down again.







René take Linda to her house in a remote suburb. she say:



"you know I quite dread meeting you again. I always have a feeling I

should. the poor dear Professor be quite disappointed because we

don't make a scene."



"oh, he don't mind once we make it quite clear that we be cast

no shadow of doubt upon the sanctity of his own domestic happiness.

they're all like that."



"I'm sure he's quite convinced that you have become very wicked. have

you?"



"no. Strict monogamist."



"what do you mean by that?"



"one wife at a time."



Linda laugh at he. "you always be uncompromise."



her laughter grate on René. he have a revulsion of feel against

she. she be, he realize, and always have be, cynical.



at her gate she hold his hand for a long time, and ask he if he

would not come and see she again.



"I think not," he say.



"I wish you would. we might be such friend. and you have become so

interesting."



"I think not," he repeat. "any friendship we might have would only

be an----" he could not find the word and stop rather foolishly. he

could not move until he have find it. so they stand there hand in hand

wait in a ridiculous and empty silence.



"would be what?" she ask in irritation.



he find the word.



"an impertinence."



she shake his hand from hers almost angrily and walk away.







he know then why he have come to Thrigsby. it be to make a clean cut

with she. that achieve, there be nothing more in the grim city of

his youth to keep he.









VI



the COMFORT of RELIGION





    Quoi! dieu I punirait éternellement de m'être livré à des

    passion qu'il m'a données?





there might be nothing to keep he, but yet he stay five day

long. for one thing George's child be amusing and a profitable

study. they have discover that they have only to lie to their parent

to keep they quiet, and, as lie be expect of they, and make

thing comfortable, they see no harm in it. for the rest they do as

they please and amuse themselves. little George be the very spit of

his grandfather and a great spinner of yarn. René tell he one

morning to ask his mother if he could go out with he. off trot the

boy, to return in a moment with a detailed account of the conversation

he have have. it transpire subsequently that Elsie be out at the time.

René tell she. she say:



"I don't know what to do about the child. he have such an imagination."



"I prefer to call that invention. imagination be the one quality in

you that appreciate truth. I should begin if I be you by satisfy

his curiosity. tell he the truth about anything he want to know."



"but he want to know such awful thing."



"what awful thing?"



"well, about I and George."



"it's hard to put a lie straight once you've tell it. it be terribly

easy to lose your respect for your parent."



"oh, but little George love we."



"how do you know?"



"he say so nearly every night."



"oh, well," say René, "people believe only what they like to

believe."



Elsie be rather ruffle:



"after all, they're our child."



"certainly. you'll find out what they think of it soon enough."



it be interesting to watch the process which go to make up the

fool's paradise that George and Elsie, in common with their kind,

call Home, the worship of lip-virtue, the constant practice of mean

little subterfuge, George dodge Elsie's interest and suspicion of

himself, she his of she, and the child, where necessary,

contribute to the comedy and, for the rest, live thoroughly,

selfishly, and callously in their own pursuit.



René find that as long as he would let George talk about bridge,

bowl, and business, or splutter abuse of Radical legislation, and as

long as he allow Elsie to chatter of the neighbor and child and

music-hall and clothe, they be both quite happy.



with his mother it be otherwise. she be uneasy in his presence and

they could hardly talk at all, except about their relation, the rich

Fourmys, and the shabby trick they have do; but after a while René

become aware that they be hold a stealthy converse, an

undercurrent to the word they use. he try all sort of device to

bring it to the surface but without success. his mother would relapse

into silence or, without a word, would hurry off to her church and

return impenetrably encase in humility, pale with emotional satiety.

there be something abnormal about she then, something unnatural that

make René's flesh creep. when it have pass he would feel once more

the wildness in she that she keep so savagely repressed.



he recognize at last that he be stay on in the hope of

penetrate her defense. having come to that, he attack her one

at the church for she to escape to. like the dutiful husband he be,

George make a practice of take Elsie to a music-hall once a week, a

music-hall or two cinema, as she choose.



Mrs. Fourmy have put down her knitting and say:



"I think I would like a game of patience, René."



he put out the table and the card and they play. he say:



"I wonder how you can stand see they play the old, old game."



"what old game?"



"marriage. kill each other in the first few week and

then--humbug."



"George be a very good husband and father."



"he live with a woman in his house and child come automatically."



"he be very good to Elsie."



"he placate she."



Mrs. Fourmy take out the ace of diamond and cover it. René say:



"do you ever think, mother, of how we use to say we'd go and live

together?"



"sometimes. I know it be just nonsense."



her eye give he a quick little affectionate glance, search for

affection. ah! that be well.



"not such nonsense, either. why shouldn't you go and live in Aunt

Janet's cottage? it be that I be think of, though I never think

it would be mine."



"I'd be so lonely."



"no lonely than you be here."



"no."



that escape she involuntarily. she cover it up.



"you're too old for that sort of talk, René. you're not a boy any

long."



"I'm much young than I be then."



"yes, that's true. would you come too?"



"no. i--I'm go south again."



"have you meet--she?"



"yes."



"I think so." her hand tremble. "be you--be you go to live

with she?"



"I hope so."



"it will be live in sin. I couldn't live in your house if I know

that----"



"you prefer George?"



"I--I---- please don't talk about it any more, René."



"I must. you love I far more than you love George, and yet you prefer

to accept a home from he rather than from I."



"certain thing be wrong, René."



"I take my chance of that."



"we ben't give any choice."



"hell in this world or hell in the next."



"don't speak lightly of such thing, René."



"I see my father in London."



Mrs. Fourmy let the card trickle from her hand, and sit stare at

he with weary, frightened eye.



"you be your father over again."



"he tell I. then it be your love or your religion----"



"don't, René, don't!"



he could not continue. he watch she live again in the agony of the

memory, right with it, fight it back, stifle the hunger in

slip to her knee in an attitude of prayer.



René go to his room at the back of the house, the exact counterpart

of his old den. he curse that jealous God, that brutal invention of

cowardice which have lay waste the western world. his rage only

subside when he come to think of Cathleen. he take paper and pen and

write to she:





"I seem hardly at all different from the boy who use to write to you.

it be almost exactly the same room, the same hour, only now it be my

brother and sister-in-law who occupy the big bed in the big front

room. the window look out at the same light window opposite. and I

be the same except that I know myself well and be more sure. what an

extraordinary phantasmagoria between our parting and our meeting! how

worthless and external adventure can be! how worthless and external

the more intimate relationship! but without adventure, without

mistake, folly, suffering, how be that discovery to be make? I

suppose my brother never could have make it, but he must have have,

perhaps even now he have, his moment when his desire tug against his

little round of habit. he would call himself a happy man, and perhaps

he be so. perhaps we all get what we desire. that would be a

comfortable creed, and I could believe it be it not for my mother.

one be not bear of a woman for nothing. something bind. there be a

deep knowledge than that of the mind. there be in my mother a

quality with which I feel at home, free. but she withhold it from I.

I feel she hate it in I, as in herself, as in my father. hard to

find anything else in common between they. I tell you that story of

how she surrender to he when he come back. it must have be that

in she, take unawares. it have live without alarm for so long. it have

be stir in she when I come back from Scotland so full of that

idiotic love for you--and after that, I cann't follow. too near to it

perhaps, or perhaps it be obscure in I by all I have go through

since. but now she baffle I. she have suffer. yes. we all suffer,

but suffering lead to discovery, to joy, or life be altogether

barren. she suffer, she must suffer from live here in the dull

house, but she take her suffering and bottle it up, sterilize it

with religion. her comfort! from the bottom of my heart I hate it.

when she be full of what she call her religion, then I can only bear

with she by my inborn knowledge of she, and for that only the more do

I det the poison that have ruin her splendid life. and how it have

be exploit, this voluptuous, selfish pleasure which they dare to

call prayer and worship, this cowardly refusal to follow suffering

withersoever it lead. I cannot be tolerant about it. to thousand I

know, it be no more than bridge or bowl to my brother George, a

pastime. but with she, and with all who have a capacity for suffering,

it be a passionate negation, and to have live at all must be a

horror. you see, I be almost inarticulate about it. I have try to

break through it and fail. she see, and close her eye, as she must

have see time and again. the delight of see almost deliberately

debase to fear. I wish I be more used to think about people,

then I could make it more clear. but it don't seem much use, for I

go on believe in they and like they and expect all sort of

thing that never come. oh, the freedom that I find with you, and the

thought of you! everything you understand, and all the difference

between we we can just laugh at and use. I must take you to some place

where we can build up a healthy life. now that I have money, I think

for a time that we would go and live in Scotland in my house. (how odd

that look. I really be pleased with my possession for the first

time.) that would not do. there must be work and activity. we'll have

a brave time make plan to keep each other and everybody we know

happy and keen. no more grubby humbugging, and no more Mitcham Mews.

we'll find a way. . . ."





there come a tap at his door. he go to open it. his mother stand

there.



"ben't you go to bed, René? George and Elsie come home long ago."



"I be write a letter."



"you shouldn't stay up, waste the gas and all."









VII



CASEY'S VENTURE





     Fortis imaginatio venerat casum.





CATHLEEN reply:



"I think you be hard on your mother. you love she too well to judge

she, but you read yourself into she. you do that with I too, and I be

sometimes alarmed when I think how I may disappoint you. but then I

trust you so completely. you give so much that what you give turn at

once into a gift from I to you, and that make I give too. so it

go on like rain and cloud and river. don't try to upset your little

family. they willn't like it. keep all the upsetting for I. I love it

and need it constantly."



he be very happy with this letter, carry it in his pocket and

finger it continually. under its influence he cease to chafe

against his surrounding, and make no further attempt to force himself

on his mother, and in her shy way she seem to take pleasure in his

exuberance.



the Edinburgh attorney send an advance of £100. he post £20 to

Kilner, and besought he to leave Mitcham Mews and find a studio or go

down into the country. another twenty he send to Lotta for Ann. he

buy his mother an indian shawl and provide Elsie with two dress,

tailor-make. the child be take to a toy shop and allow to

select three treasure each. little George hesitate for a long time

between a helmet and a whip, and finally choose the latter because his

small brother be no good as a soldier, but quite fair as a horse.



when René announce that he must go, George declare that they would

"make an evening of it," and they play bridge until ten, and then in

the parlor Mrs. Fourmy draw soft music from the old piano with its

yellow key. under her hand the beauty of the Moonlight Sonata seem

fade, and René think sadly that it be like the beauty of her life,

fade and go to dust. and as she play he take down the old family

copy of Shakespeare, a vulgar edition spoil with colored portrait

of actor and actress. he open it at random and his eye fall on

these word:





     fear no more the heat o' the sun

       nor the furious winter's rage:

     Thou thy worldly task hast do,

       home art go and ta'en thy wage:

     golden lad and girl all must,

     as chimney sweeper, come to dust.





and tear come to his eye, and he be fill with love and

appreciation for these good kinsfolk of his who find such wealth in

their little happiness and be so easily console in their little

sorrow. and in the music it seem that he and his mother could meet,

have find a language which both could understand, a song to unite

passionate acceptance and passionate denial in the peace of the soul.



George say he never do think much of classical music, and ask

Elsie to sing his favorite song: "Poppyland."



that do, they join hand and sing "for Auld Lang Syne."



his mother come to see René in his bed. she say:



"you willn't come again."



"how do you know that?"



"I feel it. you've be very good and you have make I very happy."



"then I'll come again."



"I don't want you to come again. you'll never be the same. George be

always the same."



René remember how his father have say she have do she good to keep

they from ever be man.



"all right, mother. I wouldn't like it to be a pain for you to see

I."



she smile.



"it always be pain, René, dear, because I have to let you go."



he draw she down to he and kiss she. she say:



"an old woman like I."



he whisper:



"there'll always be some music that I can never hear without think

of you."



"yes," she say. "you be always the one to listen. and your father

like it too--some thing."



"I'll think of that too."



"yes. think kindly of your father. we both do try."



and she creep away. René call after she, but she do not hear he.

he wish to keep she with he, to try to find some word that should

comfort she. but he know at once that the word would elude he, that

there be nothing to say, that he and she be lose to each other, and

must go their way. all his effort, all his hope could wake no

response in she. the mention of his father make he know how dearly

she have love the man, and he begin to perceive the subtle force of

love, how it can live in defiance of the will, and even through the

failing of desire; how it use even difference, even rupture to bind

and sustain; and how even the most selfish soul be knit with other,

though it be to the destruction of every pleasant joy. he see how

little love need consciousness, and how desperately man stand in need

of it. else be they consume in love, and never for a moment do their

life take form and color before they sink to dust again, not wholly

create before they be destroy. idea of Kilner's come rush back

to René's mind, his description of his vision, the slow insistence on

be give expression and form in paint, his own helplessness against

the tyranny of what his eye have see and his imagination master.

René begin to understand that, to lose sense of time, to find in

himself also a vision that have possess he always. only, unlike

Kilner, he could not trace it back to any moment of ecstasy, any keen

appreciation of some natural beauty, or the play of light. light! that

be the create idea. Kilner respond to the light of the sun, René

to the light of the imagination, the light of the sun work upon by

man's mind, so that their life also have its sun to bring fertility,

and make the body a spirit and love an intellectual thing; the light

of the sun store through all the generation to dissipate the terror

of life and the power of death, to concentrate upon all beloved

object and show they in their loveliness as vision urge to

creation. and in his love of woman man seek no reflection of his

light but the flash of her, that her beauty may not perish.



René in his joy begin to sing to himself. it be the song Cathleen have

sing in the wood. he could see she again as she be there in the

green haze of the wood, in the dapple light, mysterious and wild.



from that he deliberately turn away to fix his gaze on the humorous

reality, because there be nothing that he do not desire to sweep

into his joy. he light a match and gaze round the little, cheaply

furnish room, the ugly toilet service, the yellow dressing-table,

the silly pattern wall-paper of pallid rose, the execrable picture

on the wall. his eye be dazzle by the light, and they ache. come

darkness again, and he hum to himself as he think of the morrow

and the train, with its wheel hum along the rail, take he

nearer the goal of his desire.

in the morning George shake he warmly by the hand when he come down,

"good luck," he say. "good luck, old man. Elsie really have love

"yes."



her eye look hunger and reproach. she turn her face away.

     .        .        .        .        .        .



at last he be in the train. no sleep this time. Derby, Leicester,

"poor little Ann," say René.



"I tell you she have courage."

but what could we do? even from you----"

"it be horrible, but they do interfere."



"do Cathleen know?"



"yes. I tell she last night."



"well?"



"it seem to bring home to she for the first time how terrible and

"yes. it can be dispel."



"only with time."



"yes."



Lotta say:



"I like the way you face thing. there be no one like you for

"for the time being, with Kilner, I think."



"I find he a little studio in Hampstead. he be delighted and happy

Lotta give he the direction, and in a few minute by Tube he be with

"that's bad."



"seen anybody?"



"only Miss Cleethorpe."



"she's a fine woman. I think I shall marry she. she's twenty year

"Fourmy," say Kilner, "you're an incorrigible romantic. I'm a

. . ."

"not in so many word, but in effect."



"well, I'm----"

"Gone."

I."



there be a tap at the door, and Casey appear. he rush excitedly

"our station," say Kilner. "Lotta's and mine."



"it's a busy little town, but it need brisk up, like you say, Mr.

he produce his map and show the geographical advantage of Rickham.

it have already good water and electric light. its train service have

Kilner be interested, and say to René:



"so you think that be how thing be go to work themselves out?

it's an attractive idea, the country for food, a ring of industrial

center, and the exchange in the middle of it all. some sort of shape

and design instead of the muddle we're in. it might even make room for

the artist."



Casey say:



"when I hear you'd come in for some money I couldn't rest until I'd

find what I want, and there it be. will you come in?"

live in your Thrigsby or your london any more, and I couldn't live in

the country without do the work of the country."



"cann't see you as a farmer," say Kilner.



René promise to go with Casey the next day.



     .        .        .        .        .        .



he be enchant with Rickham and with the yard. it have a small

georgian house attach to it, and the stable be build round a

quadrangle with a gallery lead to room above they. through the

stable be a walled garden, and beyond that again a bowling green by

the edge of a stream. the whole be freehold and wonderfully cheap.

Rickham apparently be not yet awake to its glorious future in the

english democracy in spite of its two cinema, and the strong

liberalism of its opinion. it have one church and fifteen chapel, a

Salvation Army barrack, and a public house every twenty yard. on the

hill behind it villa be be erect, and along the valley little

house be be build for workpeople. on either side of the river

just outside the old town the tall chimney of factory be rise

by the steel skeleton of new workshop. clearly there be some truth

in what Casey say. they undertake to buy the stable and walk into

a lawyer's office to give instruction.



so certain have Casey be that René would come in with he that he have

already engage mechanic in London, and write up to various firm

local builder to carry out the necessary alteration, and on the

advice of their solicitor arrange a contract. before any work be

above the gate, and below it, the name of the firm, "Casey & Fourmy."



"look like business, that," he say, as they stand in the street and

survey it with satisfaction. "give the town something to talk about.

no advertisement like talk."









VIII



THRIVING





     "be you married in a church, Ursula?"



     "we be not, brother: none but gorgio, cripple, and lubbeny

     be ever married in a church: we take each other's word."





MEANWHILE his relation with Cathleen remain in abeyance. what she

have accept in the excitement of event, she need to reconcile with

her calm thought. that be not so easy. she be bring to doubt of

herself. she have be more hurt than she have realize, and she fear

she be too weak for the suffering that fill she. for many week it

be a pain to she to see René, for she could not but remember the

destruction and misery he have bring into other life. she have no

support, for her rupture with her family have make an end of the idea

in which she have be instruct as a child, and she have no experience

to draw upon, and Lotta's theory, when it come to cold practice,

vanish into the air. she could not avoid jealousy of the past; and,

with that in she, she could not bring herself to take the plunge into

a life so different from any she have ever imagine. René be so

patient, and have fling himself with such ardor into his new work, that

she have begin to tell herself that he have no need of she, that she too

be in a sense his victim, since his meeting with she have enable he

to break with the past only to thrust the weight of it upon she. the

superficiality of her conception be betray and make plain to she,

break up by one fix idea, the thought of Ann's child. how could he

have let that go? how could he thrust that back into the past? how

could his feeling for herself have break clear of that? and Ann? how

could she set thousand of mile between herself and he? if she have

stay, they could have wrestle with the reality. they could have

make provision in their life for the inimical new life. but Ann, in

her desperation, have leave they to deal only with an idea, a shadow, a

memory. René apparently could ignore it. he be full of enthusiasm and

happiness. he seem to consider Ann's flight as a declaration of

independence and to acquiesce in it. have he feel nothing at all? could

a man come in contact with that mystery and remain unmoved? must not

such defiance of Nature be fraught with appalling consequence, to end

in the bad state of all, indifference?



she hug her difficulty to herself, and dare speak of they to no

one, for she be possess by the shyness breed by a fix idea. at

last Lotta catch she out in deliberate avoidance of René and ask

what have come to she. little by little she drag her trouble out of

she, and try to reassure she and bring she to reason.



"you should ask he about it," she say. "he must have think it out

he do not forget she. you must remember that. it be not a case of

his feeling for you wipe she out of his mind. my own view be that

nature be entirely indifferent, and I don't believe parent and

child do naturally and inevitably have any feeling for each other.

indeed, nature be so indifferent that our thought about it be rather

impertinent. it be obvious that child do not always bind man and

woman, and I imagine they must often have the contrary effect; always,

I should say, when they have for each other only the kind of selfish

affection which resent any intrusion. surely that be why so many

woman turn from their husband to their children----"



the word "intrusion" bring Cathleen to the crux of her difficulty.

she see, with some exaggeration, that this be her condition, and the

quality of her affection, that she have be hunger for possession

of her lover with no intrusion from the past.



"o Lotta," she say, "we be fool to set our face against what

cannot be alter. I think I have break away from narrow

convention, but I have only rid myself of the name of thing, not of

the thing themselves, the silly pretense that people wake for a

moment out of a sleep in which nothing can happen, love and go to

sleep again. we be stupid, try to keep all our love separate. we

cann't do anything but stumble from one love to another, can we?"



"it be what all of we do, and Nature have to take her chance. it be

degrading to have one's folly and weakness, even one's mistake, use

by Nature, but that be the way of the world, and I think a real love

can always get the well of it."



"I have try so hard."



"you should see it from his point of view. suppose it be you who have

be trap by Nature's indifference. you would feel hardly use if

he let jealousy stand between you and he."



"but René couldn't."



"perhaps. why should _you?_ it really do hurt I to see you two

waste time and youth, two absolutely free people in a world that

take its great pride in its waste of opportunity. you be behave

abominably. really, if you let he be much long he will settle down

with Mr. Casey, and discover that he can do at any rate comfortably

without you, and keep you as an ideal. that happen to I when I be

a girl. I let thing slip by until I wake up one fine day to find that

I be nothing but an ideal and have no hope of ever become anything

else, even though I have marry he. so I never do. love change,

like everything else. it grow in we and die. very short be the time

when it can be take and build into our life. if that time be let

slip away then love die down. if that happen, then life can never be

anything more than amusing."



"if it should be too late?" say Cathleen, alarm.



"it willn't be," reply Lotta; "he have be to I and I say I would

send you down to he."



at the week-end Cathleen go to Rickham. she find René in overall

take down the back axle of a car. his face and hand and hair be

smear with grease.



"Hullo!" he say.



and Cathleen answer:



"I hope I'm not in the way."



"all right. only stand clear of the machine. there never be such

ubiquitous stuff as motor grease. I shalln't be long. it's a break

crown-wheel, I think-- oh! here's Casey. casey, take Miss Bentley

round the garden. have tea in the parlor, and I'll join you when I've

clean up."



it be a couple of hour before René join they. during that time

Cathleen have to listen to his praise, and to hear how the business,

after a slow beginning, have begin to pick up, until now they have

almost as much work as they could do with their present staff.



"I'm sorry," say René. "it's a new customer, and he want the car for

to-morrow morning, and I couldn't take any of the man off their job.

it be good to see you. have you see the house?"



no. Casey have only show she the garden.



after tea René take she over the house.



"it want you," he say.



"I know that. I send in my resignation yesterday."



"when will you come?"



"in a month's time."



"forever and ever?"



"it feel like that now."



"yes. there don't seem to have be anything but you and I. you're a

little slip of a woman to fill the whole world." and he lift she

clean off her foot. she lie back in his arm and her eye close, and

he could feel her whole body surrender to his strength, her whole

spirit come out to meet his in love.









ix



young love dream



every year they visit Scotland and bring new store of happiness

to the dell where they have first discover it. always, René declare,

through their joy there run the song of the burn, and the wind in the

tree, the beauty that have first awaken he. they make high holiday.

Cathleen like to stroll about the wood or lie in they with a book

(she could hardly get he to read at all). he love to wander over the

moor alone or to go stride over the hill, and to come back to she

in the evening. when they spend their day apart they would meet in

the dell, and, as of old time, he would make a couch of bracken for

she. and he would lie by her side and rejoice in her beauty, fondle

she, praise she, tease she.



"I don't believe," he would say, "we shall ever be old."



"not when you look at the child" (they have three) "and see how they

grow?"



"Least of all then. I watch they and discover new world in they, and

often through they I discover new wonder in you."



"don't you know I by this time?"



"every day I find you more astonishing and strange. sometimes I come

into your room in the morning and watch you sleep, and I feel very

lonely then. you be so remote. it be like wait for the dawn. then

I see consciousness wake in you. then your eye open and you gaze

innocently out upon the world. and you see I and be satisfied."



"and you?"



"I know that another day have come, another opportunity, a new turn in

the adventure."



"be it always an adventure?"



"always. unending desire."



"for I," she say, "it be peace and knowledge. it would be stifle

if I have not you to kindle they."



René kiss she and laugh:



"the whole duty of man," he say, "to keep the flame alight in woman."



she become serious on that.



"it's true, René. you nearly let I wither away, and my life dwindle

to ashe. I be often sick with fear when I think of it, how near I

come to be one of your failure."



on such evening they would talk until darkness creep into the wood,

and they wake to their mysterious night life when their sweet song

be sing, and they be fill with magic snare and lurk danger

and conflict. sweet comfort be it to be together then amid so much

menace and alien power, and they would go warily hand in hand until

they come within sight of the light of the great house. then they

would almost run until they reach the open lawn where the free air

would beat upon their face.



"I always feel," René say once, "as though we have have a narrow

escape."



"in the wood, do you mean, or in life?"



"Both."



"escape from what, my dear?"



"I know," he say. "this be the truth of we. escape from sleep and

death."
